The Red Page
London Verses
Cherry Gardens (Rotherhithe.)
My man fell in, when he was drunk;
They'd thrown him out o' the •“ King's
' Head,"
From Wapping stairs he fell, and sunk.
He was my man; he’s dead.
On the cold slab, a sight to see,
They've laid him out —poor handsome
chap —
In Rotherhithe's new mortuary.
Ilis head should dent my lap,
But I mayn't warm him where he lies
Because I hove no ring to show;
Yet I've his bruises on my eyes:
And bore his child a month ago.
Acacia Road.
All down Acacia Road there are small bow
windows
Jutting out neighborly heads in the street,
And in each sits, framed, a quiet old woman.
They watch the couples who pass or meet.
And some have borne sons, now ageing men;
And most have seen death in their narrow
house;
Heard wedding bells for their grandchildren ;
Seen boys seek the bar for a last carouse;
And heard wives cry, through thin plaster
walls,
And watched babies laugh in the sun out-
side.
They treasure things up in their withered
old hearts,
And always they sit looking out, with eyes
wide.
These queer old women, they watch, as they
sit
Through the whole long day, what happens
beneath:
They miss not a thing. Sometimes they
knit,
And sometimes dream a little, holding their
breath.
THE BALLAD OF THE BRAVE SOLDIES.
She tcandered by the river's brink,
Her stricken heart stood still:
She listened for his hastening step,
With mind to win or kill.
From Ipswich up to London Town
Long days, long nights walked she:
And now had tracked her soldier down
Who caused her shame to be.
She could not breathe, her throat grew dry,
Her soldier looked so brave and strong:
“Why, Moll, my girl”—she heard him cry —
“What brings you here along?"
“From Ipswich, Dick, I've brought the son,"
She moaned, “your broken promise gave."
He looked and laughed: “Poor little one!
I've used you ill, I have."
She sank, and saw him smile good-bye —
She who had thought to kill or win.
He was too fine, too bold to die,
The weak must suffer for his sin.
From “Streets: a Book of London Verses,”
by Douglas Goldring (Goschen, 35.).
Britain’s "Local” Navy
Richard Jebb’s Studies in Colonial
Nationalism was the first outside recogni-
tion of a fact that is a commonplace in the
Dominions —that each is already a nation
and inspired by its national patriotism. And
in The Britannic Question (Longmans,
Green) he continues the good work of
educating Bull out of his hereditary im-
pression that he “owns” his “colonies.” The
Britannic question is the question of the
alternatives before the Empire; whether it
shall become a closely united Imperial
Federation or remain an Imperial Alliance.
The problem is simply this: how to co-
ordinate five or six separate governments
under some system which, without impairing
their several sovereignties, would enable
them to work together more effectively for
common purposes.
j*
The British Empire has “just growed.”
But it cannot, without the grave possibility
of disaster, be allowed to muddle through.
The whole recent trend of British effprt
has been to exclude the Dominions from all
consultation on Imperial foreign policy and
the important question of defence. We are
to be “informed,” not “consulted.” And
Bull’s recent policy with regard to the
Dominions is to centralise the Imperial
navy and discourage the creation of allied
fleets. Unfortunately for this British ideal,
the Australian navy is already in being.
The policy of Conservatism in England is
thus stated: —
The creation of a central authority
equipped with power not only to repel
aggression but also to maintain Britannic
law and order, i.e., to repress inter-State
disturbances or any attempt at secession...
Conservatism would find the key to the
whole problem in centralised authority, par-
ticularly as to the naval and military forces
of the Empire. Get the fighting power cen-
tralised, and then you will have clipped the
wings of the colonies and there could re-
main no real danger of a Laurier or Botha
or desperate White-Australian asserting in-
dependence.
This attitude is the result of the “our
Colonies” fetich :
A century of empire-getting and empire-
ruling could scarcely have failed to imbue
the British at home with a complacent sense
of possession in the oversea territories of
the Crown, and of suzerainty, at least, over
the populations thereof. In British eyes the
people of the self-governing colonies —-free
tenants of England’s territory, but still sub-
jected to a suzerain in international affairs
—acquired an inferior or “colonial” status,
carrying with it the moral obligation of
humble and grateful “loyalty”.... Nature
constantly reasserts herself in the familiar
phrase “ our colonies” —not colonies merely,
but colonies the apanage of Britain —which
is still (despite the reluctant official reform
in 1907) the ordinary way in Britain of
referring to the Dominions.... The ex-
pression marks the user as mentally dis-
qualified for Britannic statesmanship.
Judged by this simple but sufficient test, the
present British Cabinet (Liberal) has been
found to yield not a single member with the
modern outlook.
J*
In reference to the despised statesman-
ship of the Dominions, Jebb contrasts the
handling of the Asiatic immigration ques-
tion by California with that of the
Commonwealth, Canada and the Union.
The Dominions have had the power to em-
broil the Empire, had they chosen, yet they
have made no trouble for the Imperial
Government like that which California has
made for the Washington Government.
An Imperial Federation, with Britain pos-
sessing -the majority of the voting power,
would mean that the Commonwealth must
surrender the control of its navy, and all
the Dominions some part of the control of
their military forces. Also, the Dominions
must resign their independence in regard to
Asiatic immigration, their separate right to
make commercial treaties, their control over
their tariffs, and would have very little in
the shape of enlarged powers to balance
those considerable losses of autonomy.
However, Britain, in a federated Empire,
with voting powers given to each dominion,
would have to modify her foreign policy in'
upsetting the old tradition that nothing ob-
noxious to the United States must ever be
insisted upon.
Certainly Canada, Australia and M.L.
would not be disposed to take “lying down”
such treatment as the threatened discrimina-
tion on the Panama Canal.
Freetrade within the Empire is possible
on one condition. National Protection
against Britain is our policy only so long
as Bull’s standard of living for industrial
workers is lower than ours.
Were the level of “white” wages practic-
ally uniform among the Britannic demo-
cracies, the case for protecting the industry
of one against another’s might soon lose its
mainstay. Already Freetrade between Aus-
tralia and M.L. is regarded as a possibility
of the near future, their social conditions
being on a common level.
Britain is the backward partner, the
blackleg in regard to labor conditions. Let
Britain lift herself to our “white” level and
we should receive her. But, of course, Jebb
has not taken full cognisance of the need
we have of some measure of protection even
against nations working on the same wage:
we have to plant new industries in a new
country, to compete on level terms with
industries already established in Britain.
And even an inter-state Freetrade Empire
would need a tariff barrier against all other
nations. But the position of the Dominions
in an imperial Federation would be intoler-
able. For such a Federation would “dis-
perse young and vigorous national patriot-
isms which are successfully evolving the
institutions required for their expression.”
And Jebb puts the point strongly that had
the Empire been a federal one in the recent
past, there would have been no insti-
tution of national service in Australia,
M.L. or any other part of the Empire, since
Australia and Maoriland were impelled to
take that plunge mainly bv. the withdrawal
of the British fleets from the outer oceans
to the home waters of Britain.
J*
The “local navy” of Australia, with its
higher rates of pay, is effecting a reform
that Britain has hitherto refused, except
partially, to undertake. We are entering
into competition with the British recruiting
officer, and we have better conditions and a
higher wage to offer. The only course open
to Britain, the employer who pays low
wages and offers inefficient accommodation,
is to improve the conditions of her seamen.
And that, Jebb says, “may be worth, at no
distant date, a good many dreadnoughts.”
But it is also whispered that the British
naval officer can make nothing of “the un-
ruly Australian.”
One cannot readily believe that men so
adaptable as are the Australians are
impossible material for a modern naval ser-
vice. To say so must be to condemn not
them but the naval authorities, who would
stand convicted of incapacity to do what is
being done in every other business, namely,
to meet chanered times with changed
methods. If the Australian and Canadian
peoples, undismayed by taunt or difficulty,
will set themselves to the task, they can be
trusted to evolve eventually a new order,
under which their own naval services would
not be repellent to men reared in larger
freedom, and the example of which would
stimulate the progress of similar reform in
the Old Country.
Thus our delightful task is to show an
example to Britain in the up-to-date man-
agement of modern navies.
The critics of naval alliances are
dealing with an alliance that is the
first of its kind in history. Ours is a naval
alliance which consists of individually
designed “fleet units” for combined service,
standardised as regards types of vessels,
guns and ammunition, uniform as regards
schools of strategy, officers’ training, tactics,
and language of command, intended to be
associated in manoeuvres and through inter-
change of personnel, and arranged to be
placed under one control in warfare.
It seems a curious instinct, to those who
do not share it, which distrusts the potential
competence of Australia and Canada, as
States in alliance with Britain, to discharge
the same responsibility which has cheerfully
been entrusted to foreign Japan. All navies
must have a beginning, and less than 30
years ago the Japanese .naval power, as also
the German, was non-existent. Yet the
modern proposals, first in Australia and then
in Canada, to begin the creation of a Do-
minion navy, have been successively received
in English quarters with derision of the
possibility, and at a later stage with ill-
concealed alarm at the impending actuality.
.. . Strategically, then, one may safely con-
clude that if the principle of a naval alliance
can be worked in conjunction with Japan
and E’rance, a fortiori, can it be worked in
conjunction with the Dominions.
The trouble is that the Bull cannot recog-
nise that the Dominions are for naval pur-
poses separate nations like Japan and
France. As for centralisation, technically
it is superior, but “the superior driving force
of national patriotism as compared with
imperial compulsion” is an important factor
of efficiency. And :
Decentralisation, again, might minimise
the risk of the whole system becoming rotten
together, or of its being destroyed by a heavy
blow at the heart....As for the strategical
disposition of fleets, if the British Govern-
ment was politically able to maintain a
squadron in the South Pacific, why should
it be deemed politically impossible for an
Australian Government to maintain ships in
the North Sea or the Mediterranean?
Again. Aren’t we, perhaps, a little too
apt to regard the British Admiralty as a
divine institution?
The implicit confidence shown by Mr. Bor-
den in the continuous wisdom and efficiency
of the British Admiralty is characteristic of
imperial-federalists overseas; but can hardly
be shared by any who have followed the in-
dependent criticism of its modern adminis-
tration, and have noticed that certain specific
and telling indictments seem to remain un-
answered. The analogy of the centralised
War Office, with the gigantic incompetence it
displayed in South Africa —an incompetence
mitigated in the crisis by the happy fact
that it failed to drag the Dominions into
its net —must inspire some misgiving as to
how the Admiralty might tunerve from that
vital test which has not arisen for a hundred
years
For the alleged Imperialists of Australia
Jebb has something further to say about
“local navies.” First, the British navy is a
“local” navy, as it is a force, like that of
Australia, designed for general service
abroad, but restricted by political exigencies
to home waters. Also it is a “local” navy,
inasmuch as it is normally kept under local
control instead of being assigned to the
control of, say, the Empire. In fact, it is
more of a “local” navy than that of the
Commonwealth, for Australia contemplates
merely the localisation of peace control —
not, as in the British navy, of ships. And
the R.A.N. is a fleet unit, originated and
approved by the Admiralty .itself only four
years ago, though now, according to the
London Times, even the Admiralty can make
a mistake.
Probably the local feeling in Britain,
where invasion panic is now chronic be-
cause -the danger seems real, would forbid
the despatch of any British unit to the
Pacific for the purpose of combined training
or for strengthening an outlying fleet of the
Empire. That fleet would, therefore, have
to be provided by the Dominions alone.
In their case the fear of invasion is now
relatively negligible, because they, in con-
trast with Britain, are already taking mili-
tary measures to deal with any hostile ex-
peditionary force that could probably be
sent against them at short notice....
Therefore, the Dominion fleets’ units would
seem likely to be mobile, instead of being
strictly localised like Britain’s navy to-day.
And in his final plea against the “gift
dreadnought” foolishness of Maoriland. and,
possibly, Canada, Jebb concludes
Surely the best service that the Dominions
could now render would be to make clear
that their own resources shall be applied
resolutely to retrieving the lost position in
the eastern hemisphere rather than for re-
lieving wealthier Britain of her own local
duty to herself and to the Empire.
A heartening book for all patriotic Aus-
tralians —and a bomb for all local Empire
Day elocutionists.
For every person who condemns the
“local navy” is disloyally expressing his con-
tempt of the most tied-up and most “local”
navy in the Empire—the British Navy.
Verses by Fritz S. Burnell
Fritz S. Burnell is another of them. He
writes little verses pleasantly, and he wiH
probably keep on writing them. He loves
a sonorous or a tricky line, meaning
nothing:—
Of chrysoprase and beryl and chrysolite ....
Of the cold kisses and white arms of death..
Insate and insatiable lust....
Dark with the doom of all the centuries —.
An infinite, indefinite desire....
And he is addicted to dots, which give that
vague feeling of so much that is inexpres-
sible, and, incidentally, save the poet the
trouble of expressing it. Aud he is fond of
polyglottic titles: “Ultra” and “Exiturus”
and “Taedium Amoris”; he has a “Nacht-
stuck,” “Un R@ve,” a “Traumlieder,” a
"Fantasie de Lune,” an “Impression de
Nuit” and a “Fin du Jour.” These give an
air of erudition and exoticism. And
his “Other Poems” are all readably brief.
Thus, one of them, “The Lover Wishes that
he could Build an Ivory Tower for his
Beloved,” takes three lines of title to crown
a six-line poem. And, after all, he dodges
building that ivory tower. Now, an ivory
tower would be a beautiful thing, and, set
anywhere in George-street, it would prove
a fine advertisement for his beloved; but
Fritz S. Burnell merely builds a six-storeyed
poem. This is not fair either to his beloved
or to his reader. Yet most of his poems are
poems, dainty, vague, fleeting things, not
powerfully felt and therefore but faintly ex-
pressed. The pulse that beats through them
is weak, but the rhythm of that pulse is
pleasantly lulling. And, really, there is no
reason at all why Fritz S. Burnell should
nrtt go happily on doing this delicate,
dainty sort of thing indefinitelv. For he
really does it very nicely.
ONCE.
She came and stood by my side,
Once —it was long ag0....
I was writing a poem, I know,
And she left me: I think she sighed. . ..
Once, she spoke of a need. .. .
But I could not hear her, it seems,
For voices' called in my dreams,
And bade me pay her no heed.
And once, I recall to mind,
She sang to me, all night long,
But the wind had another song,
And at dawn I followed the wind. .. .
But the poem was never read:
And one's dreams depart with the years;
And the wind has a sound of tears....
Not mine. .. .1 have none to shed.
A MANGROVE SWAMP.
Look how the slow, fat bubbles break in
rings,
As though a man were stifling underneath.
The black, stagnating water by no breath
Is stirred, a mirror for all evil things.
The long roots writhing upward from, the
mud,
Like fingers crooked ih lust or pain or greed,
Have pendent tresses of putrescent weed ,
Like dead men's hair clogged stiff with their
own blood.
No light of flowers nor songs of birds dispel
The breathless, stealthy silence of the place.
Only a ripple o'er spreads the water's face
At times, like soundless, dreadful mirth in
hell.
Only the grey mists come and go beneath
The pallid shadows of the sickly moon;
Only dead voices in the night-breeze croon
A drear and melancholy masque of death.
From “Before Dawn and Other Poems,”
(W. Brooks, Sydney).

Business, Robbery, etc.
“W.F.”: On top of his sugar and oil monopolies
the Wellington (M.L.) man, who also pays extrava-
gantly for his gas, begins to smell something in the
cow-fat trade. Of course, the dealers deny that
there is a ring; but when it is known that in Pal-
merston (97 miles away) and New Plymouth (200
miles away) the same quality butter for which
Wellington pays Is. 6d. is retailed at Is. Id and
Is. 2d., people begin to suspect that the dealers
could find Ananias somewhere in their family tree.
“U.J.S.”: Commissioner Russell, of Adelaide—he’s
S. Australia’s nearest equivalent to a District Court
Judge—fell like the walls of Jericho the other day
upon a debt-collector who had foisted his “ex-
penses” upon <a miserable debtor. His Honor pointed
out the illegality of the action; but nine times out
of 10, illegal or not, the debtor (or more usually
the debtor’s wife) pays the charge, not so much
to avoid the court proceedings as to “keep out o’
the papers.” The collector hereabouts is very often
a money-lender as well, and in such case his ulti-
matum of “immediate payment or immediate legal
proceedings” is accompanied by a notice which
states that money is lent on reasonable security. If
the fly is caught, the spidei- weaves a beautiful web
about him, and when he escapes, there isn’t much
blood left in him. But the methods of some business
firms can be as bad. Here’s a case. A boarding-
house-keeper was on the books of her butcher for
£34. She had taken six years to owe him the
amount. At the lowest computation she had paid
him £312 in that time. He stopped her credit
and got a verdict. Under the threat of the
dreaded Unsatisfied Judgment summons—a savage
thing which in S. Aus. usually means gaol—she was
pliable to his proposal, Tl)is was that she should
borrow the money from him, giving him a bill of
sale over her household furniture. She already had
a bill of sale for £5O, of which £lO was still
owing. 'The kind butcher paid this, and then his
joyous little bill pranced after this fashion:—
Debt •. ..£B4 0 0
Interest on overdue accounts for
' six years at 6 per cent. .. 10 4 o
Money lent to pay moneylender 10 0 0
Interest on new loan .. .. 15 16 o
Cost of registering bill of sale. . 10 0
Total £7l 0 0
This she has to pay off at the rate of £1 per week
under pains and penalties that make the woman
shiver every time she thinks of them—which is
nearly all the time. When will our up-to-date muni-
cipality provide us with municipal pawnbrokery? I
don’t know anything that would give our very poor
more immediate relief.
“Sydneyite”: “Ricardo” (B. 17/7/’l3), writing of
the Maffra (Vic.) beet-sugar business, supplies only
a compilation of mis-statements. Instead of the
outlook being “very gloomy,” it is most encourag-
ingly bright. The growers for the past two years
have suffered through bad seasons; notwithstanding
this, instead of being “a complete failure,” in most
cases the only crop harvested at all was sugar beet.
As to a third of the growers having quitted, the
fact is the acreage has been more than doubled this
year. Good land will easily raise 16 tons per acre,
which sold at 235. per ton means £l7 ss. gross
return—g. pretty good paying proposition. As to
the expert in charge of the industry, I am in a posi-
tion to know that since his arrival in Australia, a
little over two years ago, offers have been made for
him to return to America, and also to other countries.
• * j, V. * •
“Pa Pooa”: George Billby, educated colored gen-
tleman and copra trader, had his station in one of
the many obscure bays on the North-East Coast of
Papua. His order for stores came to hand on a
piece of white trade calico, and read:—
“Plese sent i 11 dozing herons 1 roled Oaths
1 A.M. £7 1 t 1 Flowr and I Quean Ann. N.B
Plese tell i the rite time, i feal krook. Praps i
sent u cobra byem-boy. i doan no. good buv
Jorge Biluby.”
Being gifted with second sight Smith sent him-
2 doz. herrings, 1 tin rolled oats, 1 71b. ham, 1 tin
tea, 1 tin flour.
But the “Quean Ann” beat him. “What the devil
does he want?” Smith growled. “The ??? has three
wives now 1” The mention of our client’s “krook-
ness gave Smith the idea. “I know what the
blighter wants,” he said, making for the quinine
shelf. There, he said, dabbing the bottle on the
counter. “Now the only thing we can’t send him is
the right time.”
* * * •
. “Coorey”: Up on the North Coast (N.S.W.), what
isnt cow is generally wood. If the inhabitant—
oldest, middfe-ag-ed, or youngest—isn’t a cocky, you
can bet he’s either a timber getter or timber worker
As for the “poor cocky,” half his tuberculous herds
are never seen by the Agricultural Department’s in-
spectors; but for unblush ng effrontery the sleeper-
getter must be handed the biscuit. 'lie can take
down the average inspector of the Works Department
any day of the week. I have seen an inspector stand
30 or 40 feet away from the stack, and “pass” the
sleepers as they were turned over by the men who
had chopped them. When any are condemned (and
an inspector of that sort usually condemns a few in
order to save his face), the chopper merely dumps them
into, the nearest available river or water-course, and
awaits the arrival of the next man, when they are, as
a rule, duly passed as being part of a new consign-
ment. Another favorite dodge of the timber man is
to substitute blackbutt for tallow-wood, as the
latter commands a far higher price. The method is
simple and effctive. To the ordinary, untrained eye
there is little difference between the two timbers.
All that it is necessary to do to wipe out the
apparent difference is to spread a few bags of saw-
dust over the stack of blackbutt, give it a good
drink, and the trick is done.
STEAMSHIP M.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
O’ nights the sun goes down redly—flames through
the dust and smoke
That curls and drifts from a myriad funnels the sky
to stain ;
But the smoke of one little vessel is streaming
across unbroke,
The smoke from one little funnel is blowing across
my brain.
And nights of fog I hear whistles cry from the
water. Hark!
Echoing over the cliff face where the fog-drifts break
and part—
And the livelong night there are sirens echoing
through the dark,
But there’s one little, shrill little siren that echoes
through my heart.
And at the wharves in the morning—there are great
ships lying there,
And web and network of cables that cross and sag
apart,
But there’s one dark little cable, all wet and still in
the air—
There’s one strong little cable that’s twisted round
my heart.
And many an engine is throbbing, while the spouting
wavelets fall
Across the foam at the bows where the waters whiten
apart,
But the beat of one little engine is clearer far than
them all—
The beat of one little engine is like the beat of my
heart.
VICTORIA.
PATRICIA MURCHISON.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Great Deal of George.
Duiing his stay in England Mr. Joseph Ward saw
a great deal of Mr. George Reid.
lie is looking very well, and is in good spirits,”
remarked Mr. Ward.—C lieery news item.
Westminster Abbey, St. Paul’s and the
Tower—
Around them the sight-chasers gibber and
glower.
Ihe Houses of Parliament, Buckingham
Palace—-
All great things that awe the most worldly
and callous.
Memorials, monuments, columns and
arches—
Big Heaps on the round where Time stolidly
marches —-
Impress and confound
All those that stand round,
With their splendid profundity,
Weight and rotundity,
Until they all shrill in small voices, “Amaz-
ing !”
And stand like black ants while they worship
by gazing.
But the worshippers all would be very soon
shot of it
If they couldn’t see a Gargantuan ‘lot of it” ;
For a heap, or a pile, or a load, or a stack
Is regarded with awe, when a fair, average
whack
Would be laughed at and sc. rned, for the
gluttonous tripper
For his fair pound of fat brings a tub and
a dipper ;
Therefore George, in his way, is a regular
treasure,
For of eyeglass and vest he gives wonderful
measure.
The caller can gaze on his saccharine smile,
And thrill with the thought, ‘‘lie’s all mine
for a while!”
Who would gloat o’er a thin and an acid
Commissioner
When there’s such a splendidly fat High
Conditioner?
One might ransack the world for a long,
footsore century
Before he pulled out such a plenipotentiary.
All respecters of fat men and globular
beauty
Should visit George Howstout. This national
duty
Devolves on the tripper. His front eleva-
tion —
Too nol le for words —-credit does to the
nation.
It’s doubtful indeed if. we knew’ what his
leisure meant
When he was with us. But his splendid
-waist measurement
Advertises us well at each banquet and
dinner,
So our loyal prayer should be: May George
never grow thinner.”
May he speak well, and eat well, and drink
at each gorge,
So that people like Ward,
When they travel abroad,
May report that they’ve witnessed “a great
deal of George.”
N.S.W.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.
Topical Song.
(With moral.)
Bill Lympson caught the small-pox scare
The papers told him to “Beware,”
The doctor terrified him.
He watched himself with nervous care,
And dreamed of spots inside him.
Though none had died of the disease
His soul was agitated,
And, trembling somew’hat at the knees,
Poor Bill got vaccinated.
An illness, which began with flu.,
And ended up with pleuro-pneu.,
Took off his dad (a builder).
He lost his ag£d mother, too,
For typhoid fever killed her.
He buried them in handsome style —
Best polished oak (and pall) box
But all his terrors all the while,
Were fixed upon the small-pox.
Enwrapped in gauze to keep them warm,
Three pustules blossomed on his arm ,
It hurt him much when held up.
And then, to add to Bill’s alarm,
His old appendix swelled up.
His temples throbbed ; he went to bed.
Bedewed with perspiration :
And when he died the doctor said
“A splendid vaccination.”
Police appeared upon the scene,
They burned the house with kerosene
To rid it of infection :
The neighbors went to quarantine
And took up a collection.
For William’s wife and kiddies, who
On him had been dependent.
And then they wisely went and slew
llis medical attendant.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty -
Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques. Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 7, 1913.
OUR POOR ARMS!
The soreness of the public arm, the un-
pleasant pains shooting across the chest of
William Smith, the fever which kept
Mary Jones five davs from her accustomed
place at the typewriter, and all the other
accompaniments of the fearsome business
which went under the assumed name of vac-
cination, but which was in reality compound
mutilation, ought to have driven home a few
lessons that some people pretty badly needed.
We have it on the authority of those pains
and penalties that Sydney was utterly un-
prepared for an outbreak of small-pox. The
vaccine required was not on .the spot, and
quantities had to be obtained by ship from
Maoriland. and even from nlaces like
New Caledonia, where the leprosy perman-
ently resides. The surgical skill required
was" not on the spot, though any number of
doctors, who were as unpractised in the sim-
ple operation as they are in dry farming and
aerial navigation, got to work with the energy
and enthusiasm of a band of Bulgarian in-
fantry which had stumbled on a sleeping
Turkish village. They were aware that a hole
had to be made somewhere in the patient,
and a certain amount of lymph laid in the
trench, but comparatively few seemed to
know whether the hole should be the size of
a mosquito bite, or as big as a saucepan,
and there was a difference of opinion as to
whether a wineglassful of the extract or a
full pint constituted the right dose. Sydney
was unprepared ; and if it did not pay the
full penalty which it deserved to,pay. it has
at least suffered enough to make it thought-
ful.
W e were unprepared for an attack of
small-pox: are we equally unprepared
for worse things? Fpr there are
worse things. The invasion of Australia by
disease is, after all, a small matter com-
pared with invasion by an armed force. An
alien microbe of the deadliest sort could
hardly destroy us utterly as a nation and at
one blow blight all our larger hopes of a
White Man’s continent. It could, of course,
kill off a lot of us —and, few as we are, the
weeding out might be to the advantage of
the race. It could also fill our hospitals for
a while, and cause our medical men to lead
the strenuous life for a few weeks, and it
might spoil our beauty by leaving holes and
nodules in place of the lovely peach-bloom
that now flushes the roots of our whiskers.
But, when it was all over, we should go on
with our work of making or marring Aus-
tralia according to our fancy and the mea-
sure of our intelligence. An alien invasion
in force would be quite another matter.
When the big guns began to scatter frag-
ments of jagged ironmongery, and the orphan
bookmaker was liable to mistake his favorite
bar for an exhibit from the ruins of Nine-
veh, the actual death rate might be no higher
than in a full-fledged epidemic; but the re-
sults would be vastly different. The moment
we were compelled to haul down our Aus-
tralian flag, or even make a treaty by which
any right was conceded to Asia to remain
on this continent, our place and standing as
a nation would be gone. Our only chance
lies in our being prepared to offer real re-
sistance at the first point of attack. Once
the tide turned against us our case would
be as hopeless as that of a very angry selec-
tor when several miles of raging bush fire
were concentrating their regiments of flame
upon his doomed cow-yard.
We have shown enthusiasm enough in
meeting the smaller danger. What is our
attitude towards the larger? Let us be
frank with ourselves and say that for the
most part it at least borders on indiffer-
ence. We have a number of children more
or less under arms; a few growing lads who
have undergone a sort of military training;
a gunner here and there who knows the
difference between a 12-inch shell and a St.
Bernard dog ; a few people who wear uni-
forms and goldierly titles; one diminutive
small-arms factory; a projected railway that
may in 15, or 20, or perhaps 50 years be
prepared to carry men and arms to the point
of likely attack; some other railways that
are still practically useless for swift transit
owing to the lack of a uniform gauge, but
which may at some distant date be made
working parts in the machine of defence ; a
number of highly useful and efficient bullock-
teams, not calculated to carry munitions of
war at express speed; and an infinite wil-
lingness on the part of all our citizens to
trust to luck. On the face of it, it does
not look a very efficient organisation for the
protection of a great and valuable continent
against an invasion that would mean, not
the loss of our beauty, but the loss of just
about everything that is worth keeping.
» * * •
By makeshift methods and a wholesale
system of borrowing and importing, we were
able to make some sort of stand against the
invasion we have had. But with an armed
enemy at our gates, there would be no time
either for borrowing military supplies or im-
porting the necessary trained men to do our
fighting. Our nearest Possible helpers are
half a world away, and likely to be as cheer-
fully unprepared to send troops on foreign
service as we to put up a stubborn defence.
The United States might be willing to lend
a hand, Canada might be sympathetic,
Britain might be perturbed ; but the chances
are seriously against any of them being
able to bring one shipload of troops to Aus-
tralia in less than six months. Six months
is not a particularly long time to wait for
a cargo of cheap, imported goods; but six
months is a great deal too long to wait for a
soldier or a rifle when the actual game of
war is being played with cordite and bayo-
nets. The Turks in Thessaly might have
been all right if the Bulgarians had been
willing to wait six weeks, or even six days ;
but the Bulgarian struck at the earliest pos-
sible moment, and all the rest, as far as
Turkey was concerned, was a mere splash of
blood across a oath of humiliation. Once
the bugle blows the Australian defence forces
must be ready to play the game in bitter
earnest. No sort of desperate valor will
make up for lack of preparation in the time
of peace. In modern warfare the unready
nation has not even a remote chance of
muddling through ; and any defence scheme
which ignores this might as well be a scheme
for providing crowded and uncomfortable
graves for the population.
It may not yet be too late to put our
defence on a sound footing, if only we go to
work with tremendous zeal and energy and
persistence. Put the knowledge that the
party in alleged power has always stood for
the great gospel of nothing-in-narticular does
not fill us with hope; and there is still a
dread that the big day of testing will find
us writing off in haste to Ceylon for a barrel
of powder, and imploring the Germans in
Samoa to lend us a drill instructor; : nd,
while we are yoking ur> our war bullocks
for the march to the Northern Territory,
the Asian oickets will be at Pine Creek, and
Asian engineers will be laying their own
rails to push as far South as they care to
come. After that there will be much hasty
cursing and a quantity of sudden death. and
we shall have no time for a hundred vears
to think of erecting a statue to the national
goddess of Tnhp-onr-Dha r>oo
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“The Extortionate Demands of Labor.”
After all the talk about the encroachments
of labor and the greed of the working-man,
Statistician Trivett’s figures show that in
the N. S. Wales manufacturing industries
the proprietors have fared better even than
the employees. Of the total value of the
output in 1908 salaries and wages con-
sumed £l7 19s. per cent.; last year they
consumed £lB 19s. per cent. Thus every
£lOO worth of goods turned out of the
average factory was worth exactly £1 more
to the workpeople in 1912 than it was in
1908. But it was worth £1 13s. 6d. more
to the proprietors. The men who have really
fared worst are the suppliers of raw
material. Of every £lOO represented by the
output of the average factory the men who
turned in the raw materials took £63 10s.
in 1908; last year they took only £6O 15s.
Put in another way, on 45 per cent, more
materials than were used in 1908, the em-
ployees last year got 691 ner cent, more
wages, and the proprietors -ot 68 per cent,
more profit—which profit, of course, includes
interest. That the suppliers of raw
materials have thus got into the backwash
is most likely due to two things in particu-
lar : in the first place, so much of our raw
material is sent out for sale in the ooen
markets of the world, and the price ruling
there governs the price at home. The local
price for wheat, for instance, is governed
by what can be got for the grain in foreign
markets. And foreign markets are gov-
erned by the supplies from cheap-labor
countries like India ; and cheap-labor India
is a very serious competitor, since it pro-
duces four times more wheat than all
Australia. In the second place, the sup-
pliers of raw material suffer severely
through the want of union amongst them.
The tobacco-twisters are highly organised*,
what unionism can do for any body of
workmen it no doubt does for them. * The
last possible penny that can be extracted
from the industry is extracted. But- the
scattered producers of tobacco-leaf are
utterly disunited: they get, not what they
can force, but practically what is left after
the others have had their several-and-re-
spective cuts. It is really rather remark-,
able that, in the circumstances. the
disunited suppliers do as well as the
figures show. In fact, considering the
enormous changes in the production
figures during the past few years—the
value of the State’s factory output went
from 40 millions in' 1908 to 61 millions in
1912—it is a remarkable testimony to the
steadying influence of Wages Boards and
Arbitration Courts that the extra money has
been so well distributed proportionately.
The proceeds go into four sets of pockets—
(l) for raw material; (2) for fuel; (3)
for labor; (4) balance accruing to proprie-
ty. Between 1908 and 1912, both inclu-
sive, there is not a variation of as much as
3 per cent, in the proportion of the whole
value which has gone into any one of these
four pockets. And it says a lot for the fine
bluffing capacity of the employer—the em-
ployer whom we had supposed to be driven
to the very verge of ruin by the extortions
of labor unions—that his proportion shows
the greatest increase of any.
A Record of Three Years’ Good Work.
Ex-Defence Minister Pearce has issued a
“memorandum on relinquishing office,” and
there is enough hustle in it to make Aus-
tralia profoundly regret the loss of an
energetic administrator. The 1908-9 Fisher
Government -had given orders for three tor-
pedo destroyers—one to be pieced together
in Australia from parts made in England,
the others to be built in England. At the
Imperial Conference in 1909 it was de-
cided to establish a Pacific Fleet. Aus-
tralia, Canada and Maoriland were to con-
tribute, Australia’s share being one battle
cruiser, three protected cruisers, three addi-
tional torpedo destroyers and three sub-
marines. To carry defence further, Admiral
Henderson a year later prepared a scheme,
at the invitation of the Commonwealth, for
naval defence. Since then the ' whole de-
partment has been buzzing. The personnel
of the navy grew from 240 in 1910 to 3400
in 1913, the increase being made up by
recruiting locally and by transfers from
the Royal Navy, plus about 3000 roped in
under the scheme for universal service; and
training establishments were started at
Geelong and Williamstown (Vic.) and on
the Tingira in Sydney. The position now,
so far as ships are concerned, is: In com-
mission, including those about to leave Eng-
land, one battle cruiser, four cruisers, two
submarines, three destroyers,; two gunboats,
and two torpedo beats ; building, \ a cruiser,
a destroyer, a submarine depot ship and an
oil supply ship. Not a* dang’erous collec-
tion, perhaps, but a thoroughly ■'good start.
Naval bases have been more or less estab-
lished at Sydney, at FlindersXVic.), Cock-
burn Sound (hear Fremantle, W.A.), and
Port Stephens (N.S.W.), and lapd has been
acquired at Port. Lincoln (S.A.), - Albany
(W.A.), Brisbane (s>), and <'Hobart
(Tas.), hut none at Port DanvinP'*Wire-
less stations have' been" set up at Williams-
town and the Naval office at Melbourne;
and arrangements have a)so been made with
the P.M.G.’s Department and the Defence
Department to the Vise of the postal
stations when Ausfekfflia smells powder. All
this has meant money:—

It is a big burden for a' - youngster like Aus-

there is to defend it is, after all, a there

lying loose in a single Australian savings
bank. As for military defence, the recom-
mendations of Lord Kitchener, including
an extension of the universal training sys-
tem, have necessitated many changes.N- The
demand for small arms and, uniforms cqp-
sequent on the big enrolment meant fac-
tories; and since July, 1911, four have been
opened: one for small arms, at Litbgow
(N.S.W.), which cost £149,000; one for
cordite, at Maribyrnong (Vic.), £85,800: a
clothing factory at South Melbourne fore
naval and military, £12,300 ; and a harness
and saddlery factory at Clifton Hill (Vic!),

long has also been authorised. The small
arms factory can turn out 50 rifles, ■‘with
bayonets, scabbards and accessories per day,
or about 15,000 a year; and new machinery
now being put in will bring the output up
to 20,000 per annum. “Chargers” (steel
clips holding five cartridges) are being
turned out at the rate of 2,500,000 a year.
As the ordinary service rifle has 170 differ-
ent parts and requires 2250 separate opera-
tions before it? is fit to shoot even one Yellow
Peril, it takes time to produce much stock
of the finished goods; but those made to
date have all passed the tests. The cordite
factory can manufacture 54 tons for small
arms a month, and arrangements are being
made to supply big-gun cordite. Both the
clothing and saddlery factories are capable
of turning out stuff equal in quality to that
submitted by tenders, apd at as good a price.
As to the actual forces, the supply of: horses
has been the first consideration, and ’during
the three years 1500 animals were pur-
chased for the field artillery batteries at hn
average cost of £25. The approximateicost
of equipping a, field battery of four guns
has been found to he £20,000; of a Light
Horse regiment. £l3.ooojv of an Infantry
battalion, £12.000. Taking a mixed force,
this means an average of £2O per head,
without uniforms. Thus under universal
training, with a war establishment of
120,000, the cost of equipment will be some-
thing like £2,500,000. Of material for the
ranks the supply has moved along in this
brisk fashion: Junior cadets, in 1910.
24,005, and in 1913, 53,141: seniors, from
10.597 to 89,720 in the same period. Of
Light Horse regiments there were 19: now
there are 23. Field artillery has moved from
16 to 20 batteries; infantry from 32 to 45
battalions: smaller fighting units from 82
to 78; field guns from 56 to 84. The cost
per head (including cadets) in 1910 was
£7 10s.; in 1913 it is £7 ss. Expenditure
has been summarised as follows:—

showing ;’ and the only taste left in
the mouth is that neither Port Darwin nor
any Westralian port north of Fremantle nor

a mention.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was not quite sure whether, after the deluge
of legislation during the past three years—they had
passed 83 Bills, he believed —it would not be well
to have a period of rest. However, that was not to
be. .. .Although the present circumstances might
appal weak men, they were there to be faced, and the
present Government would face them resolutely.
(Loud cheers.) — Joe Cook at Wedderburn ( Vic.).
Work? Do yeh think I’m workin’, mum,
Becos I loves to slave an’ toil?
D'yek think, if I’d a tidy sum,
Ale precious ’ands I’d ever soil?
No, mum! These ’ardskips I indure,
An’ mentions graft, becos I’m poor.
I took this job becos the pay
Wus wot yeh might call pretty good.
But, Lord, I’d loaf the liveionv day
Quite willin’ if I only could.
But all twixt poverty an’ me
Is jist a one majority.
If I ’ad six or seven, say,
I’d sit about an’ take me ease,
An’ eat me fill, an’ dror me pay,
An’ labor only should I please.
But, mum, I’ve no alternative,
I simply ’ave to graft to live.
Now, Andy, ’im ’oo ’ad this cop
(A bigger fool I never see!)
E’d work till ’e wus fit to drop.
’lm ! With ’is big majority !
W’y, ’e wus rich enough to shirk,
An’ yet the chump ’e took on Work!
The story ain’t a long one, mum ;
It’s jist a tale of sheer ’ard luck.
I seen that Andy’s time ’ad come;
I knoo ’is boss was bound to buck.
’E’s so blimed fond er work an’ such
’E’s sacked becos ’e does too much.
So I steps in an’ grabs ’is job,
An’, as me coat I slowly doffed,
I reckons that I’m sure to lob
On somethink easy-like an’ soft.
Thinks I, “Since ’e’s put so much through
There won’t be much fer me to do.”
Ah, mum, but I wus sore mistook,
Fer ’ardly ’ad I settled down
Quite cosy, with a pipe an’ book,
When ’ere’s the boss comes nosin’ roun’.
Says ’e, “Step lively, Joe, yeh yob !
If you don’t work yeh’ll lose yer job!”
’Twas all that cussed Andy’s fault;
’Twas ’im begun this graftin’ craze.
’E never dodged or called a halt;
An’ got the boss used to ’is ways.
An’, ’cos ’is tally’s eiglity-three,
The boss ixpects the same frum me!
A weak man, mum, ’e would ev gone
An’_ dropped ’is bundle right away.
But I’m a demon to ’ang on !
I don’t intend to lose me pay.
Says I, “Now, Joseph, right or wrong,
Pull up yer socks! Yeh must be strong.”
Yes, mum, I’m strong an’ reserloot.
I got me coat off, as yeh see.
I take me oath no Labor broot
Is ?oin’ to pinch this job from me.
An’, d’rec’ly, when I’ve ’ad a wink,
Yeh’ll see me graft like any think !
No, mum, I ain’t done nothink, yet.
Yes, it’s me back mum, an’ the sun.
But I ’ave managed to upset
_ A thing or two that Andy done.
I’d love to wreck the ’ole blamed ’ouse ;
But, then, I know the boss ? d rouse.
Oh. yes. mum. I intend to start;
Me arm’s as strong as any man’s.
I’ve reely got a willin’ eart.
No. mum. I can’t divulge me plans.
I can’t ixplain ’em. like off’and :
An’ p’raps you might misunderstand.
Yes. thank yeh kindly, mum. I’m sure
If some day later on, maybe
The job’s again thrown open, you’re
Quite willin’ fer to speak fer me.
Yeh’ll say as ’ow yeh ’eard me say
I mean to graft reel 'ard, some day.
It’s jist bad luck, mum ; bad luck pure.
Rough toil an’ .Toe they don’t agree.
But you will not be ’ard, I’m sure;
I feels yeh symperthise with me.
An’, when the time comes round, I know
You’ll give a kirickter to Joe.

Freetrader —and Black Laborite.
A becent remark of External Affairs Min-
ister Glynn should cause every patriotic
Australian to watch him with particular
closeness. It appears that he recently re-
ceived private letters suggesting that the
task of “developing” the iNorthern Territory
is too heavy for the white man, and that
“black labor from Java will have to be in-
dentured for specified periods to do ,the
heavy clearing and other pioneering work.”
This is diverting, in its way, in view of
what the white man has done, without mak-
ing any fuss, in the tropics and sub-tropics ;
and in the further light of what the black
or colored man hasn’t done in the same
places; and in the still further light of the
disastrous results of introducing "colored
labor into America and S’Africa. A pri-
vate letter writer is, of course, justified in
showing himself to be an ass and an
ignoramus if he be so minded. Minister
Glynn, however, has no such right or ex-
cuse, wherefore his statement, when ques-
tioned on the subject, that “he would say
nothing except that there was no intention
to lower wages, and that he was at present
considering the question of Northern Terri-
tory railway construction, and, incidentally,
the best class of labor to employ ,” lays him
open to the closest scrutiny and criticism.
Glynn, as an anti-Laborite and a Free-
trader, is naturally open to a suspicion of
having a more tender regard for the
foreigner than the Australian democrat.
So is his leader, Cook. So are certain
other members of the Ministry. And though
it is impossible for the Cook or any other
Ministry to hope to carry a ' Black
Labor policy through the two Houses it
is quite possible for the Ministry to per-
petrate some administrative act that will
commit this country to an at least tem-
porary abnegation of the White Australia
principle. Glynn’s statement is lawyer-
like in its vague suggestiveness, but what
else can it mean? There is only one class
of labor possible for Australian railway
construction—that of the white Aus-
tralian or European or American navvy
—therefore there can be no honest
consideration of any other class. In
one way it is a pity Glynn wasn’t
more explicit. An open declaration in
favor of indenturing black labor —for the
usual limited period—would have been
pleasing to the community—and disastrous
to Glynn, Cook, Irvine and co.

Victoria Wants an Elective Ministry
and Doesn’t Know It.
As was obvious from its inception,
McLeod's weird attempt to amalgamate the
ultra-Tories-and the Laborites in the Vic-
torian Assembly and therefrom construct a
“strong” Ministry to replace Watt and co.
fizzled out. Gas-engineer Swinburne spoke
sweetly with the loyalists and severely to
the malcontents, and got the party to pass
a resolution urging Watt to “reconstruct” ;
then McLeod allowed that his pumose had
been served and withdrew his motion, and
everything was as it was before. Watt,
meanwhile, shows no enthusiasm for recon-
structiou; he has announced that there is a.
lot of work to be done this session and that
the Ministry is “going straight ahead” —
which promises little consolation to McLeod
and co. The choicest part of the business is
the attitude of the Argus, which foolishly
imagined that there was a chance of high
Toryism ruling the roost for a while. Day
after day it sternly admonished, and still
admonishes, the alleged “weak” members of
the Ministry to resign—and they still hang
on. Agriculture Graham has intimated his
intention to “go out” at the end of the ses-
sion. “But why 'not do so at once and
assist Mr. Watt out of an awkward posi-
tion?” asks Toryism. Then there are J.
Dry Brown, Billson, and poor, tired
Weary himself —why don’t they resign and
allow “strong men” of proved administrative
canacity—like McLeod, McKetchup and
Nawman Bayles, no doubt—to run the
show? The stubborn incapacity of Min-
isters to grasp the situation and go out is
simply lamentable. Of course the stupid
Argus doesn’t see that all this is a result
of the “party” system. No matter how
much a majority of the House wishes to
dismiss the weaker members of Watt’s
team, it can’t do so without sacking the
whole lot and then putting in a combination
of two dissimilar and opposing minorities
in which event the last case of that House
would be worse than the first. But with
elective Ministries a majority of the House
would sack, say, John Weary in one simple
act without disturbing any other Minister or
wasting more than an hour of public time.

Victoria’s Board of Health.
The small-pox scare revealed what a prac-
tical joke the Vic. Board of Health is. It
is an ornate collection of municipal dogma-
tists, who meet once a fortnight or so, and
raise a crop of resolutions on matters that
have aiready been dealt with by the head
or the -Department, who is also nominally
Chairman of the Board. When there is
nothing particular to resolute about, it fills
in time discussing (say) eugenics, or lis-
tening to a . weighty discourse by someone
on the marriage laws of Kamschatka. The
small-pox scare happened a day or so after
one such meeting, and the bottom was all
out of it before the next fortnightly meeting,
so that the trouble came and went without
the Board having a look in. Dr. Robertson,
m his indecent haste to act and save his
country, had forgotten to call the trusty
band together, so when it got its periodical
opportunity it hurled indignant resolutions,
and called upon him to render an account
of his delinquency, and it then and there
resolved to hold a special meeting to discuss
the same. It duly met to receive the ac-
count ; but the very practical Robertson
blandly intimated that he really hadn’t had
time to prepare the written reply it sought.
He gave it a brief and very-much-to-the-point
verbal one, however; whereupon, after fling-
ing around some more resolutions, it re-
corded its appreciation of the prompt and
effective manner in which he had acted in
the trying circumstances. At latest accounts
it was considering whether it should re-
solve itself into a comic-opera, while John
Weary was still dreaming about the aboli-
tion of the Board as at present constituted,
and its replacement by a body of experts.

In Merrie England.
The Board of Trade (Eng.) Labor Gazette
has been making the old wheeze that “Brit-
ons Never Shall Be Slaves” look suspiciously
unreliable. The last volume of the detailed
reports upon the inquiry into the earnings
and hours of the alleged children of free-
dom in the United Kingdom, during 1906
(it seems a long time to take over the com-
pilation, but the ground covered was enor-
mous), deals with 40 separate industries —
paper, printing (etc.), pottery, brick, glass,
chemical, food, drink, tobacco and some mis-
cellaneous trades. It is shown that, for
full-time work, the average earnings of men
over the entire group of industries was a
whole 28s. 9d. per week. In the paper and
printing trades, 63.5 per cent, of the men
earned £1 10s. or more ; but in the pottery,
brick, glass and chemical trades 58.4 per
cent, earned less; in the food and tobacco
businesses, 71.8 per cent, earned less; and in
the miscellaneous sections, 65.3 per cent,
had the same joyous experience. Coming
to women workers, it is set out that, in the
food, drink and tobacco sections, nearly 38
per cent, of those working a full week earned
less than 10s., and only 18 per cent, earned
15s. or more. In the paper and printing,
pottery and chemical and miscellaneous sec-
tions about 50 per cent, of the unfettered
fortunates who drudged full time earned
10s and under 155., and between one-fourth
and one-third of the light-hearted birds of
Free (trade )dom pouched less than 10s. at
the end of an average week. In the paper
and printing trades, the average wage of a
woman on full time was 12s. 2d.; ot a gnl>
6s 4d At the arduous and unhealthy pot-
tery and chemical work, the wage ran with
the expedition of a snail to 11s. led. for
women and 7s. for girls. In the food and
tobacco businesses, lia. sd. and 6s. 6d. were
the respective doles, and miscellaneous ° dd
jobbers made 12s. 4d. and 6s. 9d. Yet the
perennial parson, who is mostly an abject
caterpillar before wealth and position, is
surprised and horrified at the deadly White
Slave traffic in England s women. and
chirrups loudly for the lash as the remedy.
Perhaps it is— if applied at the root of the
evil. „

If a Sydney importer missed his train and had to
wait half an hour for the next, he and his friends
agitated for a 15-minute service—and generally got
it. But the farmers’ wheat could remain stacked up
in the open waiting for trucks until it rotted, and
his complaints would pass unheeded New settlers
lingered on their selections waiting for the promised
roads of access until they gave up the hopeless
struggle in despair and drifted to Sydney to paint
the park railings, or make holes in the sand and fill
U I ) a B' a * n .> or do any other useless job that
would save their faces in accepting a charity wage
from the Government that had made their humilia-
tion possible. The city’s demands upon the public
purse were mostly for beautifying its surroundings,
or adding to the convenience and comfort of its in-
habitants. The men outback, upon whose labor the
£>ty d upended, asked for nothing more than ordinary
facilities for getting their produce to market. To
the city dweller a park, a bridge or a suburban tram
more or less mattered little. It was merely a question of
a little delay in obtaining one more addition to the
comforts and conveniences of their surroundings. To
the man on the land a railway or an easily accessible
port means his living, and a denial entails the loss
of years of hard toil, and possibly ruin. Yet he is
always turned down in favor of the ever-increasine
demands of Million Clubs and other lunatics, who fail
to grasp the fact that the prosperity of Sydney,
their well-beloved' depends upon the prosperity of
the country that feeds it.
But, thank the gods that be, the bush-dwellers
have awakened, and the first congress of the Country
Party Association, held on July 5, was the outward
sign that their long-smouldering resentment has
burst into flame. The daily papers may scoff at
them as a lot of “disgruntled hayseeds,” but the
space they devote to the job leads one to suspect
that they are afraid that they may remain to pray
after the next general elections. “Decentralisation
and the creating of new ports north and south of
Sydney, and the connecting of these ports with the
main lines of the State,” is pasted inside the hat
of every member of the C.P.A., and when the time
comes to put candidates into the political field, the
city press will find the men on the land behind those
candidates.
The Death of William Lyne.
“Oom Bill” Lyne did not long survive the
defeat for his old electorate of the Hume.
It was the sort of thing he would feel
keenly. He could take a licking as well as
any man, but he _ never could understand a
man’s friends giving him the licking. Also
he could never understand a man standing
by while his friend was licked. Right to
the end he was the big-hearted, generous
bushman. There never was, in fact, a more
thoroughly typical Australian bushman in
the front rank of Australian public life.
It was that which kept him a good Radical:
the city’s commercialism never entered his
soul to make it mean and narrow. Tra-
ducers he had by the hundred; for the
whole importing gang was at the throat of
the vigorous man who stood and fought all
the time for a high tariff. On that ques-
tion there could be no compromise with
him. When his old Protectionist friends
formed the Fusion he declined to be a party
to it. A weaker man would have saved his
skin by pocketing his scruples and joining
one force or the other—the “Liberals” or
the Laborites. Lyne could conscientiously
bind himself to neither. He could have no
truck with a party led by a Freetrader and
kept in power by the votes of Freetraders ;
and, though the Labor platform made a
stronger appeal to him in the main, there
could be no tariff sinking with him. So,
after a strenuous life, lonely since the death
of that great leader Kingston who was
more after his own heart than any other
he had found in Australian politics, the
sick old man nominated for the Hume —the
district he had represented for over 32
years—and left it at that. The Labor
party at least showed him no opposition;
had it done the little more that it might
reasonably have done, Patten would not
have won the seat for the Liberals, and the
chances are that Hook and his team of
pettifogging meddlers would not have been
in power. But if there was no room in
either of the two parties for William
Lyne. Australia will find plenty of space in
its grateful remembrance for this bie r . useful
man, clean in spite of a hundred mean
stories to the contrary, vigorous ; n admin-
istration. ardent in patriotism, and generous
in all his impulses.
Australia may allow Agar Wynne to kick
the kangaroo off the postage-stamps, and it
may not take any savage measures when
Cook contrives a hash of the mater-
nity bonus; but it will make trouble
if the Defence business is monkeyed
with for small party purposes. Last
week work was hunsr up at the
Cockatoo Island Dock—the naval-engineering
depot taken over a while ago from the
N.S.W. Government. The yarn was that the
boilers were so far gone in the infirmities
of age that it was unsafe to use them, and
the party hacks chortled about the purchase
of a pig in a poke. Unfortunately for that
chortle, Davis. Director-General of Works,
dropped in a report to the effect that the
power-supply was poor and antiquated, and
the fact having been admitted during the ne-
gotiations. full allowance had been made for
it in fixing the purchase price. So a beauti-
ful scheme to make a bit of party capital
out of an alleged rotten bargain fell down
dead, and to excuse the hanging-up of this
part of the Defence preparations, the un-
patriotic crowd was driven back on to a
yarn which nobody will believe that work
was stopped because the Government feared
for the lives and limbs of the workmen.
Even in party-ridden England patriotism
puts the navy beyond the tricks of party,
and there should be a million ballot bricks
waiting to fall on the crowd which will do
any worse for it in Australia.
A week ago it looked as though the Chinese
Imperial Republic was about to burst.
What troops the Peking Government bad
were watching the Mongolian princes; and
the whole of the South and most of Middle
China was in arms. The “rebels” were
said to have the support of Japan, which
was alleged to be supplying officers and cash.
Yuan Shih Kai was understood to be at his
last gasp, and his departure with a carpet
bag was hourly expected. But Yuan has
always had the knack of having a good,
well-paid, thoroughly-armed and excellently-
disciplined army up his sleeve; and on this
occasion he proved more than equal to his
reputation. His well-trained, well-armed
troops moved forward with truly Chinese
deliberation, and at every battle the more
numerous Southern and Middle Kingdom
rebels were not only beaten, but routed.
Every battle seems to have resulted in a
rebel army being entirely smashed up, and
at time of writing the rebel chiefs were tele-
graphing for terms of peace. So it looks as
though the rebellion or insurrection of the
Constitutionalists is going to be stamped
out; and the betting should be about 10 to 1
that within the next six week or months
Yuan Shih Kai will proclaim himself
Emperor and silence all objectors by remov-
ing the top end of any who too openly pro-
test. Yuan is getting on in the world very
well indeed, thank you.
The straight tip of “Bake Dwyek” :
I offer no excuse for being a globe-trotter who
covers about 40,000 miles of land and water annu-
ally. I have travelled all five continents, and I
know every State in Australia from end to end, this
being my fifth extended visit. Australia has a warm
place in mj' affections, and 1 want to tell what I con-
sider its chief weakness. It is far too exclusively
British. Now, I am a Britisher myself, and my
sympathies are actively with Britain ; but I con-
sider that the British race, after having had a bet-
ter innings than any in "history, is now on the down
grade, and I fear that its most disquieting features
are being reproduced here. The Irish are the only
non-British people strongly represented in Australia,
but there is practically no inter-marriage on account
of their religion. What Australia needs is a strong
infusion of German and Scandinavian blood. Cer-
tainly there are Germans in isolated districts of
Queensland and in South Australia, but inter-marriage
is not extensive. Scandinavians, who, according to
American and Canadian experiences, make the finest
immigrants of all, are scarcely represented at all.
So far as I am aware, no attempt has been made by
Australia to obtain immigrants from anywhere but
Britain, and that is a fatal error. Australia’s ex-
clusively British character is what delays the rise of
a strong national spirit in the Commonwealth, and
Without that spirit Australia will always be weak.
My experience is that The Bulletin [honey here
extracted].... can only benefit Australia by fighting
for things Australian, and this involves fighting
against the unreasonable bias for things British. The
immediate practical thing to do is to get Australia
as extensively advertised on the continent of Europe
(and particularly in Scandinavia) as it has hitherto
been in Britain, and the result will be advantageous
from every point of view.
The mentally blind who voted against the
last Referenda, and have every day since
been squealing about the increasing cost of
looking at the sun, got an object-lesson from
Maoriland last week. They had voted down
the Referenda because the daily papers had
told them, if the proposals were carried, it
would amount to Unification, and Unification
was vaguely represented as being worse than
Beelzebub. From the Trust’s point of
view, no doubt it is. Maoriland at one time
consisted of no less than nine provincial dis-
tricts, or States, each with its own Assembly
or Council. When Maoriland got sense, ail
these were wiped out, and the country was
unified under a Supreme Parliament. It
was a clear case of Unification. The result
was that, years later, when the country
found it was afflicted with Trusts and Com-
bines, the supreme Parliament, untrammelled
by either a High Court or a half-bred Yan-
kee Constitution, passed the Commercial
Trusts Act to deal drastically with preda-
tory corporations or combinations of cor-
porations or individuals. The first gang
attacked was what was known as the Sugar
Trust, and it went down smash in less than
a week, being hit with fines that ran into
four figures and costs on the highest scale.
Last week it aopealed, and the appeal was
blown out promptly. It then asked permission
to take the case to the Privy Council, and got
it on condition that it deposited £5OO as
security for the costs of the Maoriland
Crown Law Department. Meantime the
public is protected from the rapacity of the
Trust; and if the appeal is successful, the
Unified Parliament can promptly pass a more
drastic Act —in one night, if it chooses —
and thus get over any fool-decision of the
Privy Council.
The 416th Commission of Inquiry appointed
bv the N. S. Wales Labor Government con-
tinues to snore peacefully in Sydney. It
is alleged to be finding some dream-like
remedy for Ma’s industrial ills. For weeks
and weeks and more weeks there has been
a procession of sleepy witnesses, each of
whom has been examined and cross-exam-
ined at length by one Buchanan,
representing the employing classes, and one
Connington, on behalf of the employed.
Both advocates have been flogging a dreary
procession of questions which Noah put to
the deck-hands applying for jobs on the Ark;
and the answers forthcoming have been as
those supplied by Shem, Ham and Japheth.
the aforesaid deck-hands. The chairman of
the Commission is Piddington, K.C., and.
though this paper doesn’t know what he
is drawing from the Treasury, it believes
the amount is more than eight shillings a
day. By the time the job is fifiished the
bill will be illuminating, whatever the report
may be. But don’t ask The Bulletin
when the job will be finished. Present in-
dications are that the angel Gabriel has a
good chance of being called as the last
witness.

The other day Melbourne Argus waxed
scornful over the allegation that ancient
Graham, the Vic. Minister for Agriculture,
had suggested an immediate expenditure of
£40,000,000 in establishing a Commonwealth
line of steamers for conveying Australian
goods to Europe. As a matter of fact,
Graham did not throw out any such reck-
less figures. He said that if Victoria could
find £40.000.000 for its own railway pur-
poses, the Federal Government could find
means to run a line of ships to Europe, and
carry goods at lower rates than Private
Enterprise. In short, Graham talked mod
erate Socialism, and the Argus held him
up to the s ridicule of its fat-lieaded believers.
It solemnly accused him of being “afflicted
with a craze against the middleman.’’ as
though it were an awful affliction. It quoted
instances of States making a clumsy, unprofit-
able start in the seavcarrying business, as
though Government incapacity for doing
good commercial things _ for the_national
benefit were proved bevond question. Whilst
thus feeding conservatism with the same old
lies, the Argus dropped in soc™ absolutely
truthful remarks upon the State s back-
wardness in providing proper harbor, pier
and wharf accommodation for big vessels,
but these sins of omission were merely in-
stanced as wrongs done to Private Enter-
prise. They were presented as final reasons
for terming poor old Graham a
Plunger’ among Ministers, with the tacit
suggestion that Premier Watt should care-
fully exclude him from a reconstructed Cab-
inet in favor of Private Enterprise’s chosen
nominee. The animosity of the Argus
comes as a well-earned compliment to
Graham. It does him proud. People who
had thought nothing about him will now
regret that he was born too lone ago to be
of much further use to the anti-Tory cause.

A plethora of pretty flappers in a “finish-
ing” school; the latest freak dance; the
’ porcupine patrol”; an Alpine scene with
more or less real snow and more or less real
ski and more or less beautiful girls in white
sweaters or in more or less Bulgarian or
Vulgarian dresses; a glittering ball-room
scene in London, with the sort of costumes
that evoke the loud “Oh!” of awed envy
from the women, and sufficient knee show-
ing through the slashes to prevent the at-
tention of the men from wandering; a comic
butler with a fatuous yet ingratiating smile
and much zoological and historical chatter
and many costumes; a lord who pretends
that he is a riding master, and who would
probably make a better riding master than
he does a lord ; a villainous baron who sings
something as near as he can get to “Won’t
You Come to the Ball?”; an aeroplanist
who leaves his aeroolane in the umbrella
stand while he makes love to a girl; a girl
who rather likes being made love to by an
aeroplanist; a brief view of the aeroplane
wobbling in the aerial distance : a ski-dance ;
a “tuckwoman” at . the finishing school, with
a Scotch accent that skids a lot, and rather
frequently volplanes; a variety of Scotch
and other barbaric costumes and dances ; a
girl named Bella Peach, and a delightful,
adorable, delicious, lovely, chic, charming,
piquant French mistress (sc7iool-mistress, it
is perhaps necessary to mention ) • two very
long intervals, some fine lighting effects,
several tons of mountainous scenery, and
some cheerful music—these things make up
the entertainment presented at the Cri-
terion on Saturday evening, vyhich the cos-
tumiers, Buckley and Nunn and the Misses
Clapp had, in a moment of irresponsibility,
labelled “The Dancing Mistress.’ Also,
there was a plot, and about four authors.
"A BUTTERFLY ON THE WHEEL”
AT SYDNEY ROYAL.
Lewis Waller , K.C., and Halliwell Hobbes
try to crush the tearful little butterfly.
Mr. Jack Cannot had his traditional part,
that of a butler in a ladies’ school, who
pursues §ll _ the other characters all over
Europe, waiting only just long enough to
change his costumes. His irrelevancies and
his patter were ancient, but absurdly divert-
ing, and his imitation of the engine of a
motor-launch suggests that perhaps he
would make a better motor-launch engine
than a comedian. Frank Greene, as a vil-
lainous baron, occupied most of his time in
singing innocuous ballads in waltz time.
Mr. Leslie Holland, as the aeroplanist who
starts from Switzerland to race to England
and arrives there in evening dress to find
all the other characters already awaiting
him, had small scone for anything but the
conventional love-making of the conventional
musical comedy hero ; but even this he con-
trived to make somewhat different. Miss
Jessie Lonnen was as Scotch as this typical
English ingenue can be, and whether as the
tuckwoman or the plaided mountaineer her
careless charm was as evident as usual. Miss
Olive Godwin, besides wearing a wonderful
evening dress, romped through her part of
a flirty maiden; and though Miss Vera
Pearce had little to do except with her
eyes, she threw her glances with ease and
dexterity. Miss Blanche Browne, as the
dancing mistress, worked hard, imnarting
the last nuance of expression to every word
of her songs and every gesture of her feet,
but it seems a pity that she allows a slight
sense, of strain to mar her otherwise quite
sufficient charm. A practised vivacity is
no doubt necessary for any comic-opera
star, but her art should be of the sort that
conceals art. The success of an evening
that had many obvious successes was the
tine character acting of Miss Grace Palotta
in the part of Mile. Touchet. the French
mistress at the finishing school. (For ad-
jectival expression of opinion see above.)
Miss Palotta was in every way satisfactory;
her animation, her dramatic feeling, her
grace, her striking costumes and her man-
ner of wearing them could not have been
bettered.
. The costumes, the music, which is suffi-
ciently like the music of all other musical
plays to be recognised at sight, the lighting
effects, and the efficient and artistic stage
management and grouping of the characters,
will give “The Dancing Mistress” a long
career in easily-amused fevdney.
•1' ‘c v
“The Speculators,” which followed “The
Grafters” at the Palace (Sydney) on Satur-
day night, is, like its predecessor, musical
burlesque based on nothing in particular.
Its alleged plot is even more incoherent,
and vague, and invisible to the nude eye
and absent at the roll-call; its burlesque is
mostly supplied by Moses La Blanc and
Frank back’s indiarubber collision bulk-
head, as. formerly; and its music and danc-
ing are no better nor any worse —which is
to say, they are of similar quality, or there-
abouts. Also, there was no marked differ-
ence on the opening night in the apprecia-
tion of the audience. The hob-nailed ap-
plause in the gallery was at par, and the
joyous cackle of the milliner’s apprentice
in the stalls added to the gaiety of the occa-

took such a fancy to “The Grafters” will
■find the same flavor in “The Speculators.”

The Butterfly is still on the Squeal at
Sydney Royal. The Waller-Titheradge
melodrama has hit the great heart of the
feminine public, and thousands of women
are “just dying” to hurry along and weep
at it. Poor little crushed Peggy, who looks
so sweet in her real ermine lurs, stands and
sobs for the whole downtrodden Sex —the
Sex that is insulted and imposed on, and
faints under heavy furs, and doesn’t have
to work, ' and reads Elinor Glyn with its
feet up, while its cynical brute of a husband
goes away into town eVerv morning to drink
and joke, and doesn’t give a thought to the
patient, contriving woman at home, who
hardly knows how to make both ends of her
blouse meet. You realise what brutes men
are when you see the way Butterfly Madge
is bullied and frightened. Make that brute
of a husband take you! It will be a lesson
to him ; or, if it isn’t, it will at least cost
him money, and enable you to show the
jealous frump next door that you still have
a decent evening dress to your back.
“Get-Rich-Quick Wallingford,” which
was revived at Her Majesty’s on Saturday,
made so good an impression 12 months ago
that it isn’t yet necessary to more than
recall how the two crooks, Colonel J. Rufus
Wallingford and “Blackie” Daw, descended
upon sleepy Battlesburg and trimmed all
the boobs, but fell in love and through no
fault of their own turned Battlesburg into
the largest manufacturing town in the mid-
dle W’est —wholly and “solely through a cov-
ered carpet tack. There is no change in
that part of it, but the cast has had numer-
ous alterations, and suffers from some of
them. Beatrice Holloway, as the steno-
grapher, has a big task to play up to
Josephine Cohan, but she does it admirably.
“Andy” Dempsey, the hawtel proprietor
previously so delightfully handled bv J. II
Manning, falls away at the hands of Fred
Cambourne, who 12 months ago made a hit
as the ’bus driver —a part now taken with
less success by Ronald Bryam. Nor does
Bessie Meers, the head waitress, receive
such good treatment from Marie Avis as
she did from Margaret Dale-Owen; and
Maurice Dudley doesn’t make Blackie Daw
deal so convincingly with the boobs as Cor-
son Clark used to. On the other hand, Fred
Niblo, as Wallingford, is the same superb
company-promoter and crook: and E. P.
Bush, E. Lester and 11. 11. Wallace retain
the excellent characters they had in the
former production. Wallingford will cease
wondering “if that damn thing is any good”
on Tuesday night. On the following Satur-
day Gen6e and her dancers will float in and
supply Sydney with a new thrill.

“Her Road to Ruin” belongs to the more
subdued type of Adelphi bellowdrama. The
supply of bloodstains is strictly limited;
there is but one murder, and that murder
so uninteresting that the present N.S.W.
Government would be sure to hang the mur-
derer. The villain in the case is the nephew
of a bishop, who will believe nothing bad
of him, though the victims of his villainy in-
clude one curate, three girls, the Bishop
himself and (more or less) a newspaper
reporter. If any one thing stands out above
others in the story, it is the mountainous
collection of improbabilities. Still, as Mar-
lowdramas go, it is well up to the standard.

hours.

The Crystal Palace has been specialising

Manager Barrington reports a rising
market at the Alhambra (Sydney) for all
vaudeville lines. A nice parcel of girl
dancers _ (Jessie Lee and Thurlite Sisters
brand) improved booking quotations during
the week, and the demand for comedians
increased. Max Martins, Joe Charleses and
Will Washingtons were specially inquired
for. Generally there was enough business
to keep the entertainment mill running full
time.
