The scenic attractions of “Bella Donna,”
staged for 10 nights only at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.), are calculated to save it from neg-
lect as a talky drama with a slow, painful
plot and a mostly unreal set of characters
It is hard to estimate how much the play
loses of dramatic interest in its present per-
formance by the weakness of the acting. The
extravagantly affected mannerisms of Miss
Irene Browne’s heroine aggravate the seem-
ing foolishness of the infatuation of her hus-
band (Martin Lewis), whilst the lack of
magnetic force in her Egyptian lover. Mah-
moud (Leonard Willey), makes her subser-
vience to him equally unaccountable. To a
lesser degree the capital performance of
Julius Knight, as the London doctor who
comes along to Egypt in the second act and
prevents the villainess from completing the
poisoning of her dupe, is discounted by th >
lady’s glaringly obvious uneasiness in his
presence. A doctor who did not suspect her
of trving hard to kill her husband would
have to be half an idiot, and, presumably, it
is in order to explain his obtuseness that
Greenaway makes the local physician appear
an abnormal ass.
Miss Browne’s mannerisms of speech and
ultra-melodramatic facial expressions leave
an improbable character without a sem-
blance of possibility. Her most impassioned
speeches in the earlier acts remind one of an
affected society mamma talking to a child.
Her manner of expressing dislike for her
husband in the last act is natural enough,
but it is the same manner in which she was
previously supposed to be dissembling her
feelings. Her staccato utterances when she
gives orders to her Egyptian servant at Dr.
Isaacson’s command are absurdly artificial:
and when her tinted lover eventually dis-
cards her, she fails to attain any height or
depth of amazement and despair. Julius
Knight, as afore-mentioned, is always suc-
cessful in the part of the onnortune Dr.
Isaacson, and Leslie Victor’s impersonation
of an Egyptian servant is only a l ; ttl" too
'oily and mysterious. The novel of “Bella
Donna” may explain what sort of creaturp
Ibrahim Ahmed is. The play leaves one in
doubt as to whether he is an ordinary pro-
duct of the country or an TT i-; a b
Heep. As the husband of the foolish
lady. Martin Lewis does his worst work
at the beginning of the play and his at
the finish. Irrelevant minor characters in the
clumsy opening scene are neatly played, and
so are the minor colored characters in Egypt,
and the staging is worth a visit, anyhow.

aity “Grecians” are mostly pedantic like
aforesaid.) In their English translation the p ays are
merely unnecessary reminders of a dark age when
the art of play-mak ng was in its infancy and Z
teaching was heavily trimmed with crude supersU
tion. Considering the wealth of material at the
command of the half-dozen or so Greek dramatists*
who could drag gods, seers, embodiments of passions'
and all imaginable shapes of supernaturalism into
their plot, their plays are not even evidences of liter
ary genius as the term is now understood
Those old relics of early Grecian civilisation had
no imagination worth speaking of. Thev were just
the brainy educated individuals in a community of
ignorant, credulous citizens. Nobody knew much
when Euripides was about. There were famous sculp
tors, certainly, but these men merelv had the imitative
instinct more strongly developed than it is in their
modern disciples. . Doubtless the Greeks of old had
keener eyesight, sense of smell and sense of touch
than the oyster shopkeepers up to date. But as
W f n w ° f J, C , tlo s they wel £ arn ateurs, and as writers
of fact—well, Lurip. could never have composed so
pungent a paragraph as this! 1
Why does Dorothea Spinney go around the world
telling it that Orestes, being haunted by furies for
murdering his mother, is commanded to seek a cure
for his lunacy in a wooden image of Artemis at
Tauris? The antique merits of the plays she recites
are perceptible in one’s own reading of them but
are not made evident by her emasculated recitals of
them. Apparently she does this thing to sat’sfv her
vanity, which is mostly fed by pretentious learned
professors who sit at her feet, and call the enter-
tainment beautiful anti find some sort of satisfaction
in pretending that they have enjoyed a treat which
the soulless non-students of Euripides are incapable
of conceiving. c
AT Melbourne Olympia last week Asta
Nielsen was shown as a lady of shaky eye-

The entertainment proffered by The But-
terflies at Melbourne Majestic is distinctly
threatened with intelligence and humor. The
merit of their musical work is undeniable,
and their burlesques are calculated to throw
even an audience of churchwardens into a
merry mood. Every member of the gang is
an artist of value; three are respectively
learned in piano, fiddle and ’cello ; while two
have voices that betray considerable quality
and cultivation. Altogether, a bright and
fresh lot, who get their humorous effects
without resort to clamor or violence.

Billie Reeves, the “original drunk,” was
noticed for the first time at Melbourne
Opera House on Saturday. Unhappily Bill
hasn’t any fund of humor in himself, and
when he tries to get his laughs by produc-
ing snakes and other objects by machinery,
his laborious beer jest begins to pall and a
Rechabite meeting seems blither.v by com-
parison. The “business” of Cummin and
Seaham remains the quaintest thing on the
programme. Lillie Langtry also continues
to be “among those present,” and nowa-
nights tickles the Nuts with patter that
keeps carefully within the bounds of ambig-
uity.

“In the Dark,” the one-act drama by Bernard
Lspmasse, which came to light at Frances Ross’s
benefit fn'atinfie in Melbourne last week, fully ex-
plains Walter Baker’s long-bottled-up affection for it.
• an a( ;t or s point of view the stagey little thing
is strong. Espinasse wrote it years ago and stuffed
all his young experience of theatrical devices into
the plot. The blind author has fallen in love with
his lady amanuensis, ignorant of the fact that she
was his dead brother’s girl, whose photo, he has kept
m a drawer, and that her Past coincides with the
Fast of the heroine of his forthcoming novel. A
breezy male friend discovers the lady’s identity, but
chivalrously refrains from giving her away to the
blind man, and breathes his blessing on their pro-
spective union. All the situations are stage conven-
tionalities, and everything happens for simple the-
atrical effect. It is neat, talky, stale and uncon-
vincing.
“Jo Smith” writes from Melbourne: Re the play-
let by Bernard Espinasse, performed at the Frances
Ross benefit. It-might interest The Bulletin to know
that this piece was first performed in the Richmond
Town Hall in 1893 by the Melbourne Dagonet Club.
A one-act play of my own, “Drifting,” was pro-
duced at the same time. It should be remembered
that when Espinasse wrote it, 20 years ago, he was
an inexperienced boy, and considering that he has
since attained some distinction as a playwright in
London, it is a bit rough on him that he should
be now judged (locally) on a very early and amateur-
ish effort.
Passed out at St. Bart.’s Hospital, London, Fred
W. Millis, Australian ventriloquist, aged 55, after
an operation for ari internal trouble. Born at Ade-
laide and educated at Sydney Grammar School, Millis
was intended for the medical profession, but took
to storekeeping up Clarence River way. While there
he practised ventriloquism assiduously, and qualified
for a billet with a panorama show at £1 per week.
Next he toured Australia, and thence passed to
London, where he quickly made good, and was in
constant demand at the halls, until he became
afflicted with total blindness a few years ago. Millis
was a kind-hearted fellow, who took a very active
personal interest in all charities connected with the
music-hall profession.
Adrian Stephen, whose University play made a
hit in Sydney last week, should be a credit to his
country before he is finished. He has done well all
round at his University. His play is full of wit
and humor; as a speaker, extempore or otherwise,
he can give the average middle-aged Parliamentarian
or business-man lengths and a beating ; and he is but
21. He belongs to the legal Stephen family, and is
going to the Bar.
“F.”: Dead at Melbourne on July 28, and buried
at Kew, Edward Pole, born Poole, aged about 75.
He was the “Australian Roscius” of the mid-’so’s.
As a youth he showed some dramatic talent, and
played Othello, Hamlet, and other leading characters.
He failed, however, to catch on, though his father —
a tailor in Fitzrov —spent all his earnings upon him.
Later he dropped down to small parts, and appeared
with McKean Buchanan at the oid Prince of Wales,
in Lonsdale-street. Sensibly he gave up the boards
after that, and followed the painter’s trade. At the
funeral, in the Booroondara Cemetery, but two of
the old-time men were present—Johnny Cowan, the
minstrel; and F. Hailes.
“The Oldest Inhabitant”: The late H. P. Lyons
did not cross Niagara Falls on the back of Blondin
or any other man, as asserted by a score of news-
papers. That feat of Blondin’s was performed in
August, 1859, at a time when H. P. Lyons, then 19
years of age, was “in advance” for a comedy co.
formed by Fred Younge and Dominick Murray to
tour Australia and Tasmania. Lyons met Blondin
in California in 1874, and after showing that he
knew his Australia well, was engaged as manager.
Lyons toured Australia and Maoriland with the show,
but went no further. He told this writer an incident
in connection with Blondin’s first arrival in \ ictona.
The Customs demanded a heavy duty on the enor-
mous tent which the performer carried with him.
Blondin would not pay, but gave an order to a local
tdnt-maker to build another, and the original one
was warehoused.

Felice Lyne, the not-long-discovered prima donna
of the Qumlan opera co., is a lyric soprano, and she
will sing Melba parts in Australia, beginning with
Hilda in “Rigoletto.” Later on, she will be the
Marguerite, Mimi, Rosina, Butterfly and Nedda (in
Pagliacci ). She doesn’t knock her pretty voice
about in Wagnerian music.
WEN MAN, the producer of plays,
watching Gentle.
“Macko”; I have not seen anything so pretty for
years as the ingenuous love-making of the Russians
Volinin and Schmolz in their duet dances. Rumor
has betrothed them, and, on appearances, she may be
for once truthful. They are both so young; and
while there is not such intensity of passion as we
once saw in “La Mascotte,” these pretty pirouetters
are bubbling over with the joy of life, and take the
audience into their confidence. In Strauss’s Polka
“Violette,” danced with the- gay abandon of two
cheeky sparrows, the simpffeiyy of their steps is
almost an impertinence, yet just to see him kiss her
—I want to encore the item all night.
Queenslander Carroll, the “big” moving picture-
man of the North, who is at present on a tramp
abroad, drops a line from Buffalo, N.Y. (U.S.), to
say that h s health has greatly improved since he
visited Riverview Park, Chicago. There he encoun-
tered a couple of old folks from home, billed, regard-
less of expense, as follows: “Cannibals! Man and
Woman! The Strangest People on Earth! Living
on Raw Meats Only ! Australian Savage Man ! Aus-
tralian Savage Woman!” Posters depicted a most
ferocious-looking party of black half-humans, half-
beasts, tearing sheep to pieces and devouring them in
the wool. These “w Id Australian guys” as the
intelligent locals called them, were kept in a deep
pit, presumably so that they could not escape and
devour Chicago’s flock of lambs that haven’t already
been devoured by the packs of ravenous grafters.
“St. Anton”: At no-license Masterton (M.L.) the
other day a local picture-show orchestra proposed
to delight its audience with a composition entitled
“Whistle to Me,” and duly submitted the name in
its programme copy. But the comp, was th'rsty,
and, letting his mind wander, set it as “Whisky for
Me” ; and the audience liked it that way ever so
much better.
“Satis”: The “Russian Imperial ballet” seems to
be as numerous as the Smiths. We have a small
assortment of them just now in Australia. A whole
mob is dancing at Drury Lane during a saison a la
Russe, run by guinea-a-box Beecham. Pavlova and
her troupe are kicking up behind and before at the
Palace, Shaftesbury Avenue. At the Empire
Lydia Kyasht is once again tripping it. These
“imperial” dancers should really have a little more
consideration for the poor old moody Czar. He must
be sadly in need of a little cheering up these days ;
yet his ballet seems to have deserted him by the
million.
Archbishop Mannix, the -R.C. assistant to
Archbishop Carr, of Melbourne, is men-
tioned as the next Archbishop of Dublin.
Archbishop Walsh, of that city, authority
on bimetallism, church music and other
things too numerous td mention, is totter-
ing to the tomb, and Mannix, Maynooth’s
late president, stands well with the feeble
prelate, and with the clergy, whose votes
will be submitted to Rome before the final
selection is made.

In the north-east of South Aus. there is a place
called Tooraniinga (“toora”=snake, and “minga”=
prickly acacia). Mater at Tooraminga was per-
manent and plentiful, and the spot had evidently
been much used as a camping-ground in bygone
days ; but at the time 1 saw it none of the aborigines
would come within cooee of it. They explained
that their aversion was due to a dreadful epidemic
which, according to tradition, seized Black Brother
there and killed him off so rapidly that few escaped.
There were plenty of skulls and thigh-bones around to
give color to the story. Amongst the local tribe
was one Peter, a very old blackfellow, whose face
was deeply pitted with what were unmistakable pock
marks. These, he explained, were caused by the
same disease, which had recurred years afterwards
in a less virulent form. I know the evidence isn’t
conclusive, but it at least suggests that small pox
was a visitor to inland Australia long before the
coming of the white man.
Moss Vale (N.S.W.) snatched what it
regards as a distinction out of the Wollon-
dilly by-election, as its “school of arts” was
used to hold a Cabinet meeting. Not only
did the Cabinet meet there, but the im-
portant business of determining the points
of the Governor’s speech was discharged.
But one other N.S.W. country town has
had what is claimed to be the still higher
distinction of housing the Executive Coun-
cil, with the Governor present. Bathurst
was the town, Loftus the Governor, and the
place the fine old Suttor house, a landmark
of the West. Loftus happened to be there
at the time, and as special business popped
no, the question was whether to drag the
Governor to the capital or have Ministers
up to a picnic. The Government of the
day had a rare stomach for picnics, so the
members trundled off at the country’s ex-
pense. Eminent lawyers at the time
doubted whether it was constitutional to
hold an Executive council outside the capi-
tal ; but no dreadful consequences followed
the outrage on centralisation.
The seafaring “Vagabond” from Lon-
don :
The newly-issued report of the Departmental Com-
mittee on Boats and Davits appointed by the British
Board of Trade doesn t seem to be of much use.
It finds that there are serious objections to new-
fangled notions of “shutes, slipways, moving plat-
forms” and similar cranky devices, and doesn’t
offer any encouragement to inventors to wrestle
out a feasible fixing of the kind. It says there are
several types of davits good enough for anything
and able to lower boats from any old height. I only
hope the committee has to be lowered one dark and
stormy night in a boat hanging from davits which
barely project over a wildly-rolling ship’s side, with
goodness knows how many yards of rope tangling
up in the purchase blocks. The committee recom-
mends, what really should be made compulsory, the
carriage of a proportion of motor lifeboats. Every
seaman admits that, such boats might be able not
only to mote themselves and their passengers out
of trouble, but also to tow off other boats and save
them from being stove in by the sea and the rolling
ship. There is a heap of evidence (and heaps more
liei drowned in many fathoms) of the helplessness
of row boats, especially when seamen who can
handle an oar are scarce, and have to be replaced
by cooks and stewards.
“Lee Shore”: It will be interesting to
hear the judicial opinion concerning the
action of the master of the schooner Tramp,
which was picked up the other day and
towed into the Manning River (N.S.W.).
At the end of June, the vessel was dismasted
near Port Stephens, and by July 5 had
drifted 100 miles off the coast. When there
was every indication that it would keep
going until the southern end of Maoriland
threw' up a barrier, a steamer hove alongside,
and the Tramp’s skipper decided to abandon
the inebriated wanderer. The faithful sea-
beast, however, promptly turned and started
to dog the crowd back towards the Austra-
lian coast. Arriving off Crowdy Head on
July 30, it was towed to an anchorage close
to where the old man and the boys had
landed from the rescuing steamer. Of course,
the desertion was quite justified, but a
Marine Court can’t always be made to see
it that way, especially when the said Marine
Court is composed of persons who are barely
qualified to conduct even a marine store.
There was the case of the steamer Port
Chalmers, which lost its tailshaft en route
to Maoriland, and started to drift away
south. It was out of the track of shipping
when a storm-worried barque, which had
been driven Polewards, took off the crew and
landed them at a M.L. port. The local
Marine Court (plenty of Court and not much
marine) was scandalised that the master
had not remained aboard with the crew to
be frozen or starved, and hung up his certi-
ficate for a long period. The verdict was re-
ceived with boiling language throughout the
whole British mercantile marine, but that
didn’t affect the Court. Only boiling oil
would have made a reasonable impression on
it.

MOIST MOVIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Not a Dry Eye Last Night.
-—Alluring line in picture,-show advt.
Hasten, my love! Pull on that glove !
Ho, for an evening that’s merry and bright!
Rush to the show, thousands will go,
Caught by the ad. that makes the heart glad—
“ Not a dry eye last night” !
Oh, let’s be joyful—not a dry eye!
Love, here’s a treat nothing can beat!
Talk of enjoyment, this film’s “out of sight” !
Gracious, we’re late! Oh, what a fate
To hear, through the “boom,” the dread words
“No room.”
“Not a dry eye last night.”
Bring your goloshes—not a dry eye!
Ready? Not yet? What keeps you, pet?
We’ll miss an evening of purest delight!
Raining? What odds! Praised be the gods!
Useful the “gamp” for weather that’s damp—
“ Not a dry eye last night” !
“Hankies” and Heaven, not a dry eye!
VICTORIA.

"Shay, ole chap (hic), ’ave you been poxin-
ated?"
“Snob”: Melb. City Council is tearing
down the ugly white wooden fences round
the reserves and squares in the back parts of
the city, cutting down the death-like rinus
insignis, that dreadful abomination imported
from Scandinavian cemeteries, and planting
gums, peppers and wattles in their stead.
Not content with doing well and letting it
alone, the city fathers are naming these in-
offensive parks and nlantations after them-
selves. Here is the first list: Burston Square,
levers Park, Pratt Reserve, Sir Henry
Weedon Square, Alderman Burton Reserve
and Gardiner Plantation. The cruelty to
posterity of foisting such names on the plea-
sure resorts of the future will be best under-
stood when it is stated there are still gardens
to be named after Hennessy, Benjamin,
Flegeltaub, Zevenboom and Cockbill.
The yarn of the pew-opener : '
The kindness of the Archbishop of Sydney
(Anglican) in not permitting his curates to flood
The Bulletin's waste-paper basket —at any rate, not
till they can preach a little —gives some Bulletin
interest' to the story ,of. his appointment. For a
great many years the Church of England has been
convulsed over a question of clothes to be worn by
ministers at certain times. In Sydney many worthy
clergymen, neglecting all else, have devoted their
energies to compel their brethren at two churches to
wear the same millinery as themselves. Under the
late Archbishop Smith they had no success; that
worthy man was possibly too clever, or possibly too
good a Christian, to needlessly poke up his neigh-
bors. When he died their chance came. At the
Synod for electing a new Archbishop they had a
majority. Archbishop Wright, incredible as it may
seem, was once quite an obscure worker in the vine-
yard at Manchester, but had distinguished himself
by speeches and writings as a bitter opponent of the
unpopular garmc is. The ecclesiastical wire-pullers
therefore brought his name up. The unhappy High
Church party, hopelessly outnumbered, was com-
pelled to choose between him and an utterly im-
possible person. Hence the arrival of Dr. Wright,
who, from a humble archdeacon, became at once an
Archbishop—much as if a corporal were made a
general. At first the new Archbishop talked toler-
ance, but in 18 months he had abolished the un-
popular clothes. For the rest, Dr. Wright has a leg,
a fine presence and a never-failing flow of sonorous
platitudes, given forth according to the best elocu-
tionary standards, but, unfortunately, with just a
slight provincial accent which, to people who are
really “there,” classes him among the “not quites.”
Old-fashioned sheep, who prefer Christian doctrine,
are wont to look on his sermons as windy and filling,
but not sustaining. As a shepherd he has shown a
distinct partiality to prize merinoes, and moved his
house from old Bishopcourt to the prize merinoes’
Darling Point to be “nearer his work.” A hand-
some man, a good organiser, ambitious, and vowed
to narrow Puritanism, he is a great favorite among
the worldly holies. As regards his views, he is
quite the correct Englishman. He has published one
hook, “Advice to District Visitors,” or something
such, and lays down with some distinctness the
duties of the poor to the rich. In the democratic
life of N. S. Wales he will be as useful as the bell
of his own cathedral, and as much considered.
The righteous curse of “The Under-
signed” :
Who are the immortals? There is evidently so
much doubt that 1 move for the appointment of
some responsible authority to regulate the erection
of public memorials and statues. Here in Mel-
bourne we have a most indifferent lot of bronze and
other effigies, one or two of them representing worthy
enough subjects; others who may pass; others again
who have not the slightest title; and to the last
section we are adding. On Saturday, July 26, was
unveiled a presentment of one Harry Skinner, pub-
man and sport, surely the smallest individual who
ever lived on in statuary. What mortal right has
the circle of petty “admirers” of this caterer to
foist upon present and future generations this
grotesque! The statue is the usual heroic-sized
bronze, standing upon the customary two ot three
hunks of granite, and it was in all seriousness “un-
veiled” before an admiring crowd of South Mel-
bourne and Albert Park ratepayers. Statues are
notoriously badly dressed; this one is especially so,
and in a sac suit—perhaps a copy of the very suit
this immortal wore on race days at Caulfield, where
he was wont to cast an eye over his booth to spot
the barman who nipped the marked florin. Well,
there he is, Skinner the caterer 1 And now, Bent!
There stands in a conspicuous place at Brighton
the foundation ready for a pedestal which is to
uphold the grace and dignity of Mr. Thomas Bent.
In a few weeks we shall have another solemn un-
veiling, with the usual flapdoodle of which we are
so tired concerning the things Bent did for Brighton.
This “statesman” outside the boundaries of his
borough was as nought. But “Tommy done a lot
for Brighton” ; how impossible he was in anv other
constituency was shown once in South Melbourne,
where he got 35 votes and lost his deposit. The
man was a travesty of what a statesman
should be. His clownings were a shame and a de-
gradation to the Legislature, and now we citizens are
to have our eyes offended by sight of a statue to
this embodiment of cunning ignorance. Skinner
may have had his good points; but running a pub,
serving as president of a football club, and as an
M.L.C. for a very short period, certainly give him
no claim. Surely it is time for us to have some
responsible body constituted to say who shall and
who shall not have this, one of the supreme honors
of the people, accorded him.
Concerning a town which has some reason
to shout for itself: —
lialgoorlie (W.A.) got a good municipal start
under such men as Staniforth Smith, nowadays Lieut.-
Gov. Murray’s brigadier in Papua; and when its own
virtues weren’t strong enough to keep it on the
straight financial path, Cohen’s doubts about its
future did the trick. Wherefore to every loan it
negotiated there was securely nailed a sinking fund,
with the result that, though it has electric light and
power works, markets, swimming baths, and such
like, its total net debt now is only about £4OOO.
In fact, its sinking funds have been lately getting so
far ahead of requirements that the last contribution
for a debt due in 1915 was paid in during 1912, and
it has been found that for the next five years a
contribution half the size of that hitherto made will
suffice to wipe out another debt due in 1918. The
money actually borrowed for the electric light busi-
ness, to specify one department, was only £22,500.
Things boomed at Kalgoorlie, but instead of water-
ing stock or issuing bonus shares or doing any of the
tricks that are played when Private Enterprise is at
the helm, the profit was just poured back into the
business— £83,105 of it to date. And naturally the
profit on that £83,105, which was costing nothing
in interest, allowed the council to swish off the old
debt,. until to-day there is only £2607 of it left.
And that could have been wiped out long ago if the
loan hadn’t been for a fixed term, and there was
nothing to he gained by hurrying along the sinking
fund ; so the money that might otherwise have gone
in wip : ng out the debt, and the money that under
Private Enterprise would have gone in paying per-
haps a 15 per cent, dividend on a watered £lOO,OOO
of capital, has either not been collected from the
consumers, or it has been passed over to the council
for the improvement of the city. In one recent
vear (1911) the business handed over £4250 for
this latter purpose. In three or four years there
won’t be a penny of debt on the undertak-'ng. Kal-
goorlie will then absolutely own its whole lighting
outfit, and every coin collects beyond the actual
cost of making and supplying the light and power
will be available for public purposes.
SHE'D HIT IT.

“Final Notice”: The other morning the
postman brought me the expected hint from
the co. that is alleged to supply gas to North
Sydney. It said, in effect, that, unless I
settled my account on or before a date
which was duly specified, the blessed co.
would “take further steps,” or execute a
few additional handsprings. It didn’t go so
far as to say it would improve the gas and
make the substance of some real use ; there
was just the plain, murky hint that there
were steps ahead —a whole flight of them—
and that they would be taken at short
notice. The words were printed on dark
blue paper; presumably it was intended as
a gentle reproach, the tint of the paper
symbolising he blue ruin to which the co.
would be reduced if I didn’t disgorge
promptly. But I duly paid the amount —
paid it calmly and without threats of any
kind. As a matter of fact, I could have paid
it ever so long ago; but I chose instead tp
make the suppliers of inferior vapor wait
for their money until the last possible in-
stant. It cheers me up to think of a gas
company biting its fjngers and kicking the
gasometer because so much money is out-
standing. That beautiful provision, too,
whereby the helpless customer deposits a
sbvereign or more with the co. as a guar-
antee that he will pay for his inferior gas,
must yield a pretty crop of interest. But
the co. gives no guarantee to the customer.
I have dreamt dreams of forming a Final
Notice League, the members of which would
regularly hold back their cash, and one day
descend on the co. in a cloud of dust, and
demand their deposits back. That other pos-
sible disaster —the storming of the Federal
Note Reserve by a party of stout bank
managers—would be an afternoon tea party
to my Great Gas War. I may say that I
would not be able to write this at all if 1
didn’t possess a good kerosene lamp. I
would not even be able to read my gas co.’s
final threats. It is a sadly absurd situation.
Imagine me at home, under a sort of flicker-
ing tomb-light, peering through spectacles at
the last blue reouest for the money I am
deliberately withholding, and incorrectly
diagnosing the thing as a draper’s circular,
or an invitation to dinner! It’s a good
thing the average gas co. isn’t a milk co.
The milkman is liable to be pursued at any
moment and bailed up for samples. And,
if the samples are not up to standard, there
is a fine and a lot of unpleasant publicity.
But, of course, it is the milkman’s own
fault. He could save all unpleasantness by
being a monopoly and delivering his short
measure of inferior goods through pipes.
John Mayes, of White Cliffs (N.S.W.),
an ancient opal-gouger, was lately shifting
some logs that banked up a big dump, when
the loose mullock slid away under his feet,
and he slithered down into an abandoned
shaft at the bottom. Then the infernal
dump started to come down after him,
slowly at first, but with a persistence that
meant business. Mayes hollered for help
and frantically tried to keep himself clear
of the cascading mullock, but the dump was
out for keeps, and before the advance guard
of the rescue party could get there, it had
the man buried up to the chest. By the
time his 30 or so mates had got their shovels
to work it had him up to the chin, and it
kept him at that for an hour, and still it
came relentlessly down —down. Once it got
a spurt on, broke through the grimed,
sweating, panting defence, and covered
Mayes right out of sight. A couple of men
jumped to the rescue and scraped and
scooped breathing space for him, what time
the other men, in the effort to keep back
that hellish dump, shovelled and heaved like
a score and a half of sappers building a
Last Ditch. For three hot hours the fight
lasted, and then the shovels got triumphantly
home on the point and the dump w*>s
counted out.

THANKSGIVING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bright, warm weather... .Winter not yet over....
Must expect some more cold days and nights.
—Weather column.
Tt isn’t really Spring,
But I love it just the same;
For a little while I’ll sing,
And, because I am not lame
With rheumatics, I will dance,
Flinging both my legs afar,
Praising gay climatic chance
With a cautious “Tra-la-la!”
It wasn’t really Love,
But I loved it just the same,
When my hair was thick above,
And I played Youth’s foolish game.
But the imitation goods
Served their purpose. (Let us dance!)
There’s no wisdom in the woods
When we’re troubled with Romance.
It isn’t really Spring,
And I’m very well aware
That some winter wind must fling
Clouds and blossom ev’rywhere.
But I simply must rejoice,
When the Earth smiles like a friend,
Though I haven’t got much voice,
And my joints are loth to bend.
It isn’t really Spring.
(Any solemn ass knows that!)
But I hear a bush bird sing,
So I think I’ll take my hat
And explore one sunny mile—
It’s not wise to go too far,
For it’s but a passing smile —
Singing softly, “Tra-la-la!”
N.S.W.

lery is to be extended inderinitelv on its
original lines the Commonwealtn a hundred
years hence will have a horribly stodgv col-
lection of forgotten politicians and figure-
heads on canvas. The bare thought of its
National Portrait Gallery in a.d. 2113 is a
nightmare. The first batch of pictures
oainted on approbation will be chosen
shortly, and now preliminary sketches are
invited of Lord Denman, J. c. Watson
Party Salmon, Albert Gould, Senator Turlev
and ex-Speaker McDonald. It is to be
hoped that tablets into the gilt frames
of the motley gathering of notabilities will
give something more than names and dates
Dim, future descendants of the present gene-
ration will want biographical details to edu-
cate them up to an understanding of the
subjects. When the Jokook lot come to be
painted in due course—not till their retire-
ment from office —it should be stated on

TOUJOURS LA POLITESSE.
"Pardon me remarking your magnificent
figure."
"Certainly. Excuse me referring to your re-
markable cheek."
Melbourne possesses one “hero.” at any
rate. He was negligently supporting a
verandah post in Swanston-street the other
afternoon, when a man, hastening by, whis-
pered a hurried word or two and dashed
on. A few yards behind, on the roadway,
came a bottle-oh’s turnout, conveying the
owner and a gesticulating constable, who
were both urging the ancient prad to unac-
customed speed. As they drew 'near, the
loiterer stepped smartly on the road, grabbed
the horse by the reins, and held him. Long
before the Law could persuade him that it
wasn’t a “bolt,” the quarry—a house-
breaker —had disappeared, and the Law was
a dour and disgusted animal.

- Australia is suffering from Ivmpho-
mama, says The Bulletin’s old anti-vae-
cunationist cynic.

The bulk of the population of Victoria is
unrepresented by any politician in the pre-
sent Federal Parliament. What is needed
is a member for football —one who plays,
talks, eats, smokes, kicks, sleeps, argues,
dreams and spits football. He should legis-
late solely for football—propose referen-
dums, executions, fires, murders, sudden
deaths, litanies, creeds, dogmas, decrees and
epistles to the honor, advancement and glori-
fication of the. expanded bull’s-skin. Such a
person would poll about five million votes
for the Senate, and have a job lasting
through all the eternities. The State is one
oofball field; and if any Paradise exists
where the hollow plunk
of foot on ball cannot be
heard, it should be re-
served as a prohibition
a rea for all time. On
Saturday afternoons from
the Murray to the sea
there rises to the clouds
one huge, opaque cry of
“Tear ’im down !” “Plug
ther cow !” “Yar ! Who
squared yer?” No town
in the State is dead, no
mining field played out,
no settlement deserted, if
it can find 18 men to
boot a bag of wind at a
given time on a
prearranged programme.
Special trains are run
for the convenience of
the god ; extra trams and
’buses, motors, dog-carts,
shays and delivery-vans;
steamers and four-horse
gunboats; obsolete
prams, coaches and bar-
rows, and all types of
wheeled, vehicle it has
occurred to the ingenuity
of man to devise. Foot-
ballers’ unions and
wages-boards; umpires
and stewards’ logs;
strikes of timekeepers
and lock-outs of women
with more than one hat-
pin—all are within view
of people of normal eye-
sight. Elections, small-
pox, finance, collisions,
divorces, droughts stumble
into conversation now
and again ; but they fall
back in front of “Sowth’s
eharnce” on Saturday,or
Seymour’s hope of biffing
Ararat. When the day of invasion comes, it
is practically certain that nine-tenths of the
population will be surrounded in the hoofball
arena, and staryed into submission. The
other tenth will probably be away erecting
an herofc statue to somebody who was pre-
sident of last year’s premiers.
The Adelaide Tramways Trust has won a
law-case at last. It has had plenty, but,
being merely a kind of Greater Adelaide,
representing all the corporations and dis-
trict councils in the metronolitan area, it has
generally seemed to juries to be fair game
for damages., This time it had smashed up
a motor-lorry belonging to the Adelaide Cor-
poration, whereupon the two public bodies
had a willing go, out of which nobody profited
except the lawyers. The lorrymen had spilt
some tar on the tram-rails, and in the end
this was held to have prevented the car
brakes from acting quickly enough. But the
•Case which really makes lawyers’ mouths
water is about to begin. The firm of Teas-
dale-Smith and Timms, which laid down the
trams, has a disputed bill, said to be about
£30,000, for extras of various sorts, involv-
ing over a hundred distinct items, each of
which has to be proved or disproved separ-
ately. On a preliminary _ canter, it was
mentioned in court, with a kind of rapturous
despair, that a small army of typists had
been at work oxt the documents in the case
for and had made hardly any impres-
sion on the mass.
The Baptist Chapel in Albert-street, East
xMelbourne, where the ' Rev. Isaac New
preached and prayed in the ’so’s, and
where he was succeeded by Charles Clark,
who won fame as a lecturer afterwards, has
been demolished. A hospital, to be run on
modern lines, has taken its place. The
change seems to conform to the spirit of the
time, somehow.

“Signor Trowsalini”: The Balkan Tur-
key-trot recalls that about the ninth cen-
tury, when the Bulgarians were changing
from Paganism to Christianity, they were
much harassed in mind about the proper
clothing of their women’s legs, and they
asked the Pope to solve the worrisome
problem. He solved it this way:—
Whether your women wear trousers instead of
skirts is of no moment to me; what I am concerned
with is faith and good works. Your customs are
not those of other Christians. Your women wear
trousers, and you fear that that may be accounted
to you for a sin ; for you know that in our Books it
is written that trousers were not ordained for
women, but for men. Hence you have thought it
your duty to consult me. Do not be anxious in this
matter. Do as you are accustomed. Preserve your
ancient customs, or adopt ours, since in some degree
you become other men in becoming Christians. After
all, whether you and your women preserve or aban-
don trousers," it will not help you one jot towards
salvation, nor increase your holiness.
In other words, the clothes don’t make the
Christian. This is, of course, only the view
of a Pope—and a fairly long-range Pope at
that. Still, I ask The Bulletin (with
awe at my own blasphemous temerity) if it
doesn’t look as sane a view as that of some
of our good missioning friends, who will in-
sist on presenting converted Binghi with a
shirt which he wears on his legs, and trou-
sers which he ties around his neck, not to
mention a hat in which he tries to boil yams?
The cheerful soul who would “sooner have
small-pox than be vaccinated,” had a bad
time last week. Four of him, in each case
the only unvaccinated person in the house,
were stricken in three days. What view
the cheerful soul takes of it now
has not yet been made public. Another
joke was provided by the man who made a
fiery speecli at an anti-vaccination meeting,
and when he got home found that a house
close to his own was infected. Thereupon
all the anti oozed out of him, and there was
another arm jabbed very early next morn
ing.
Mauritius is a
useful little con-
venience. Herbert
Henniker Heaton,
son of “Penny
Post’’ John, who be-
comes Assistant
Colonial Secretary
there, is not the
only Australian or
Australianised offi-
cial whom the little
handful of people
have had to take
at the Crown’s bid-
ding. The island
is looked on as a good
training ground. A.
H. Gordon, who
steamed around here
as High Commissioner

graduated, through

George Bowen, who
was Governor in Vic-

picked up something
worth knowing in the
Frenchified settle-

also, after being Gov-
ernor of Tasmania
and Victoria, was
dropped ; for a while
into Mauritius, but
that was because no-
thing else could be
done with him just
then. The stepping-
stone may perhaps yet lead, 'if the
Labor party continues to swallow its pledges
to abolish the office, to young Herbert Ilen-
niker riding out behind postilions to Cran-
brook, with a copy of the Evening News
under his arm. At any rate, he ought to do
something for the Evening News ; for his
mother was a Bennett, and her share in the
profits allowed the young man to enter the
“aristocratic” service.
“Y”: The Australasian says the motto
of Henry Willis, late Speaker of N. S.
Wales, was “Stand off the grass.” Not so!
He never looked on himself as grass. “Nemo
me impune lacessit,” he has been heard to
murmur, or, roughly Englished, “If you hit
me, I’ll hit you.” Which brings me up to
the point: Is “Stand off the grass” English
or is it good Australian? I think it is Aus-
tralian. The first time I find it used was
in connection with the present Wynyard
Square, at a time when it was the muster-
ing ground of the old barracks. There were
a few grassy borders, and, as people used to
flock in to see the drills, Colonel Despard,
then in command, put up notices: “Stand off
the grass.” So much was his Order resented
that he became known through the city as
“Stand off the grass.” This Despard, by
the way, was the son of a captain who had
joined a smothered conspiracy to get rid of
the dreadful old King George lll—and
“Sassiety” never forgot it.

CALF LORE.
[FOR TH EBULLETIN.]
The slashed skirt is usually open on the side as
high as the knee, and with it are worn stockings on
which snakes, lizards, birds and such things are
woven.- —Melbourne paper.
Because young Bertie drank delight
Of battle with his beers,
And looked, on wine and lips when red,
Each croaker wagged a pious head,
And entertained grave fears.
They reckoned Bert a hopeless case ;
Then heard with blank amaze
That he, who used to smoke, drink, sup,
And go the pace, had taken up
The nature study craze!
And yet ’twas true. Bert studies snakes
And lizards now, and knows
Where may be seen a splendid pair
Of wallabies —they have their lair
On Kitty’s silken hose!

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“F.II.”: After all, the 3*f earthworm, it
seems, is a godsend to Australia. Even the imported
earthworm, which “continually drives back and
starves out” the native in settled districts, does his
share in nitrifying the soil. By dragging vegetables
into his burrow, he fertilises Australia, and assists
in drought-prevention by making the soil better able
to hold water. These facts have been made avail-
able by the Victorian Government, in the Julj'
Journal of Agriculture. The worm-article in
question, though in general full of comfort, makes
one allegation of quite a disturbing nature. It is
that Gippsland is infested, to its benefit, by a large
earthworm, specimens of which have been found
measuring 86in. in length and 2Rn. around the
waist. “Their burrows,” according to the worm ex-
pert, “are very long and twisted, and the animal
produces a peculiar gurgling noise as it moves about
in them.” The giant earthworm, one learns, has
nothing worth mentioning in his brain-box. But it
seems a good thing for the beer-biters of Gippsland
that he has brains enough to make a practice of
staying in his burrow. After 20 drinks at the Farm-
ers’ Legs, it would be a nasty midnight experience
to meet him (7ft. 2in. of him) out with a bunch of
his mates. If 150,000 of well-organised Gippsland
worms would go out to meet an invader, what a
time that invader would have!
: If the evidence produced bv
ar 00 6/2/ 13) has failed to convince
“Souter Johnnie” (B. 10/7/T3) of the efficacy of
Morrissey’s snake-bite antidote, perhaps the following
undoubted facts might help to shake Johnnie’s scep-
ticism. On and about the Northern Rivers (N.S.W.)
there are, at the present time, at least half a dozen
persons who have been cured of snake-bite by
Morrissey. One of these, a boy of 12, was in a state
of collapse from two bites of a huge tiger-
snake—a species admitted by “Souter J.” to be
somewhat on the venomous side—before the appli-
cation of the cure. At Lismore, whilst giving an
exhibition with a sizeable death-adder, which
he customarily housed within his shirt, Morrissev
was h.mself bitten on the lip. In such a case, the
only available place for a ligature would have been
around the neck, and this might not have been good
or Morrissey’s wind. He, therefore, trusted solelv
o Ins own antidote, and, although much affected by
the venom, recovered. Three fowls and a dog, sub-
sequently bitten by the same reptile, died almost
immediately. Morrissey makes a standing offer to
divulge his business to any Government, Board of
Health, or Medical Board for a consideration, and
he is also open to back its effectiveness, with him-
self as the subject for trial, against all-comers of
the sixth-day creation—the cobra of India included.
How much more can a man do to get a scientific
inquiry ? Does anybody expect him to offer to be
bitten by elephants, sword-fish, hippopotami, lions,
sharks, devil fish, dogs and mining speculators?
Melbourne Herald lately had an informa-
tive little article on place names in the Weary State.
Two original names are the invention of surveyor G.
W. Wilmot, who plotted the Mallee district where
the townships of Willaura and Ilelendoite now are.
He wanted names for these districts, and, bethinking
himself of his lady friends, mused “Will Laura or
Helen do it?” An “e” was added to the latter,
and they stand as they are. Ballarat is alleged to be
Irish, from the hut of James Curtis, a shepherd
thereabouts in the pre-gold days—Ballagh hut, the
hut on the main road—thence Ballahut, clipped or
smoothed into Ballarat. Some, however, say the word
is native, and means “camping-place.” This seems
improbable, for almost every place was Binghi's
camping-place when he wanted to camp. As well
call a town in England Beer Place, because beer is
sold there, as if it wasn’t sold anywhere else. War-
renheip is the name of a hill—“emu feathers”—-in
the district. The obvious Swan Hill was so named
in 36 by Minor Mitchell. Lillydale—always so spelt
by the Age —is allegedly called after Clayworks’ song
of that name, then popular; but the Argus always
spells the name Lilydale, after Lily de Castella, of
St. Hubert’s vineyard, a few miles farther out. Ben-
digo was sponsored by a boxing shepherd, who had
been nicknamed after the famous Yiddish bruiser,
Abednego. Captain Bull rechristened it Sandhurst’
after the English military college, but a
plebiscite in 'B7 restored the old name. Port Fairy
got its monniker in ’27 from the barque Fairy,
Captain Wishart, which sheltered there in a gale.
The well-named Walhalla or Valhalla, which is
high enough to suggest Heaven, was so branded by
John Johnson, jun., chairman of claim seven at
Stringer’s Creek. Gippsland got its name from
N.S.W.’s Governor Gipps. Count Strezlecki, who him-
self stood sponsor for a still-thriving mining town,
did the christening act. There should, by the way, be
only one “e” in the name. The Austrian Count
was properly Strzlecki. Yarra-yarra is “ever-
flowing” ; Yan Yean was one of the chiefs who
“signed” the Batman treaty; Jika-jika another.
Bungaree, an aboriginal State Fighter, was the last
chief of the Sydney tribe. So far no one
has touched more than the fringe of an attractive
subject. 'Whence, for instance, came Toorak,
Prahran, Footscray, and what do they mean?

From the King Country (M.L.) :
A cocky over here was complaining to the Roads
Department about the state of his road of access,
and wanted it fixed up. The Department diun’t Know
much about the track, and had no officer handy to
report on it; so it wrote to the cocky, asking him
to say just how bad the thing was. The reply ran
this way: “In reply to yours, the road I have been
complaining about has got so infernal bad that I
have now got to lead my dog along it.”

“Congolley”: Hard luck is what I called
it, but you ought to have heard Scotty’s interpre-
tation. He was sitting with a look of intense suffer-
ing on his dial when a pal ambled up and nodded
invitingly towards the pub. Scotty rose, looking as
sour as the Devil on Ash Sunday. Knowing he was
“stiff,” we thought he should, at least, have looked
pleased. Presently he came out and explained: “1
was as dry as a bone all the morning,” he grizzled,
“and at last I went and had a big swig of water.
I’d hardly put the billy down, when, strike me dead,
along comes Snuffler and says, ‘Come up and ’ave a
pint.’ ” The rest of his remarks were put out by
the fire brigade.
“G.R.N.”: “J. 0.8.” (B. 10/7/’l3) says
the naming of Garden Island, in Sydney Harbor, is
“lost in mystery.” “Historical Records” (Vol. vi.,
page 169) leaves the reason for the name in no
doubt. When Phillip arrived in Port Jackson with
the first fleet, it was foreseen that one of the party's
most serious difficulties would be the food
supply, and every officer was at once
given an allotment, so that he might
grow his own vegetables. The two men-
of-war that were to remain on the station
were also allowed each a plot for a
cabbage garden. The Supply was given
a lot on Dawes Point, and the Sirius was
given Garden Island. When the Sirius
was ordered to the Cape for food supplies
at the end of 1788, a member of the
crew was left behind to look after this
ship’s cabbagery. The following entry
in the Sirius's log-book settles the date
of the christening of the island: —Febru-
ary 11th, 1788. Sent an officer and
party of men to the Garden Island to clear
it for a garden for the ship’s company.”
After the Sirius left, the crew of ' the
Lady Nelson cultivated the patch, and the
island also began to be used for other naval pur-
poses. Another interesting fact in connection with
the speck is that the first poultry farm in Australia
was established on it by Governor Hunter and placed
under the care of Mrs. Winch.
“Bulloak” : When William, of Tree Fern,
made up to Kate Ryan, the moon was bright and the
weather was feeling good. He escorted her home
over the two-mile road, and never a word said he
but, “It’s a bonzer night for ’possums, Kate.” Next
night he met her again and poured the same remark
into her shell-like ear. This went on for a week
and Kate bore it patiently in the belief that when
the moon waned and darkness was upon the earth
and the sins thereof, William would be more ex-
plicit. So one dark night she encouraged him all
she could, but without making her bashful lover
loosen his tongue until the journey home was nearly
completed. Then he blurted it out. “Kate,” he
said, “er” “Yes, William,” she murmured.
“Er —a bloke wouldn’t have a dorg’s chamce with
the ’possums to-night!” That settled Bill.
“Billy T.” : It had been a rousing tight
from the shake right to the dead-finish in round 18.
Micko was down twice for nine seconds before the
fifth round was finished. In the seventh he began
to revive, and then it was Ponny’s turn to touch
wood. Ponny examined the cracks in the flooring
five times in rapid succession; then a happy cross
reached Micko and so sickened him that for two
rounds his energies were spent in pedestrianism.
Three fairly even, ding-dong rounds followed, and
both lads sustained grievous facial derangements. It
was Micko’s luck to finish Ponny with a solid body
punch in the 18th, and he came out a sad and sorely
man-handled conqueror. They assisted him to the
bathroom. Ardent admirers flocked round him. A
big bull-butcher who had taken 3 to 1 about it was
heartiest in appreciation. “It was a great fight,
lad —a lovely fight!’’ he said. “Oh, yaas,” Micko
admitted, holding down an eye that threatened to
assume the dimensions of a football, and with the
other surveying his disarranged countenance in a
hand-mirror ; “’twas a good enough fight. But, yeh
see, me sister died this mornin’, and / didn’t enjoy
it a bit.”

A MATTER OF SOUR BULLS AND THE GRAPES
OF FORTUNE.
[FOR TH EBULLETIN.]
It is a sad and solemn fact,
As those who know' her ways agree,
That Fortune has her cards all stacked
Before she deals to you and me ;
Her glance is fair, her eyes are sweet,
But the old party is a cheat.
For instance, Jane and I went out
One evening to the Bondi sands.
We strolled a little while about,
And I was fain to hold her hands.
I do not like her over-much,
But found some pleasure in the t ich.
I think her eyes were rather fine,
But that was all the charm she wore ;
Her contour had no single line,
Viewed from behind her or before,
Likely to hold my roving eye
Or twist my bosom to one sigh.
We caught the tram at half-past eight
(With Lucy I had stayed till ten),
I took her home and did not wait,
But wandered forth to beer with men ;
For I was satisfied that Jane
Would never lure me out again.
Now Lucy had a mighty charm ;
The simple roundness of her waist
Fitted the curving of my arm,
And both ner lips were good to taste,
While an especial sort of prize
Lurked in the corners of her eyes.
I loved her very much indeed,
And frequently would tell her so ;
Our courtship went along with speed.
It seemed that no one had a show
To win her young and tender heart,
And break our pleasantness apart
Till that confounded uncle died—
Jane’s uncle—with his herd of sheep,
Or flock of cows, on pastures wide
All left by will for Jane to keep.
And then, of course, I had to see
Whether the girl would marry me.
I asked her on my bended knees
In such a fine and knightly style.
1 said: “Dear maiden, will you please
Consider love like mine worth while?”
And when she spoke a tender “No,”
My friends remarked, “We told you so.”
And so I thought the wealth could go—
I did not want her mob of bulls ;
She should not have a second show.
I left her to the other fools,
And stalking forth in all my pride
Asked Lucy to become my bride.
The maid declined without ado.
She said that she bad just arranged
With one nice gentleman she knew
To have her name and status changed.
She recommended me to Jane,
Who owned some bulls, though she was plain.
And that is why I married Kate,
The nicest girl of all the three,
Who, though I sought her somewhat late,
Was always very fond of me;
And Kate, though very far from plain,
Was really wealthier than Jane.
And this is how old Fortune plays
Her mad and foolish wedding pranks,
And mixes up a lover’s praise
V ith soulless talk of bulls and banks ;
For all her careless make-believe
Some card is always up her sleeve.
x.s.w.

Ideas. James Watt, after inventing his steam
engine, invented a pop valve, which avoided undue
boiler pressure and prevented explosions. Then he
invented the governor. Next he invented the glass
water gauge, which plainly ind : cates the height of
water in the boiler. Next he invented the steam in-
dicator. There are portraits of Watt painted by Sir
Joshua Reynolds, by Romney, and by Gainsborough.
Watt died aged 83, respected to the last. Phillips
Ormonde * Co., 17 Queen St., Melbourne, patent and
trade mark attornevs.*
AMAZING HAIR GROWTH.
WOMAN’S HAIR REACHES TO HER
FEET.
Astonishing Results from World-wide
Tests of Possibilities of Hair Growth.
London, May 9. —The publication of the
results of wliat is doubtless the most exten-
sive series of tests ever conducted for deter-
mining the possibilities of hair growth, shows
beyond doubt that it is at last possible for
any man or woman, whether afflicted with
dandruff, grey hair, falling hair, or even
baldness, to secure a strong, luxuriant
growth of glossy hair of almost any length
desired. In making these tests, which num-
bered many thousands, and extended over a
period of more than two years, a lotion or
tonic was used consisting of 3oz. Bay Rum,
loz. Lavona de Composee, and 5 dram Men-
thol Crystals; this being rubbed briskly into
the scalp twice a day. The results were
simply astonishing. Dandruff, scalp itching,
and falling hair all stopped in from one to
three applications; grey hair gradually as-
sumed its natural color, new hair was grown
on heads that had been bald for years, and
in countless instances hair increased from
one to two feet in length, one lady reporting
that her hair reached to her feet, having
grown thirty inches in less than two years.
Averaging the whole series' of tests, the num-
ber who reported unsatisfactory results was
only nine to the thousand, or less than one
per cent. While any chemist can readily
prepare this tonic from his stock of standard
pharmaceutical products, it is interesting to
note that, owing to the frequency with
which they are asked to put it up, most
chemists now have it prepared by a well-
known London firm of manufacturing
chemists, and keep it in stock ready for im-
mediate delivery under the name of Lavona
Hair Tonic. In view of the remarkable
proved merit of this article, every man and
woman who is suffering from any form of
hair or scalp trouble, as well as those who
desire to retain and enhance the natural
beauty of their hair, should at once get a
bottle of Lavona Hair Tonic, and give it a
thorough trial. You will be astonished and

“Catamain”: “Souter Johnnie” (B.
10/7/T3) discredits Morrissey’s snake-bite antidote.
There are a couple of reasons why it has not been
heard of so much lately. One is that a
Mullumbimby (Tweed River) firm has patented the
remedy, so that the crowd which had worked with
Morrissey, and had got hold of his remedy, has been
squeezed out, and has left off giving exhibitions.
The other reason is that Morrissey is ill, and that
Rookwood Benevolent Asylum authorit'es don’t en-
courage demonstrations with death-adders on their
premises.
“Lagani Namo” jettisons some literary
cargo from Papua: One of the curses of the Posst'
sion is the multiplicity of languages or dialects.
Don’t know how many there are, but I reckon that
each of the 400 known villages has one to its
own chin. Frequently, in a court case, three native
interpreters have to be employed in order that the
complications may not be overlooked. I g've you
my word they never are. Motuau (Port Moresby
yabber) promises to become the official lack of taci-
turnity. Missionaries have made some efforts to
teach English to our savage brother, with results
that call for no remarks likely to preserve the
peace. After several years at a mission school, under
the tuition of the white shepherd, the cannibal has
been known to learn, “Yes, sir” ; “No, sir” ; “C-a-t,
cat.” Even the colored teachers (Samoans, Raraton-
gans, etc.) at the out-stations serve out this sort of
hash: “My wife’s love to you very big strong.
You very pretty nice man.” Further, each village
and, sometimes, each section of a village has cus-
toms as different as the dialects. One or two: A
boy, when smitten with girl-fever, procures the big-
gest green betel nut available (the betel nut is
about the size and shape of a small hen egg), carves
his most cherished motifs on it and sends it to
the chosen damsel. If she reciprocates his views,
she eats the kernel and displays the husk when next
she meets the donor. The “boys” do not mind how
affable the girls were before they were taken to
wife; but deceived husbands get fatally busy on
co-re. with a stone club or a tomahawk —unless
co-re. purchases the lady. Some Papuans are very
considerably married. I met one gay old chief in
the Purari with 17 wives. Some have more; pro-
bably it serves them right. In the delta, when a
man kills another, he, in thanksgiving, goes up to
the dubu (men’s assembly) with a flaring torch.
This he will suddenly throw away ; then anyone who
wishes can go and make love to his wife. Which
shows an uncommonly friendly spirit.
“Maire”: Henare Maori need not go to
the bottom when the inventive mind class is called
up. I’ve taken out a patent for his cow-locating
scheme. Up in north M.L., where the average small
cocky puts his trust in Providence until he can
afford a cheap fence (after which he wouldn't trust
P. with anything on hoofs), the goddess has a
habit of lying down in some secluded spot to in-
dulge in the American habit of cud-chewing.
Although the horned lady generally has a bell
attached to her necklace, it is, on those occasions,
silenced. I was raging through the scrub one even-
ing when I met Henare, and explained the trouble.
“Aw, eripes,” he said, “that te easy one. Next
time you turn te cow out, grease te horns with
stinkin’ fat. Te werry stinkin’ all te petter.
Orright. Te blowfly come roun’ an’ settle on te
cow's head, and te cow shake te head like hell.
Then te bell ring.”

“Flinderino” : In “A Sydney-side Saxon,”
by Rolf Boldrewood, there is a yarn about two un-
clean shepherds, Tom and Dick, who returned to
their hut one evening and found some writing in
chalk on the hut door. Neither of them could read,
and, thinking the writing was an important message,
they took the door off its hinges and carried it some
miles to the homestead, where the boss read, to their
disgust:—
Tom and Dick, of Yalco Green,
The dirtiest pair that ever was seen.
In Cairns, three years ago, I visited a one-time short-
story writer named Bell, and he showed me a book
of camp-fire yarns that he had published long ago. In
it I found an illustrated story about another “dirtiest
pair that ever was seen.” I told Bell that I had
read that yarn before, but he claimed it as his own
manufacture, and said Brisbane Figaro had printed
it first. Now I have just read “On the Wool Track,”
by C. E. W. Bean, and find a story of one Slimy Sam,
and the same old writing on the door, only this time
the inscription was —
Slimy Sam, of Mumblebone,
Was the dirtiest beggar ever known.
There are many Australian books I have not read yet,
and I’m wondering how many other people have car-
ried that old door five miles to the nearest scholar.
“Navvie” : Re the homing instinct of the
horse (B. 3/7/’l3). I knew a nag that was bought
by the S.A. Government from a small station near
Strathalbyn, and sent out to a survey camp in the
Pinnaroo country. He was in camp about a year,
when he broke away in hobbles during a thunder-
storm, and arrived back at his old home three days
afterwards. That meant a flight (in hobbles) of
about 120 miles, and he must have swum the Mur-
ray (still in hobbles) to do it. Evidently water was
no trouble to him, as he was afterwards shipped to
Port Lincoln, per the steamer Investigator, which
grounded up Wallaroo way. The stock were put over-
board, and all, with the exception of this old warrior
and his mate, made shorewards. These two steered
a course for Port Adelaide, and had to be rounded
up with a dinghy. His last survey job happened on
Kangaroo Island. There the old longing for home
must have come over him again, as he broke away
and struck out for the mainland. The lightkeepers
at Cape Willoughby sighted him doing eight knots
across Backstairs Passage; but as he has not yet been
reported by Cape Jervis light, and it’s two years
since he left, it’s a fair thing to say he was drowned.
“Catamain” : Among the Richmond River
(N.S.W.) blacks the man-making ceremony didn’t
deprive the newly-fledged warrior of any privileges,
but admitted him to full rights of citizenship in
every way. Until he was made a man he could not
marry, take part in a corroboree, or eat kangaroo,
carpet-snake, codfish, porcupine, or kobbi (species
of native bee honey). On one occasion I was
enabled to be present at the ceremony. When the
women had been sent out of sight and hearing, the
bucks formed a ring round the principal actors. A
boy being stripped, two bucks got hold of him, one
on each side, and with one hand held his arms
extended, and with the other, pressed against the
top of his chest in such a manner as to prevent him
moving. The operator then cut slits across the
youth’s chest with a sharpened stone, at the same
time making a “thithithith” sound, evidently sup-
posed to represent a cry of pain suppressed by
thrusting the tongue between chattering teeth. Then
he gashed a few slits in the upper arms, at each
cut giving a gasping “ugh.’’ These were the only
sounds uttered the whole time, the boy and all the
bucks being as silent and still as statues. The boy
was then led forward to the fire, and ashes were
thrown into his wounds. After all the eligibles had
been treated like that, the whole crowd sat down
to a feed, at which the new men got their introduc-
tion to the previously-prohibited articles of diet.
By the way, among the same blacks the gins didn’t
lose any teeth at marriage, but, instead, were pre-
sented with a wedding ring in the form of a strand
of hair tied round the little finger close to the
hand. In a comparatively short time this com-
pletely severed the finger and left a stump with the
skin drawn over the end in approved surgical
fashion.
“Silas Snell”: Witnessed an entertaining
hubbub in our train from “Sout’ Melbin’' the other
evening. The platform at Flinders-street had just
been reached. Two men stepped briskly aboard for
the return journey, and, making for the same cor-
ner, collided and rebounded. One sat in the
cuspidore, the other knocked his occiput with some
violence against the corner of the high seat. In-
stantly words began to flow ; then they flew. “Where
the warmth are you goin’, you bun-footed, hen-faced
blitherer?” “Can’t you see what you’re at, you
blighter ?’’ They scrambled for the corner again.
Blitherer just got it. Blighter trod on his feet.
“Excuse me—my seat!” he said, with the air of a
man meditating murder. “Not much!” responded
Blitherer. “Are you going to shift?” “Not an
inch! Take the rest of the train —you’re welcome.”
Blighter seized Blitherer by the head, sudden-jerked
him out of the corner, and dumped his own carcase
into it. Blitherer was justly indignant. From his
place on the floor he took Blighter by the left leg,
and smartly dashed him among the cigarette butts.
The pair compared holes, and tumbled about a bit
for 30 seconds, fighting for the corner. Blighter
got back, but sadly dishevelled, and with a second-
hand cigar in his left ear. Blitherer fastened on
Blighter’s head, and, pulling him off, resumed pos-
session. Blighter returned to the attack. Blitherer
clung to the woodwork, and hung on. “My seat!”'
said Blighter. “Yeraliar!” said Blitherer. No
blows were struck, but garments were torn, and
collar-studs went missing. Blighter’s hat was
trodden out of recognition, all the buttons were off
Blitherer’s vest, and two-thirds of his shirt was
flowing free. A porter stepped aboard. “My seat!”
reported Blighter breathlessly. “’S a lie!” retorted
Blitherer, “’s my seat. I got here first.” The porter
was unmoved. He merely barked: “This carriage
not goin’!”
“Gouger”: One of the most depressing
jobs I have undertaken was on the Russell (N.Q ).
A fall of stone buried an old fellow, and we got
him out with a broken leg. It was his lucky day.
While a stretcher was being made, an old hand split
up a kerosene case, constructed splints and set the
leg. It happened to be a clean break. Then nine
of us set about carrying the patient to Herberton,
spell about, one man humping the tucker. The hos-
pital was over 40 miles away, and the track lay
through scrub, down ravines and up the sides of
precipitous mountains. The first night we camped
half-way, squatting down to wait for the light of
day to show the track. Around the old cove on the
stretcher we wrapped a tent-fly, but all we had for
our own covering was on our bodies—-a flannel shirt,
old dungarees and sodden boots. All day it had
rained, and all that night the deluge went on sul-
lenly and without ceasing. At the first streak of
dawn we went on again, stumbling, dispirited and
with blood squelching in cur boots as a result of
the attentions of myriads of accursed leeches. It
was dusk when we reached Herberton, and, although
we were deadbeat with fatigue, we made the few
bob we had between us get into circulation to
brighten things up. Herberton reckoned things were
illuminated considerably. The doctor said the broken
leg was set as well as he could have done it himself.
And he is not the first expert by a mile who has
had occasion to praise the quick-and-ready surgery
of the bush.

BACK O’ LILYDALE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is a path along a fence,
And scrub on either side—
Dogwood and messmate, green and dense,
Where all the wild things hide.
And on this path the lizards glide
Across the sun-barred clay,
And over all that green hillside
The boisterous breezes play.
Ten miles bad: o’ Lilydale,
And forty miles away.
Between the pathway and the skies
The dogwood branches spread,
And curtain from my dreaming eyes
The path that lies ahead,
And yet I see the old homestead
(For dreaming-sight is keen)
And the ploughed paddocks, fresh and red,
The hillside grassland green.
Ten miles back o’ Lilydale
And forty miles between.
Lilydale—and ten miles back.
A weary road and long,
And, at the last, a narrow track
Beneath the currajong,
And, here, the echo of a song
Out of a bygone year—
A whisp’ring voice that drifts along
Where once it rang so clear.
Ten miles back o’ Lilydale.
And forty miles from here.
VICTORIA.
PATRICIA MURCHISON.

‘‘D°m Bill” Lyne was impecunious ; “Oom
Bill Tyne didn’t always pay his debts. He
would put money in his pocket to pay a
tradesman; three minutes later he would
empty it out to relieve a deadbeat. This was
the only foundation for many cruel stories
that were told—vague stories in which the
mg, bluff Australian figured as a sort of
political three-card man. A managing direc-
tor of one of Australia’s biggest financial
concerns was talking of “Oom Bill” a while
back. Bill had been put on the Board of this
concern very many years ago, and had been
permitted to hold his seat ever since. “There
ba 7 e b ? en .dozens and dozens of times when
old Bill might have made a commission,” he
?aid. ‘The fact was he didn’t even know
the chance was offering. He had no more
idea of turning a bit of early information to
his own financial advantage than he had of
Hying. He sometimes let 'people put him
into a false position because he didn’t grasp
what they were driving at. When he did
grasp it there ikas a big, wild man using
vigorous language. I’ve never known Bill
do a crooked thing, and that’s on 25 years’
close business acquaintance.” But the best
proof of all that the old man’s enemies lied
about him was his persistent poverty Not
that it worried him: he really had‘no use
for money except to give it away.
Thomas Sutherland, chairman of the P.
and O. Co., in London, and oracle on all
matters connected with the British mercan-
tile marine* has decided, at 80 years of age,
to retire from his strenuous job. Consider-
able part of his strenuosity was employed
in shovelling hostility upon (he White Aus-
tralian policy. Also, he perspired mentally
in opposition to the Australian conditions
imposed on Britain’s shipping owners under
the Federal Navigation Bill, which is at
present awaiting the assent of George V.
In between times he attended to other
things, but those already mentioned made
him of interest to Australians. It wasn’t
an absorbing interest.

Exit, in London, G. W. Taylor, the Man
who really Broke the Bank at Monte Col-
iins-street.. Taylor was a decent, kindly
super-imaginative speculator in real estate
who thought, when Victoria borrowed
twenty-five millions between 1886 and
1890, that Melbourne was destined to be
very soon as big as New York, and the
capital of a . thickly-populated country,
laylor s belief in the greatness of Australia
was with him mere megalomania. Only
one land-boomer was more obsessed with
the idea, and that was B. J. Fink; and
both backed their opinion splendidly—with
other people s money. G. W. Taylor was
Mayor of P’ran in its palmy days, and it
was from C. H. James that he learnt the
art of buying suburban paddocks at 3d. a
toot, and cutting them up to sell at £3.
He had more bills under discount—
frequently several sets for the same block —
during 1888 than any other two land-
boomers unitedly. Here was the group:
B. J. Fink, T. Bent, C. H. James, G. W.
laylor, J. McA. Howden, W. L. Baillieu,
M. 11. Davies.. Every one of them was a
paper millionaire. Of course, there were
satellites, but these were the captains of
the industry of buying land cheap and sell-
mg dear. Finally, the Bank of Monte
Collins-street became oppressive and menac-
ing, and a company was proposed to buy
all G. W. Taylor’s assets. The capital
suggested was £4,500,000. The directors
were to be B. J. Fink. Alfred Deakin, J.
McA. Howden and G. W. Taylor. But the
scheme failed, and Taylor was given a
cheque to go to London; and he went with
much celerity.

Randwick-they secured nine boxes of Cigars, thr ee

of tobacco.

Catholic “Reformer” who has made a splash
in London with his plain talks about “the
sins of Society,” is to visit Sydney next

THE NATIONAL BRAND.
Ginger : The Australian track has seen
more illustrious swaggies than Ralph \v ii-
liams, who rose to be a colonial Governor and
k.C.M.G. (B. 17/7/T3). We’ve even had
a king among us—Jorgensen, of Iceland.
Australian caricaturist Will Dyson, who
has developed into an effective public speaker
in England, was called upon recently to ad-
dress a large gathering of students at
uxtord, m a furiously radical vein, on “The
conspiracy to convert people into things
easily governed.” He took so well that
there was an enthusiastic demand for an-
other dose, and Dyson responded with an
address on “Cartoons.” Oxford had been
split into fierce factions over the question of
excluding the Daily Herald from the read-
because of the “blasphemous” and
disloyal _ qualities of its caricatures. In
the resulting disputation the artist was em-
braced as a sort of Bird of Freedom by the
large, democratic minority. By the way,
Dyson’s upward movement in England has
been given further impetus by Frank Rutter.
8.A., Curator of the Leeds Art Gallery, and
a foremost critic. In the course of a lecture
ue advised those English people who doubted
their own taste in pictures, to cut out Will
Dyson s brilliant cartoons,” mount them on
brown paper, and decorate their homes with
them. _ ‘ln a few years’ time,” he said,
collections of Dyson’s cartoons will be as
eagerly sought after as those of Gillray are
to-day.”
The youngest Anglican Bishop in the
Commonweaßh is John Oliver Feetham, of
JNorth Queensland, who entered the forties
on January 28, and wore the gaiters for the
rif st °. n 25. A Bishop’s job in
the .North is neither lucrative nor restful
but in those respects it matches Feetham’s
previous billets. He put in eight years as a
curate in Bethnal Green (London), and six
more m a Bush Brotherhood with its head-
quarters at Dubbo (N.S.W.), though he
was chiefly occupied in arid corners
° f t m-u v i ne > T ard such as Canbelego
and libooburra. As head of the
Brotherhood, he was allowed 10s a week
for picket money. The Dubbo Brotherhood
has never boasted a "nug” like parson Sams,
who belongs to a similar society in Central
Queensland , but it used to have a good demo-
crat in C. 11. S. Matthews— “Brother
Lharles —who was practically hounded out
ot Australia by the wealthy members of his
flock because he dared to speak on a Labor
platform.. Feetham was similarly an object
of suspicion, because of his fondness for con-
sorting with sleeper-cutters, tank-sinkers,
fearers, and other riff-raff, and because he
didn t dress like a gentleman.” The prin-
cipal peculiarity of his dress nowadays is
that he is one of the few Anglican Bishops
in Australia game to wear a mitre in public.
Most of the others keep that article only
tor display on their notepaper and motor-
cars.
THE HARP AND THE TROMBONE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As tyrants, women are ridiculous; as ministering
angels, there is no limit to their power. The harp
is their instrument—not the trombone.
—Beerbohm Tree.
Peace, lovelj' woman 1 Keep your nerves in check,
And let not passion crack your voice’s tune;
We know of sorrows you’ve a perfect peck,
Beset by squalling brats and trousered loon;
Yet none the less suppress a grievous groan ;
Touch up the harp and drop the mad trombone!
If Bill comes rolling home, aflood with beer,
And dances fiercely on your cups and cans,
Slang-whang him not, but hail him “duck” and
“dear,”
And let him dance fandangoes on the pans ;
And should he sing some ditty all his own,
touch then the harp and leave the mad trombone!
When to your sweet domestic tribe of ten
Another comes by Fate’s supreme decree,
And podgy Bob and sturdy little Ben
In hourly conflict grip and disagree,
The while the bonused infant makes its moan—
Again the harp and not the mad trombone!
When scandal-mongering neighbor Betsy Prig
At your expense wags round a vicious tongue,
As thus: “She’s got a temper like a pig,
And drives ’er ’usband ev’ry night to Bung;
The likes of ’er is better left alone”—
Again the harp and not the mad trombone!
And when you can’t get even with the rent,
And Grubbe the grocer growls about his bill,
And then you find, with worry overspent,
The “boss” has drowned his sorrow in the swill,
While Jack, your eldest, proves a useless drone—
Take up the harp, and leave the mad trombone!
And, last of all, should Chadband unctuouslv
Drop in and hail you, “Sister, pray for Grace,
Remembering we all must chastened be!”
Nay, do not throw the teapot in his face,
Or heave behind him malediction’s stone—
Take up your harp, and drop the mad trombone !
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

Fin's bank lias the foundations; now it wants the
(business. The foundations are very fine There is a
share capital of £1,195,398, besides £1,692,092 of
4 p c. inscribed deposits, the latter repayable at the
bank s option, although £15,000 must be put aside
■each half-year to reduce the debt. The £15,000
need not, however, come out of profits. Moreover
ithere is every reason to believe that the whole of
this share and inscribed capital, as well as the re-
serves, is represented by live assets worth 20s. in
the £. The management, when it was hammering
•out a scheme to reconstruct the old A.J.S. Bank
wrote assets down to what it believed to be bed-
aock ; and they were written down again after that
With intact funds of this size the A.B.C. could
safely take £.12,000,000 or £14,000,000 of deposits
xind other public money, and lend out £10,000,000
■or £11,000,000. As a matter of fact, all the de-
posit and other public money it has is £2,799 035
•and its advances total £3,864,629. One result of
having so much fixed or good-as-fixed capital is that
•depositors’ security is at present and must for some
.years to come be extraordinarily good. The bank
•could pay in prompt gold more than half of all it
•owes to depositors and other creditors, and it has
other assets valued at more than twice the total of
the other half of the debts. If the public is looking
for good security, it won’t be deposits that the A.B.C.
will be short of for some time to come. What the
bank needs more than anything else is a good bor-
rowing connection. And gradually it ought to
attract that too, for the wise borrower seeks a bank
which is strong enough to help him not so much
when times are good as when they are bad ; and this
bank is strong enough to do that. At present it
really is much too strong for the immediate profit
of its shareholders.
As to the year’s changes:—
„ ~ June, 1912. June, 1913.
