This paper would rather have seen the advances move
the other way. There was certainly no need in this
case to call in money in order to strengthen the
cash- position, for, as mentioned before, more than
oO p.c. of the liabilities (other than the inscribed
deposits) are represented by liquid assets; and those
liquid assets are nearly all gold and Australian
notes, which locally are as good as gold. Still, this
isnt a business that can safely be forced. There
was a time when bank managers were practically
driven out with lassos in their hands and told to
bring m borrowers, by gentle methods if possible,
by force if necessary; but that policy didn’t pay
then, and it wouldn’t pay now.
Regarding the shares as an investment, two things
at least must be noted. (1) The security is appar-
ently Al. The £1 shares are marketable at only
about 12s. Unless there have been dreadful miscal-
culations, there is 20s. of assets for every £1 of
capital, so the 12s. share is backed by 20s. of
assets. (2) The dividend is small, and is not likely
to be large for many years, for the capital is so big
that to pay even 5 p.c. would mean distributing
£23,900 a year more than is paid at present. Pro-
gress will probably be steady—assuming good man-
agement, of course—-but it is too much to expect it to
be rapid.

On capital and reserves combined the profit for the
past half-year was at the rate of £7 6s. 3d. p.c.
p.a., so it begins to look as though most if not all
of the capital has been reinstated. It has been a
long, uphill fight. Probably this bank was in no
better position than the average after the financial
crisis of the early ’9o’s; but, unlike the vast
majority, it didn’t get rid of its troubles by shut-
ting its doors for a few minutes and then compound-
ing with its creditors. Instead of even asking its
shareholders to regard some of their capital as gone,
it set to work to make good the losses (which it didn’t
admit) out of profits (which it thus wasn’t able to
admit either). It was heroic in a way, but was a mis-
take; people didn’t know how bad the position inside
might be, so they mostly passed the institution by.
Fortunately the bank had some strong friends, and
with the help of these the long struggle was main-
tained, and, as remarked above, it looks now as if
it had about reached an end. Certainly the market
doesn’t yet put a par value on the shares; one lot
of £8 shares are quoted at somewhere about £5
125., and the other lot (with a £2 liability) at
£5 ss. But the market is governed very largely by
the dividend paid and doesn’t take much heed of
the fact that in the past 12 months, wh ; le the
shareholders have had only 5 p.c., another 2J p.c.
or more has been put by or written off the premises
account.
Handicapped though it has been, the bank has
made pretty good progress during the past 10 years.
As at June, 1903, all the public’s money in the
business (deposits, etc.) was £1,251,999; to-day it
is £1,943,560. And while 10 years ago the ad-
vances were £1,204,941, they are now £1,601,900.
Nothing tremendous, of course, in the way of
growth; but probably the quality has improved
more than the quantity. At any rate, if the steadv
growth of the disclosed profits means anything at
all, it ought to mean that. This was a case in
the balance-sheet figures didn't mean what they
saia. assets, tor instance, were never openly written
down, no matter how little they were really worth

patient s health has been improving very rapidly
and his temperature isn’t now far from normal. y

This co. has been in the business 35 years, which
have been 35 years well spent. And it has accumu-
latea something besides wisdom and experience,
though it set out with a paid capital far greater
than most trustee cos. have thought necessary, it isn’t
content to offer its customers the security of that
capital ( £90,000) alone, but it is steadily building
up a reserve fund, and makes no secret of the fact
‘hat it is aiming at £lOO,OOO. This, of course, is
all to the advantage of the beneficiaries under the
trusts committed to the co. As it found out years
ag°. even the most careful trustee co., fortified bv
what looks like the best legal opinion, may misread
its instructions and become liable to beneficiaries ;
hence the T., E. and A. offers its clients a substantial
guarantee that, if it goes wrong, it will be able to
stand the consequences. As a matter of fact, the
risks are not large. But they are there; and the
tact that there has been no trouble for many years
is no proof that trouble won’t occur when, for in-
stance, a bad slump has reduced property values, and
left beneficiaries disappointed and disposed to fasten
the blame on to somebody. Moreover, this co. aims
at giving something more than good security on
paper Of its capital. £60,000 is represented bv
a Collms-street freehold which, though no doubt of
greater value than when it was purchased, has been
written down from about £70,000 to the present
kas £41,557 in public securities and
£6721 in cash; and of the balance £35,232 is on
mortgage. _ Considering the size of the reserves, the
dividend is small; but shareholders, as well as
clients, have fine security. And shareholders have
excellent prospects, too. The business has grown
steadily—the estates in hand were £7,467,202 10
years ago, and they are £10,120,497 now—and
such a strong case can be made out in favor of hav-
ing trustee work done by trustee cos. that it seems
impossible for the business, speaking generally to
go back to the old, untrained hands.

A very profitable little business, like that of most
trustee cos., and carrying comparatively small r.sks.
Capital and reserves combined amount to £36 588
and nearly £34,000 of that lot is in cash or public
securities; and practically all the rest is repre-
sented by severely written down office premises. Not
many businesses can show an average profit of about
9 p.c. on capital and reserves while keeping; those
funds in such gilt-edged securities. Certainly there
are other risks: a bad mistake made in reading a
will, for instance, might cost the co. thousands
But, with care, such mistakes seem to be easily
avoided. Where there are doubts the courts can
always be applied to for directions, in which respect
trustees have an advantage over most people who
have to do a thing first and find out whether it is
right or wrong afterwards. Moreover, the progress
of the co., so far as new business is concerned is
satisfactory. Five years ago the estates in hand
were valued at £1,341,899; now the total is
£l, (12,250. And there is plenty of room yet for
m spite of the advantages they offer, it is doubtful
whether trustee cos. do more than one-third "of the
trustee work in Victoria, notwithstanding that they
are more numerous and have a firmer hold there than
in any other State of the Commonwealth.
Automatic Telephones (Australasia),
Ltd. (Sydney).—'This co. was formed a couple of years
ago to acquire certain Australasian rights to the
patents of the Automatic Electric Co., of Chicago The
authorised capital is £250,000, in 245,000 ordinary
£1 shares and 100,000 deferred Is. shares Thus
far 100,000 ordinaries have been issued and are now
paid to 10s.; the deferred shares, which were ap-
parently issued for goodwill, belong wholly or mostly
to the Standard Waygood and Hercules Engineering
Co. (Sydney). An excellent bargain was made in
the purchase of the rights or agency: £10,500 was
paid as a deposit, to be forfeited if i. 0,000 lines were
not ordered before June, 1914. If the order came
to hand, the deposit was to be part of the payment
for the goods. As already 13,000 lines have been
sold and ordered, the deposit is safe. So far of
course, very little profit has beeen made; the pub-
hshed accounts nevertheless show a book-keeping
profit of £3 for the first year and £567 for the
P uwi^ ear ‘.,i® n ,J une 30 the value of contracts (made
™ lth 7he Commonwealth Government) was
£120,000, and tenders for another £115,000 worth
were then under the Department’s consideration.
Of the capital of £55,000, £31,357 is represented
by work in progress (less payments received), £5OOO
by goodwill, £10,500 by the deposit with the
Chicago co., and the balance is made up of cash,
iieposics with tenders, uiitl sundries. The accounts
naturally give no indication of the profits which the
co may expect; much depends on the bite the
Chicago people take, and as “it would seem desir-
able that some negotiations now in progress should
be conducted personally,” one of the managing direc-
tors is to go on that errand. These managing direc-
tors are the two king-pins of the Standard Waygood
Co., and what they thought of the prospects of the
c°- and the remarkable invention may be judged by
the fact that for the goodwill they took deferred
shares, which merely entitle the holders to half the
profits left after the ordinaries have got 10 p.c.

This co. was born in 1887, and a little while after-
wards it wished it never had been. That was when
it blew off the froth and found that the more or less
solid substance underneath represented only 4s. 6d
in the £ of one lot of shares and 9d. for each" 15s
of the other lot. Still, there was the nucleus of a
business, and G C. Willcocks, “Tom” Welsby and a
few more people took it in hand. After nursing it for
a while, they saw possibilities ahead, especially as
was waxing fat, and forgetting all its
old troubles, and showing- a disposition to fill-’em-
up-agam. In 1905 the capital, having been severely
written down, was still onlv £16,025. The build-
ings being poor and the equipment out of date, the
directors decided that the co. must have more money.
By degrees the capital has been brought up to £6O 000
the last issue of 66,666 shares having been made
last year at par (4s 6d.). Even that, however, has
f lv , en , . the co. less than it could do with very com-
fortably ; for in view of the ranid increase of busi-
ness, so much more has been absorbed by stock and
hook debts apart from the new plant altogether that
the iiab.lities haven’t been reduced at all: ’ toat

Thb Bulletin puts it that way with some reserva-
tions. Besides the formal reserve fund there is a
“depreciation insurance reserve,” which has hitherto
been built up of amounts shown above as written
off. But during the past six months £7OOO has
dropped into that account without mentioning where
it came from ; and there is no h.nt as to its charac-
ter. It may be ordinary trading profit, which is un-
likely ; or it may be profit made on a sale of pro-
perty ; or it may be simply that the co. has revalued
some assets and reckons them worth £7OOO more
than old book-values. With this addition the de-
preciation fund stands at £14,567, which amount
is not included with the reserves shown above. The
co., which is still under seven years old, has so fai
done very well; the Westralian thirst remains just
about the richest gold-bearing reef over on that side.
Capital is £60,000 ; liabilities of all kinds
£16,446. Against this and the reserves there is a
miscellaneous heap consisting of freeholds and lease-
holds, plant, goodwill and water supply, £104,856-
and stocks, book debts, etc., £11,270.' Whether the
miscellaneous heap is worth what the books allege
or whether the £14,567 to the credit of deprecia-
tion fund should be struck off the total, this paper
hasn t the slightest idea. But in anv case, it looks
a cheerful set of accounts, not the least cheerful
feature being the fine margin of profit left after the
10 p.c. div. is paid.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*
Mine managers and engineers contemplating Re-
inforced Concerte construction should write for par-
ticulars of the Indented Steel Bar, which gives the
greatest bond, and is the strongest and cheapest re-
inforcement on the market. Used by the N. S.
Wales and Queensland railways for bridges, by the
Sydney Water and Sewerage Board for elevated re-
servoirs, by the N. S. Wales Government Architect,
and by all the leading architects. Should guaranteed
designs be required for concrete structures, our ex-
pert will be pleased to furnish plans and descrip-
tions. Apply, Wm. Adams and Co., Ltd., Sydney,
Melbourne and Brisbane.*
Efficient grinding is one of the many problems
that mine managers have to solve. The Hardinge
Conical Mills have proved to the Calumet and Hecla
Mining Co. that grinding is not such a difficult
problem ; they have installed 64 of them, and say
“the Hardinge Conical Mill is the most efficient
grinding machine we have yet gotten hold of.” If
they say this of the mills, it should be worth the
while of mine managers here to inquire about them.
The Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are
the sole representatives for them in the Common-
wealth, and will be glad to forward particulars.* [1

Ample evidence of the increasing popularity of
the Hornsby manufactures is the fact that no less

Ltd., Uralla; The Phoenix Gold Recovery Co., Hill-
grove; and The True Blue Mines, West Wyalong—
within one week after exhaustive enquiries have
placed their order for the “Hornsby” Suction Gas
Engine and Plant. These plants are recognised by
experts to be thoroughly up to date, simple to work,
and a vast saving in fuel used, charcoal or coke
being all that is required to produce the gas, and
the small amount of attention required. R. Hornsby
and Sons Ltd., Melbourne, Sydney and Brisbane.*

plus the interest earnings. Of course an office can’t
get policies for nothing. It costs, roughlv, just as
much to rope in a policy which endures for onlv a
year as it does to lasso one which endures for' 50
years. But to charge a policy of this sort with, sav,
£B5 strikes this paper as evidence of either a vora-
cious appetite or a stern resolve to discourage the
early surrender of policies, no matter what change
the circumstances of the insurer may have under-
gone.
One moral of the story is: Beware of over-insur-
ance. Lots of people are under-insured ; and many
of them are under-insured because at some time or
other they have taken out a bigger policy than they
could comfortably manage, and have then let the
whole thing go. And another is: Beware of child
insurance. It may be all right where the insurer
is afraid of himself and doesn’t believe he would
keep up the payments if he weren’t obliged to. If
he isn’t a person of that sort, he will find it in
nearly every case preferable to put money in a
savings bank, from which he will get back his pre-
miums with interest added if the child dies. A
lump sum, paid in on the birth of a child, can often
be afforded, and is probably the best investment:
£lOO lodged when Phyllis is born is worth £206
when Phyllis is 21, even if the interest averages
only 3i p.c. And if Phyllis dies at 20, there is
£199 to comfort Phyllis’s sister, or her mother,
which is better than the return of the £lOO without
interest. Or if £5 a year is dropped into the
Savings Bank at 34 p.c. and £5 is added every
year, by the time Phyllis is 20 there will be £l4l
waiting for her; and if she died at 18, instead of
the bare refund of the £9O of premiums, there
would be £122 10s. for someone to draw—and pro-
bably more, for the interest is often over 3£ p.c.
As a matter of fact, infant insurance is insurance
only in the most limited degree. Unable to protect
themselves against under-standard risks, since there
is no medical examination, the offices merely consti-
tute themselves bankers of the premiums until the
child has reached 21 and has more or less proved
that it is a good life ; and it hangs on to the whole
of the interest earnings to pay itself for its trouble in
holding and using the money until then. If the
child survives till 21 the bargain is only a very
moderate one. If the child doesn’t survive till 21
it is a wretchedly bad bargain. If a man must have
insurance for his child, he had better take the policy
out on his own life, under a table which provides that
premiums stop on the death of the person who takes
out the policy.
* * * *
The North Coast (N.S.W.) Co-operative
Co., Australia’s biggest cow-country concern, made
the trifle of 4119 tons of butter last half-year, and
put through 21.714 pigs as a side-l ; ne. The average
price realised for the butter was 10.9 d. in the Syd-
ney and inter-State trade, and on the quantity
shipped to some place that the directors call “Home ”
the average drawn was 9.57 d. per lb. The foreign
(that is to say, Svdney and inter-State) market paid
the co. £319,375 for the cow-fat; the “Home”
market paid £87,518 ; and cured pig ran into
£61,108. There wasn’t much profit in it for the
co., whose chief object in life is to make things
good for its co-operative suppliers. After providing
£3OOO for depreciation and £lOOO for the marine
insurance fund, there was £5663 left over, and this
will be carried along to the end of the year, when it
wijl be cut up in dividend and bonus.
* * *
Ormis Metal Manufacturing Co., Ltd.
(Sydney), is to have a capital of £lOO,OOO in £1
shares, and issues an undated prospectus asking for
subscriptions for 30,000 shares. Vendors are to get
25,000 paid shares and £2500 cash for the patent
rights of the metal for the Commonwealth, and
45,000 shares are reserved to be issued at the will
of the Board. Ormis metal is said to be an
alloy of aluminium, tin, copper and lead, and has
excellent qualities; but the metal bears a strong
family resemblance to navaltum, an aluminium alloy
with Rothschild money behind it, an English factory
glutted with orders, and a threat to start a factory
in Australia. Ormis metal is an Australian inven-
tion, and it is to be hoped that Australia spoke
first. But it is a pity that the people who have it
in hand opened their mouths so wide when they
spoke to the public. The shares may pass ; but no
money should have been taken in view of the fact
that the metal hasn’t yet been produced on a com-
mercial scale.
Gold divs. amounting to £436,821 were
paid by Westralian cos. for the first half of this
year—£26,733 more than in the corresponding
half of 1912. To the end of June the aggregate
sums loosed by the principal producers werej —
Great Boulder, £4,022,425; Ivanhoe, £3,118,750;
Golden Horseshoe, £3,075,000; Oroya-Brown Hill,
£2,157,241; Great Fingall, £1,734,375; Lake
View, £1,503,000; Boulder Perseverance,
£1,461,116; Kalgurli, £1,276,500 ; Sons of Gwalia,
£916,112; Associated, £715,217; and Associated
Northern, £708,750.
* * * *
After paying for everything Lake View
and Star (W.A.) wound up its year on February 28
with a surplus of £34,398. This has been applied
in payment of dividends aggregating 16 p.c., divi-
dend duty, and directors’ percentages. Altogether
212,606 tons were treated during the year for
£252,890, equal to 235. 9d. per ton. Costs aver-
aged 19s. lOd. Ore reserves are estimated as fol-
lows:—Lake View leases, 107,050 tons, worth
30s. 4d. per ton; Hannan’s Star leases, 305,720 tons,
averaging 265. 9d. ; together, 417,770 tons, worth
275. 7d. With the exception of a block of
27,520 tons, no ore below 500 ft. in the Hannan s
Star has been included. Taking into account that
good values have been met with in the 600 ft., i 00tt.,
900 ft. and 1100 ft. levels of the Star, the reserves
have been estimated on a conservative basis.
Bulletin remarked a year or so ago that tor its
size the Lake View and Star was one of the best ot
the Kalgoorlie bunch, and it still thinks so.
Algernon Moreing told shareholders from
the chair at the Fingall (W.A.) meeting that the
co. had done very well out of the upper workings
during the past year, and that it was intended to
continue cleaning up around the old stopes as long
as ore could be found of high enough grade to show
a small profit. This stuff would be mixed with the
richer material from the new deep-level shoot, but
what proportion of the monthly tonnage it would
form the management was not yet able to say. tie
added that during the past 12 months the .co. had
had a look at a fair number of offered mining pro-
perties in the West and elsewhere, and at present
held a half interest in a small exploration syndicate
which was testing a promising sold show.

* * *
W estralian Mines Dept, is being ridi-
culed in the West for publishing information about
gold yields, developments, etc., months after the in-
tormation was ripe. Not so long ago a mine which
had been given up as hopeless was referred to in the
departmental budget as looking splendid, while an-
other, which had previously snuffed out, was described
as having good prospects.

A fair amount of hard cash in the form
of unclaimed dividends was held by a number of
Westralian gold-mining cos. at the date of their last
balance-sheets. Great Boulder had £11,306; Ivan-
hoe £2751, Great Fingall £2630, Sons of Gwalia
£2437, Kalgurli £1939, Oroya Links £913, Asso-
ciated Northern £BBl, Boulder Perseverance £546,
Golden Horseshoe £575, Lake View and Star £251,
South Kalgurli £197, and Hainault £34.
Lionel Robinson and party have aban-
doned their option over Rollo’s deep-lead claims at
Kanowna. The show is stated to be all right for a
small party, but not big enough for a co. to make
money out of. Robinson and co. still have the Gim-
let at Ora Banda and the Golden Valley leases at
Kanowna on their hands.
* * * *
Geologist Dr. Maclaren has just com-
pleted a. tour of the W.A. goldfields. It was on
Maclaren’s advice that Moreing and others sunk an
internal shaft from the No. 13 level of the Great
Fingall, and the result of this work upset for once,
at any rate, the theory that quartz reefs do not hold
their values to any great depth in the West. Lat-
terly Maclaren has been examining the Fenian and
Queen of the Hills at Meekatharra, the Sons of
Gwalia at Leonora, and the Ivanhoe at Kalgoorlie.
It is wholly and solely on the advice of this expert
that the Ivanhoe management is continuing the
dreary work of sinking through a poor zone in the
bottom of the mine, and it is also on his tip that
the prospecting of the western portion of the pro-
perty (including the old Ivanhoe Junction lease,
which he believes will carry high values in depth) is
to be undertaken.
Golden Ridge (W.A.) has made a start
to prove the worth or otherwise of its latest option
at Yerilla. Last year about £3OOO worth of gold
was got from one portion of the show and about
£5OOO in bullion from another part. The district
has always been noted for rich patches, but, as a
rule, they don’t extend very far or very deep. The
Ridge’s present property at Waterfall is gradually
passing away, but it may be some time yet before
its name appears among the obituary notices.
The accounts presented to Boulder No. 1
shareholders on June 30 showed a total credit of
£8873. On account of the abandonment of the St.
George mine at Magnet, this balance is not dealt
with, but is carried forward. The co.’s lease at
Kalgoorlie was worked softly by tributors during the
year, and is still in their hands. Out of the final
profits made from the St. George lease a slimes
dump was purchased at Long Reef, Lennonville; in-
cluding the removal of plant, the total outlay on
this spec, was about £4844. The treatment of this
dump was commenced in January last, and a fair
profit is showing. A “constant search” is being made
for new property, and with the plant now on hand
a small mine could, if found, be well equipped at a
low cost.
Edna May (W.A.) recently offered 5000
of its 7200 reserve shares for the adjoining Myrtle
Central lease, and the syndicate which owns the
latter has now expressed its willingness to sell if the
remaining 2200 shares are thrown in. The inclusion
of the Central in the Edna May holding would give
an additional 300 ft., or thereabouts, on the line of
reef, and make the property a more solid proposition
in every way.
The local paper, which generally knows
what’s what, states that the Marvel Loch (W.A.) will
cease operations at its present mine at the end of
this month, and give its whole attention to the
Comet option. Low returns, arsenical conditions
and a deluge of water are said to have influenced the
Board in coming to this decision. The Comet is
declared to be opening up well, and 500 tons of ore
raised from development are about to be put through
the mill. Strong' doubts as to the permanency of
the reef in depth are still held by experts.
* * * *
New Bendigo. St. Arnaud (Vic.), still
fancies it is a mine. Because last half-year >t got
an average of 7jdwt. from the reef at 360 ft., it
wants to work up sufficient enthusiasm to unwater
the north shaft to 450 ft. and climb through the
slimy drives to find out if the old miner left any-
thing worth having behind him. Remembering old
efforts to make the claim pay, “the future policy”
so much talked about should be to keep on the
7dwt. stone, and not waste cash in finding out where
such stone is not.
A little plan is in the air to fuse Dayles-
tords North Nuggetty on to the Central Ajax. But
it won t come off unless the cost to the Central is
mignty low. One yarn says the North goes in on
, are bas i s - This certainly would let out
the folk who bought heavily from 11s. to 215., and
also give the old Queen-street crowd some cash back,
out the Central isn’t in the bag yet. If anything is
done, the Central people should cast an eariy eye to
the question of filling at the North Nuggetty.
The old South German, Maldon (Vic.),
having opened out some 15dwt. dirt in its inter-
mediate level north under the prospector’s block, is
now' in the lists as a bit more than a humble pawn
in the mining game. With a record of 163,0890 z.
from 173,000 tons, and £341,000 paid in divs., any
speck of gold found was sure to bring in buyers. The
present flutter arises not from pluck on the part of
shareholders, but through a party of tributors grub-
bing under the old 1750 ft. level north and getting
Pke co ” having thus had the way shown,
diant want the miner to scoop more than could be
helped, so it shut off further tribute parties, and
itself put in an intermediate between the 1750 ft and
2000 ft. levels. As 2ft. of 15dwt. reef was met
earlier than the world reckoned, the whoop went up
that the northern continuation of the old South Ger-
man eastern shoot had been brought to light. But it
is a bit early to talk that way. The last ebullition
of the kind kicked prices to the 50s. mark, but
there was barely a dividend to warm the folk who
paid that price simply to nurse a very inert ancient
out of his second childhood.
At Wood’s Point (Vic.) All Nations is
still trying to pick up the old run of gold that in
its day made the claim as glorious as a poppy on
the mountain-side. Now the old story of the field
is being re-told. Poor stuff everywhere, and no
hint of an improvement. So bad have" things got
that only 310 tons went to the battery in the half-
year, and what it yielded must for shame be con-
cealed. There seems to be ground for asking the
board to apologise for not breaking away to other
more likely-looking spots on the dyke, this might
induce shareholders to stick to their scrip, whereas
they won’t hold on merely to be told at the end of a
half-year that “mine and machinery are in good
working order.”
The Mount Oxide (Q.) option to the
slim London crowd is off, after all. The present
Mount Oxide Co. is in 200,000 shares of £l, of which
30,000 are reserved against the £30,000 debenture issue
to the original owner, Ernest Henry. The London
people had the property under offer on a share-for-
share basis in a new co. (registered in London last
June) of £475.000. The Englishmen were to get
275.000 shares in return for putting up £150.000
working capital; but right through they have been
trying to squeeze out better terms. Their last
squeeze was in the direction of reducing the vendor
co.’s proportion of shares ; but the vendor sat tight,
finally declared the option off, and started negotia-
tions for a debenture issue for working capital. A
firm of engineers is prepared to tie the mine to
Burketown with a railroad payable in debentures,
and the Queensland Government is said to be ready
to guarantee the debentures. If so, the sooner a
move is made the better. Oxide is too big a mine
to be trifled with any longer.
Oxide Creek Syndicate, which has a show
three miles from Mount Oxide, and has been develop-
ing well, goes into a co. caoitalised at £125,000 in
ss. shares. A tunnel has been driven 150 ft. along
the outcrop, and a shaft sunk from surface in high-
grade carbonates has holed into ore at the tunnel
level. No. 2 vein has been driven on 70ft. north,
and 2ft. is worth over 10 p.c. A winze from this
level is down 50ft. in rich ore, and a crosscut at
the bottom shows lift, of first-rate stuff. The mine
will continue to shin ore as fast as it can be broken.
Mt. Molloy (Q.), which came a dreadful
cropper three or four years ago, is now negotiating
with the Queensland Government with regard to its
railway. After paying regular monthly divs. for
some time, the co. decided that, to prolong the divs.,
it would build a railway with the £40,000 or so
it had got to the credit of its reserve fund. As
usual, the work cost more than was estimated, and
the co. found itself a few thousand pounds in debt.
Simultaneously, the copper market slumped, and the
co., with little development work done, was in
trouble straight away. Practically the railway,
which connects with the line from Cairns, has thus
never been used. The debenture-holders, acting for
themselves and the co., suggest that the Government
should take over the line, on condition that half
the purchase money is put back into the mine; and
that condition, added to the fact that good timber
and some agricultural country would be served, as
well as a few small mines, is inclining the ear of the
Government to the proposal. Mount Molloy certainly
deserves more consideration than most mining cos.,
for, unlike the vast majority, it did build up a
decent cash reserve in the days of its prosperity.
* * * *
Since that day 27 years ago when T. S.
Hall called in on his friend Darcy, the Rockhampton
solicitor, and told him that a chap named Morgan
had brought some nice-looking stone into the bank
and wanted some money, Mount Morgan has piled
up sizeable figures. Between July 1, 1886, and
May 31 of this year, the co.’s total income has been
£19,141,620; and here are a few of the items the
money has gone into: —
Dividends •• •• •• £8,079,164
Wages and mine contracts .. 5,587,208
Coal and coke . . ■ • 1,441,609
Firewood and charcoal . . 615,742
Machinery and plant . . 866,565
Stores and timber .. •• 44 L060
Dividend and income taxes . . 308,577
Railway freight . . • • 565,036
Some of the payments have been extraordinarily
modest. Directors’ fees for 27 years come to only
£36 996. Also, by the way, donations haven’t ex-
ceeded £7740. So far the Many Peaks Co. has
drawn £30,896 as royalty on the flux it supplies
to the co. —a bonus mostly to past and present officers
of Mount Morgan. * t *
Mount Morgan is adopting a new system,
and now reports its output for 28 days, thus falling
into line with most of the big mines. The last
return is an improvement; value of copper and gold
produced is calculated at £84,868 from 23,696
tons of ore. The average pans out over 71s. per
ton, and, as copper is taken at £6O, it is a pretty
safe estimate. For the 28 days’ run 766 tons of
blister were produced, containing 758 tons ot fine
copper and 93840 z. gold. A ton of blister contained
on the average nearly £lll worth of metals, so
the enrichment due to gold contents was approxi-
mately £5l per ton. The new control is plainly
going' to shake the market up.
* * * *
Brilliant Deeps. Charters Towers, lias
been crushing lower-grade stone, and the first clean-
up in Jnlv was onlv £ll5O from 342 tons Still,
it seems a fairer thing to take the return for the
first six months of the year, which was £15,457 from
3656 tons; average, £4 4s. 6d. per ton. Like the
Brilliant Extended, the Deeps will find it difficult to
value reserves correctlv. The old enthusiastic idea
that the crushing stuff would average £7 per ton
was hopelessly wrong. Driving in No. 6 level dis-
closes in and out values, although some of the stone
passed through in the shaft, a little higher than the
level was of great value. Brilliant Extended made
a snug profit of £l5OO in June, but it is too optimis-
tic to reckon that as an average. #
Improving returns come from Vegetable
Greek (NSW.). Last three weekly returns of con-
centrates- 50cwt., 60cwt. and 73cwt. Three shafts are
producing washdirt, which varies tremendously in
thickness and value, and it takes a deal of hard
work to keep on the track of the chain of ancient
pot-holes. There are 4000 acres to be ransacked and
130,000 shares to be fed —or starved. Hitherto
they have only been starved..
Frank Connor, M.L.C., the Westralian
cattle man who has done a lot to push the raw-meat
trade in “the East,” is back from the Philippines
and talking “placer” mining. He isn’t exactly an
uninterested party ; it would say less for his opinion
of the prospects if he had remained a mere on-
looker. The Cansuran concession, taken over by a
co. (mostly ’Murikan), of which Connor is a direc-
tor, is going to show whether there is money in the
business, or whether the calculations are all wrong.
They have a big enough area for a ’possum ranch,
though only half a square mile has been tested. On
that lot they figure on lid. per cubic yard, and
with a few rivers and waterfalls on the premises,
they put costs at what looks at this distance the
very low figure of lid. per yard. Outlay on plant
for hydraulic mining, with a good fall of water
handy, will certainly be a mere trifle, so depreciation
will count for next to nothing. There are many
other known gold-bearing areas in this island of Min-
dinao, and, if the Cansuran does well, Connor reckons
that Australia should have a cut, being handy and
knowing a thing or two about gold-mining.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, August 4, 1913.
My Dear Abini, —
What the spring races will bring forth is
foreshadowed by various exhibitions of fancy
tnlls. It is going to be a season palpitating
with color. Linen and silk coats (quite
short and semi-swallow-tail, with a rounded
effect at the back, of gorgeous blue or parrot
red) will top-dress kilted skirts of silk-
mushn or diaphanous ninon. Black aero-
plane overalls —that’s what they look like
though they are heavily disguised in the
-c rench language with cerise or emerald-
green belts, cover fantasies in foamy white
or grey lace.
~^n e, enough to be pulled through a
bangle, is an exquisite extravagance
or the premature spring. The pneumonia
blouse ot a season or two ago was a stout
wind-proof garment compared with the un-
dress of the present-day gown of conse-
rrr^ nce ’ • cu t to a deep Y in front.
V\ here it isn’t cut a film of gauzy drapery
transparent as a butterfly’s wing, is drawn
across the sylph-like form into a waist belt
of the most gorgeous color the dyer’s vat
can supply. The 1913-14 modes are designed
for the suffering rich, who starve amidst
plenty, in order to have a figure that is
lean enough to blow through a keyhole or
thereabouts.
G.-G. Denman took a brisk hand in all
the Kosciusko games, both outdoor and of
the parlor variety. He proposed Prof.
Blunno as a member of the house carnival
committee, and the ayes had it; for the
viticultural expert’s popularity runs to
about twice the length of his longest
shadow on the snow. The small, genial
Italian is still holidaying on Kosciusko’s
frozen pate. The tall Barttelot lady is mak-
ing arrangements to transfer her babes
later on to the airy hilltop for a snack
of snow sports.
Adelaide sent many handsome contribu-
tions to the carnival. From that city of
spires came Miss Billiet and Miss Baird,
notably good-looking and clad in the new
ski-costume. This gear was made of navy
blue. One coat was faced with white, the
other with canary. Bloomers of navy made
the foundation of these three-piece gar-
ments. A very short skirt is worn for the
departure from the hostel. When the
ski-er gets her blood up, and skims through
the soft snow, where she may fall as lightly
as on a bed of rose leaves, she can button
up the skirt. This leaves her nether gar-
ments free to wrestle with the fluffy land-
scape. Putties complete the weather-proof
outfit.
Melbourne _ sent a very smart ski-
runner in Miss Yencken. The attractive
lodman girls and Miss Feeder made a
party from the Momma State; and H. D.
Scott and two sisters, Mrs. Cullen Ward
and Miss Everett contributed to the amuse-
ments. With them was baritone Harold
Mulligan and violinist J. Garrison, both
amateurs of some size. One of the domestic
staff at the hostel had the bad luck to in-
jure herself rather seriously at the outdoor
revels, and the good luck to be nursed back
to normal by Miss Everett—a trained
Nightingale, who stubbornly attacked the
injury with hot compresses for 48 hours.
The thoroughly-vaccinated Nordica co.
has crept over the border to Melbourne
armed with certificates to soothe the sen-
tinels at Wodonga. The semi-farewell con-
cert on Saturday night was starred by the
American soprano’s stately ascent into Han-
del s lofty “Let the Bright Seraphim,” with
trumpet obligato.
tvt time in her Sydney season,
Nordica on Saturday wore something ap-
proximate to sleeves on her gown. They
were lacy affairs that hid the dimples in
her elbows. Her arms are as round and
shapely as those of an infant Hercules who
has been fattened on Somebody’s food, and
photographed to excite the envy of every
mother in his suburb. She abjures gloves
on the concert platform. This act of grace
gives women who take number sevens every
chance to contemplate the tiny fingers that
slip into a five suede without the aid of a
forcing machine. For the rest, Nordica
on Saturday night was heavily embroidered
with diamonds and crusted with emeralds,
and clad in ropes of pearls with motifs of
white satin and lace.
Dufault is singing like a skylark this sea-
son. “Au Pays,” one of his contributions,
is a stirring little French song. Jean Pierre
returns from the war clothed in rags and
glory, to find that his faithless Jeannette
prefers a good roof over her head. This was
the French-Canadian’s great hit in the
middle of last week. It has the tremendous
value of a big climax —“Bataille ! Bataille!”
Franklin Holding’s vaccinated leg gave him
no trouble in handling his fiddle with his
usual skill.
It was more or less by chance that
Romayne Simmons, of this company, drifted
to the piano stool. Born in ’ London,
brought up in Berlin (his mother was Ger-
man, by the way), the youthful Simmons
took music lessons from Scharwenke. If
you bumped against a Continental piano, I
believe this composer’s Polish dance would
tall out, so popular is it still. Inadvertently
lie studied music in his knickerbocker days
—partly because lie was too young to de-
fend himself; chiefly because his mother,
who was very musical, was a great friend
ol Mrs. Scharwenke’s. Later he turned to
ruts and Porous Plaster, and studied the
Art of Healing first in Berlin, then in Phila-
delphia. There he took his diploma, and
is duly qualified to look after his own vac-
cinated calf. But medicine had ceased to
charm. Young Dr. Simmons decided that
was born to be an actor. Luck and
ability put him into the part of Zou Zou in
Inlby. ’ After playing this for 42 weeks
through the States, the versatile German-
Lnglishman began to weary of the foot-
lights. What next? He was young, and
the world was wide. The late William
Steinway, then head of the piano firm, was
his friend. Steinway suggested that he
would give Simmons a line to Nordica. She
was then studying Wagnerian rbles and
intended to take a concert co. on tour in the
States in the gaps between her grand-opera
trills. Simmons had been dipped in German
music of the Wagner school in his helpless
infancy. So an association was begun
which has continued for 16 years. Inci-
dentally Simmons, being a man of many
pilgrimages, has been vaccinated about 14
times. The Sydney punctures have “taken”
in the third degree.
Governor Strickland’s first idea of giving
a dinner —the typical British mark of joy—
to the coming English Parliamentarians, has
been abandoned in favor of a ball. This is
likely to develop into a reception, with some
dancing for the fleet of foot.
Lord Emmott and his lady will camp at
State Government House during their stay
in Sydney. They are old friends of the
Stricklands. The veteran Baron Sheffield,
whose first birthday occurred in 1839, is
with the party. His contribution to the lit-
erary fare of England is a heavy-weight
volume called “Our National Education.”
“The Seaside Camps” meeting last week
brings the women and children of the bush
a few steps nearer the blue Pacific. Pro-
bably a suitable plot of land will be leased
in the Narrabeen district. The suggestion
to build huts or erect tents and have the
camp carefully organised is still being
thrashed out. A sympathetic builder is
said to be carving out a practical notion
tor permanent humpies. These he may, if
he puts on rose-colored specs, see his way
to run up at little more than cost price.
Miss Alice Currie, who mothered the idea
is hon. organiser; Minister for Lands
V£ e . ’ wllo takes a keen interest in the
affair, is vice-president; and there is a
committee which bubbles over with enthu-
siasm. After the first outlay is over the Sea-
side Camp is expected to pay its way. Cooking
will be engineered on more or less co-opera-
tive lines. The resourceful Women of the
Bush are not expected to perform miracles
with loaves and fishes. But they’re likely
to make a quartern loaf and a few fins go
a mighty long way.
The results of Hospital Saturday collec-
tion were recently handed over after an
exhausting survey of the merits of each
claimant. The economy, efficiency and
other domestic virtues in the management
of each hospital are peered into by experts
who work out their problem with marks
Prof. Anderson Stuart held out a joyfui
hand for a fat helping for his pet hobby
Prince Alfred Hospital; and St. Vincent’s
and Sydney nurseries also got away with
cosy cheques. Altogether about 30 hospitals
benefited by the £9OOO collection. Miss
Strickland handed the cheques over; but
the ceremony, which occurred in the vesti-
bule of the Town Hall, had been so scantily
advertised that it was chiefly remarkable
for the large number of persons who were
not present. Governor Strickland was in
charge of the meeting.
“Rosna” tells me of Hospital Day in
London :
Whitehall from Trafalgar Square to Westminster
was given over to Miss Australia and Miss Maori-
onnnn 11 Alexandra Day—the day whereon they and
20,000 other Misses sold wild roses and pillag’ed
London of £40,000 for the hospitals. As usilal
Maonland, which revels in “loyalty,” was most to
the fore, and had its name and address blazoned on
the ribbons of its flower baskets. Also, as usual
modest Australia went unlabelled. The marauders
were abroad as early as 8 a.m., and as the French
President was making an official call on Britannia
that day, there was plenty of populace to be shorn
of its guineas. To vary the strain in Whitehall
under-secretaries and other frock-coated persons made
tea-parties and carried off the Misses Australasia to
the War Office or the Home Office, or one or the
other seats of mis-government, for refreshment pur-
poses. And one good Australian scribe, Marv Grant
Bruce, improved the shining hour by telling the
Daily Mail, in a charming article, something of the
sister event, Wattle Day, in Australia. *
The London “sympathiser” who offers to
“sjs* British widows to emigrate to
N.B W. need not rouse the ire of the most
confirmed spinster in these parts. Provided
the widow’s mite is a helping hand in our
struggle with the eternal saucepans, she is
welcome. The really domesticated widow
promises better than the innumerable Eng-
lish governess who wants to come out to
Australia at any price. The teaching trade
here is gorged with mediocrity. Yet Aus-
tralian lecturers on the other side, who
know our needs as well as Grace Watson,
are inadvertently encouraging the nursery
governess to view this continent as the
Promised Land. Last mail brought a letter
from an optimist of this kind who has been
goaded to turn her face this way. She had
been told (in England, of course) that
“educated women were badly needed in Aus-
tralia.” A week or two ago, an educated
woman applied for the billet of daily gover-
ness in a local girls’ school. She was
offered £3O a year, and told to bring her own
lunch! The widow who can cook well will
be rushed with anything from £1 a week
upwards and her keep.
The South Sydney Hospital is to be
officially opened by Governor Strickland
next Saturday afternoon, August 9. Thirsty
visitors will be placated with tea and sun-
dries.
A Sydneysider who escaped to Mel-
bourne after proving that she had been
punctured in the left arm, had a word with
Private Sec. Victor Hood the other day.
He has just stepped into harness again,
an( l—though this has no connection with
the preceding remark—he has been unsuc-
cessfully vaccinated three times.
The late William Lyne’s first wife had
been Miss Martha Coates Shaw. She was
of the old house of Sir Robert Shaw—
the eldest daughter of Edward Carr Shaw,
Baronet, of Bushy Park, near Dublin. The
Shaws and the Parleys were intermarried,
and Mrs. Lyne was a cousin of the late
Chief Justice of N. S. Wales. But the
modest, sensitive and retiring lady never
mentioned her “lofty connections.” “They
might think,” she remarked once, “that I
wanted something from them—and what is
there that I could want?”
The Girls’ Guild Bazaar was opened
twice last week and showed its wares for a
season of two consecutive days. On Thurs-
day afternoon the Cullen lady tore it asunder
with a few remarks concerning the object,
ihis has in view the purchase of a small
motor to transport the clothes, etc., col-
lected for the Jumble Depot to its home in
burry Hills. The honorary lady-in-charge
is Miss Sutton. This enthusiast has hither-
to driven an uncertain-tempered beast in a
rocky van to gather up the garments given
by the . Fortunate to be sold at slaughter
prices in the Depot. On the second day,
Tit heradge re-opened the bazaar
which had been shut for exactly one night.
The “Butterfly” lady was motored to the
scene of action by Lewis Waller. She
slipped out of a comprehensive fur wrap
and displayed a dazzling garb of orchid
mauve, with a fluff of fur round its extrava-
gant hem. This young person finds £2OOO
a year just enough to keep her tidy, so her
frills are worth looking at. Organising sec.
Grace Burrows shadowed her bazaar-open-
ers on both days. Madge splashed money
about with both hands and refused
change with the seraphic smile of a young
millionairess. Mrs. Ethel Turner Curlewis
and Dame Hay rendered first-aid in the sale
of pot plants. Mrs. George Taylor was in
charge of the Art department, which varied
from very good bad. Mrs. Moseley,
Mrs. Macarthur-Onslow and the busy
Massey and Tatham girls were other
workers.
The Royal Art Society’s rooms, where the
bazaar raged, is more or less air-tight, and
the tea-room warmed up to about 1000 de-
grees in the shade. Singer Emilie Lambert
lent a hand in this department. Mrs. Tom
Raine, in smoke grey, was another pillar of
the cause. On the first day, the Governor’s
elder daughter and a younger member of
the Lranbrook kindergarten wedged their
way through the crowd. The veteran A.D.C.
Campbell, who mostly squires these youth-
ful damsels on their walks abroad, filled his
top hat with the smaller baggage, till it
overflowed with a pair of infant’s shoes,
which he had recklessly bought at a lingerie
stall. Assistant officer-in-charge was Miss
Birch, the governess. She’s incapable of
“ Vl P£ up t 0 her suggestive name, and the
Strickland kindergarten drags her along in
its wake.

Ball is vigorously pushing the sale of

O’Connor girls are stirring up their friends

Fancy sets will plunge our Nicest People

A Livingstone Centenary Exhibition

One of the legal Pilcher family has gone
into the show business. J. D., of the clan
is running the “Punchinellos” at the Rener’
tory Theatre in Grosvenor-street.
The last will and testament of Lewis
Whitteld, learned barrister and cultured
gentleman, is a beautiful sample of brevity
—and other things as well. It is dated
February 1, 1892: “I hereby give to mv
dear wife, Eleanor, all my real and personal
estate, absolutely, and I appoint her sole
executrix of this my will.” And in 21
years’ experience the happy gentleman evi-
dently found no reason at all to make so
much as the smallest modification or add the
tiniest direction.
The Society of Women Painters will open
its wares to the public before this meets
your eye. Lawson House, opposite Petty’s
Hotel, is the abode this year.
Our hard-working Mayoress was presi-
dent of a committee which recently rounded
up many cheerful givers. The result was a
piano, a cheque, a decorative address and a
posy of flowers. These were handed over
to the musical press woman, May Summer-
belle, last week. The Mayoress’s official
den was borrowed for the occasion. Mrs.
C. G. Wade, Mrs. Spencer Brunton, Mrs
F. Booth, and Mrs. ’Varsity David were the
chief conspirators.
The queer mixture of theology and pagan-
ism which attacks the native mind when it
is first inoculated with Christianity is illus-
trated in some news from Thursday Island.
The John Douglas memorial chapei in that
steamy speck has just been finished. Accord-
ing to the gospel of a local corresDondent,
the. Torres Straits natives have given as
their to the building a window
representing the late Administrator as St.
John of I’atmos. The logic of this is quite
simple. The donors knew the appearance
of one John —they had no knowledge of the
saint’s features. So they have made a hand-
some compromise.
The latest style in hairdressing is fighting
its way to the front. It is very helpful to
the lady with large ears. Those orifices are
now completely buried under sleek locks of
the pattern that decorated the lobes of that
long-dead lady, Queen Victoria’s mother.
At some theatricals the other night
a pretty black-haired damsel wore the back
of her coiffure in a twisted knob like the
handle of a toy teapot. The rest of her
hair looked as if it had been gummed down
over her hearing machinery. A small dab
of raven tresses was also pasted down on
the outer edge of an ivory cheek. However,
if vou re more than 20, this antique mode
1S „ ®2 fer on a Earned portrait of the Duchess
of Kent.
My dear, put this to the credit of our
sex. now they are all talking vaccination,
that it was one of us who told Jenner that,
having had cow-pox, she wasn’t afraid of
small-pox. It put him on the scent; yet
the poor, nervous man was 17 years before
he had the pluck to try inoculation on a
boy. And for a while there ■was such a
row about it that I shouldn’t be surprised
if he wasn’t sorry he had ever tried at all.

Mayor Cocks, of this town, is not given to
putting on any superfluous frills because he
has the right to wear rabbit-skin drapery.
Hence one feels a throb of curiosity to
know why the.Wodonga doctor seemed so
intent on breaking up the big man’s usually

course, over vaccination —nearly everything
that goes wrong now is ascribed to that —
and the Mayor only got through the border

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 4, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Australian Steeplechase drew Society
to Caulfield through the cold showers and
biting wind of Saturday afternoon. The
two Governors went out with their hair
jammed firmly on their heads and stayed
right through. The Forrest dame, wearing
a tan hat, sailed gaily before the wind in
company with a oright-blue niece, who had
hard work to keep up. (To be strictly
accurate, she didn’t. Auntie led all the
way and won by about a yard.) Mrs. Jim
1 urves looked demure in brown. Mrs. Cox,
the Daylesford heiress, wore three red-and-
white bows in a cheeky little hat. This
headpiece went with fur coverings. Mrs.
Leslie Stuart showed a nose-tip and one
very bright eye between a huge squirrel coat-
collar, which was turned up, and a smart
felt hat, which was turned down. The little
v iGRch Comans daughter supported a bonnet
that deserved a sonnet and other furnishings
of less degree. Mrs. Joyce looked pale after
her vaccination ordeal, which was gone
through in Sydney away from her doctor-
husband. Mrs. Moir, whose husband was
partner to the late Samuel Gillott, came
along with sister Mrs. Taylor, the latter on
the way from England to Siam. Linda
Byron Moore dared orange and green in
her hat. Half the girls present sported the
latest veils, which have a large blotch like
a splash of mud just on the cheek.
G.-G. Denman has offered a trophy to the
female bushranger who makes the biggest
haul on Wattle Day. The prize to be begged
for consists of a topaz brooch with a sprig
of wattle cunningly engraved on it. There
are any number of bosoms already heaving
with pride in anticipation of wearing this
gaud. All the theatres are throwing open
their plush seats to the wattle plunderers ;
the cafes have promised to nourish them
with unlimited buns and tea; the trams will
give them free rides ; the motorists are lend-
ing their second-best cars; a big business
college is doing all the typing; the Toy
Scouts will run the messages ; and—the man
in the street will do the swearing and the
paying. The gentle Society blossoms should
have a perfectly lovely time—and all for
Charity, of course!
One of those hitches that convulse
Society and shake Toorak to its foundations
happened at the recent Skating Carnival in
aid of the Blind Institute. Someone forgot
to tell someone else not to forget to remind
someone to be on the look-out for the vice-
regal party; so when his Ex. got to the
front door he stood helplessly on the, mat,
supported by attendant Helps, waiting for
the first note of Gorsave. The dreary tune
was not forthcoming, so the poor gentleman
and his followers froze steadily in the
draught. At last the news sort of perco-
lated through, and the band got the word
that started the tune that set the Function-
ary going again. Later on, when the prizes
were to be presented, a mass of police in-
telligence tried to make room for the solemn
ceremony by sternly ordering his Ex. to
“Stand back there, please!” Still, things
might have been a lot worse if the Law
had encountered him on arrival at the door.
Mrs. Peter Mcßride and the just-budded
Miss Peter were recently presented at Court
on the usual charge of being prominent per-
sons with ample means of support. (Peter,
you will remember, is our Agent-General.)
Mrs. Mcßride, whose appearance is always
creditable to this country, was a symphony
in coral-color and sea blue, while daughter
blushed prettily in white.
Glorious GenOe was out of the bill a few
times last week, and disappointed audiences
said all sorts of things—she was muscle-
bound, had strained a tendon, had con-
tracted ’flu’, writer’s cramp, and housemaid’s
knee. It seems most likely that the great
little Dane had been vaccinated. A band-
age could be plainly seen on her left leg:
and, later, when performing “La Carmago,”
two suspicious spots glared out through
the fleshings, so noticeable that the
tights_ were changed for the dance in “Les
Sylphides.” The adorable Schmolz is also
dressing one leg with a puttee, although she
has not (so far) missed a performance.
Mrs. Henry Stead, daughter-in-law of
Stead, departed, lately asked her friend's to
meet Dorothea Spinney, the classic play-
i-eader, who owns a yearning face, tragic
brown eyes, and a very scarlet mouth
softly _ curved. When pressed for a
recitation, Miss Spinney sat on the
corner of the table —a pretty spec-
tacle in a droopy grey dress and very spruce
black furs—and told the company about her
artistic beginnings. She was born at Strat-
ford-on-Avon, and contracted the disease at
the graveyard where William lies buried.
When she was 13 years old, an inspired man
came to her school and recited “Richard II.”
with such effect that she then and there de-
cided to get into the harrowing business
herself. She says that Stratford -on-Avon
(please be careful to broaden the “A”) has
become appallingly Shakspearian. While
performing “Hamlet” there, Benson gazed
over-lone at Yorick’s skull. A small boy in the
audience, thinking he had forgotten the
lines, prompted, “Alas, poor Yorick !” After
the recitress had told such yarns (and
thereby dodged the recitation), she was re-
warded with tea. Hostess Mrs. Stead (whose
blue dress contrasted nicely with her big,
round, brown eyes) was assisted by Mrs.
Gregan McMahon, who wore a brown dress
and blue eyes. The show, by the way, was
given in the Repertory Theatre Club rooms.
Numbers of men came in—a most unusual
thing for a mere tea-party. Statistician
ivnibbs attended and humbly handed cake,
and Morris Phillips wore the proper after-
noon-tea look. Gregan McMahon fussed
amiably. The hostess’s husband came late,
wearing a busy air, a disarming smile and
an immaculate top-hat. (The old fashion
ol creasing the belltopper by sitting on it
has gone out.) Mrs. Fred Marsh pondered
under very blue feathers how she could reach
New Guinea and yet avoid the quarantine
area. (If she can’t solve the problem under
blue feathers, she might try brown. Brown
is a good color to think in, and a
sixpenny feather-duster would serve.) Miss
Michaelis, in a purple outfit, had tea and
talked awhile to Dorothea, then dashed off
to a Bush Nursing meeting. Mrs. Vincent,
an Englishwoman who is accompanying her
electrical-engineer husband to Brisbane,
mourned for new-made Victorian friends
about to be left behind, until a patriotic
Bananalander became lyric on the glory and
hospitality of her State. Miss Spinney re-
marked that she loved being in “bright, sun-
shiny Melbourne.” Whereat the local
women hitched their furs closer about them
and remarked impatiently that she had
struck the city at its very dreariest time.
People here get annoyed when sunshine is
mentioned. It is like talking finance to a
family embittered by absolute ruin.
May Vale Gilfillan endured an invasion of
her studio as soon as the door was opened
last I riday morning. She has heaps of new
enamels on show, as well as some fresh pic-
ture-enamels of size, each of which took
months to finish. The centrepiece of the ex-
hibition is the moonlit bush cottage that the
Gallery people are stewing over. (The Fel-
top spenders go very slowly with the cash
when the item in view is a mere Australian
product.) There are some native orchids in
enamel that are worth gloating over, and a
Ilurstbridge wattle that smells of spring.
Baron von Camerer, a fine old man who
unhooked the German title from his name
and put “Mr.” in its place, died the other
day. I his naturalised Australian, who dug
his pen with great energy and enthusiasm
into Australian economics, dwelt for years
on the shores of Cope Cope, an unboomed
Victorian lake which awaits the tourist in
the St. Arnaud district. A daughter is
married to Mr. Huber, a young man who
fills a post at Federal Parliament House.
Mrs. Lesley Murdoch, a Tasmanian girl,
while wandering abroad with her husband,
visited the great exhibition at Amsterdam!
tehe says Australia was well represented by
products and photographs, but the only
printed thing that wasn’t in English was the
signboard. That is the spacious sort of mis-
take that takes the profit out of advertising.
Getting a vaccination passport is a long
undertaking for the would-be traveller whose
arm refuses to “take.” Henry Harper has
been waiting for weeks to get away to
Queensland on the firm’s business. The
first injections were declared futile after 10
or 12 days. The second likewise drew a
blank Now he is waiting for the climax of
Hie third. If his FU’m doesn’t surprise him
by erupting he will get a certificate of
immunity,” but if it does erupt he will
wait a while to see how much it matters.
“Matters” seems the right word in such a
case.
Guide Carlisle, of Mount Buffalo, is dead.
He it was who built the beginnings of the
present Hospice, 18 long years before the
road was made. He carted all the timber
and stones to the bleak heights on plodding
psckhorses. That was nearly 30 years ago,
and he had to wait a long, long time for the
place to become a tourist resort. But, after
all, a man can’t do much better than pin
his faith to a good mountain.
Mrs. Harvey Patterson (she was an ex-
pert with the foils previous to her marriage)
is departing to Maoriland with her husband.
They have bought a place 20 miles out of
Christchurch on the beautiful Canterbury
Plains, and will have a stable for race-
horses close to the home. The departure
has made a hole in a fashionable city flat-
tery. They managed to occupy 14 "rooms
with the help of their servants; and if 14
vacant rooms aren’t a blank, I don’t know
what is.
The W. J. T. Clarke family are off to
England in two detachments. The head of
the house will pilot the elder girls through
America, whilst Mamma personally con-
ducts the younger ones “Homeward” along
the Suez track. The Clarke matron is
always known, amongst the Most Intimate
People, as “Queen Mary” ; and I have not
heard of any edict prohibiting the familiar-
ity. There is a certain facial resemblance
to George’s consort, which is greatly assisted
by a careful similarity of chiffons.
The George Chirnside matron, having
aired her South Yarra house sufficiently, is
once more looking out the mummy-wraps
for the furniture. In October, or there-
abouts, she will take daughter Audrey back
to England.
Mrs. Dudley Trenchard’s Glaciarium acci-
dent, whereby she broke her leg, has not
altogether crushed the spirit of gaiety in
the “Reuter” Collins family circle to which
she belongs. They have been cheered up by
the news that soldier-son Percy, the family’s
baby boy, is coming home to spend his fur-
lough.
The Children’s Hospital is unfolding a
new wing for the shelter of sick babies who
need surgical attention. Society has been
invited to attend this week-end and watch
G.-G. Denman turn the key. The new ward
has been built with an Edward Wilson
bequest.
The Co-operative Craft Workers’ Depot is
to open this week, in the Centreway. The
originators of the scheme have been "hard at
work for some time, and reckon they have
evolved an organisation which will keep the
thing going. Certainly, Melbourne ought to
have enough people prepared to pay respect-
able prices for high-class art and craftman-
ship.
People in trade know all about it by this,
but the fashionable friends of the lady have
not heard the story. The lady is the wife
of a Melbourne luminary with whom eco-
nomy is a passion ; but in spite of that she
has a reputation for indulgence in tasteful
and simple but expensive hats. The repu-
tation has been gained easily and cheaply.
Mrs. Blank buys a hat of her own designing
for two guineas from the smart milliner, on
condition that the tradeswoman will leave
the modest tile in her window for a few
days marked “£l5 155.” or thereabout.
The ministering angels who float round
in caps and cloaks have decided to possess
an institution of their very own. It is to
be called the Trained Nurses’ Club, and, be-
sides being residential, will be a central
bureau for nursing members who are waiting
for “cases.” There are 14 “Nurses’ Homes”
in Melbourne; but they are run on plain
boarding-house lines by dear old Private En-
terprise, and the ministering angels mostly
complain that their financial wings are
clipped too close. Miss Conyers, of Kew, as
the secretary of the new institution, is eager
to find and welcome supporters.
The yachtsmen danced in the St. Kilda
Town Hall one night last week. Some of
the accessories of the unstable sea were
scattered round just to give the function a
distinctive look. A tiller (or it may have
been a binnacle) sat on the dais, and when
the girls weren’t dancing, the dashing fel-
lows told them how, when the wind blows
harder than usual, they have to pour whisky
on the troubled mariners.
A young Englishman and a daughter of
one of our “best” families are a trifle down-
hearted, because the damsel’s mamma in-
sists that the course of true love shall be
obstructed, what time she takes the girl to
England and makes Enquiries.
Mrs. Rubber Edwards and her husband
have bounced across the United States.
When last heard of they were located in
New York, in a 19-storey hut which crouches
at the foot of a 53-storey giant. Anyone
who was thrown from the top of it would
probably bounce right into the Atlantic and
be lost for ever to Society.
There are many roads to success. Old
Tom Brassey, distributing prizes at an Eng-
lish school lately, told how his son-in-law,
Lord Willingdon (who was Freeman-Thomas
in Melbourne), owed much of his success
in life to his proficiency in cricket, lawn
tennis and other games. As a Lord-in-wait-
ing, Freeman-Thomas was a favorite partner
with the King in lawn tennis—and to-day
he is Governor of Bombay. He is a lord
still, but he doesn’t wait.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
A benefit race meeting was given to Mr. Hughes,
ex-handicapper, at Victoria Park on Saturday. See-
ing that the beneficiary has been working for the
racing public for 36 years, the attendance might
have been better. Still, it is hoped that the com-
plimentary cheque will be worth about £7OO. The
Governor turned up, and there was an aide or two
to preserve him from destruction. Among those on
the lawn were the Crozier clan, Mrs. Allan McFar-
lane and Mrs. Allan Bowman, Mrs. Stanley Murray
and her sister, Mrs. Herbert Rymill, Mrs. Ralli,
Mrs. Dudley Turner and Miss Turner, Mrs. Julian
Ayers, Miss Madge and Miss Jean Stirling, Mrs. Evan
Kyflin Thomas and Mrs. R. E. H. Hope. The out-
ward garb of all these folks consisted of coats, skirts,
furs and spiked hats, garments concerning which it
is difficult, if not impossible, to grow really lyric.
Mrs. Arthur Rymill gave a dance on Thursday
evening at the house of her mother, Mrs. William
Campbell, and on Wednesday a committee did as
nearly likewise as possible at the North Adelaide
Institute. Both were complete successes. Anyhow,
no protests have been lodged to date. On the same
festive evening, in the Masonic Hall nearby, the girls
of a fashionable suburban school were having their
dance —so you see Tynte-street was about as giddy
as it has been for months. By the way, the more
or less immemorial custom at the North Adelaide
Institute of “sitting out” dances in motor-cars has
been fatally bounded upon by the large, unromantic
hoof of authority. It appears that motor-cars may
not remain unattended in a public thoroughfare for
more than five hours. What is five hours when a
nice young man and an equally nice young woman
are sharing a dark, comfortable motor-car? And
what is the good of a car in these circumstances,
when an officious chauffeur is hanging around? How-
ever, chauffeur there must be; for at a recent
smart dance in the Institute, a policeman or some-
thing else equally horrible took the names and ad-
dresses of all occupants of street-anchored cars, with
a view to “proceedings.” We are all agog to know
the identity of the criminals. Meanwhile, the abat-
toirs muddle remains as involved as ever, and various
other civic anomalies and disgraces are receiving
their customary lack of attention.

There’ll Be Many Keen
Contests at Victoria Park
Next Saturday.
This favorite Course is noted for its keenly-contested
racing, and next Saturday’s meeting promises to be pro-
ductive of some really first-class sport.
The discipline is strict, the racing clean and good.
Entries have been received for each event. An after-
noon of real pleasure is assured to all.
Zetland Trams run at frequent intervals from Central
Railway Station and the Quay, direct to the Course.
Vice-Regal Band will give selections during the after-
noon. First race, 1.30.
Programmes, etc., from
The Secretary,
VICTORIA PARK RACING CLUB,
21 Imperial Arcade,
PITT STREET SYDNEY.
