SUPERB FLAVOR.
PERFECT PURITY.
POLITICAL
POINTS
More cheerful results of the Sydney con-
gestion policy. The Northern railway people
were informed last week that, owing to the
usual “serious block” outside the bloated
capital, no Queensland produce would be
received at Wallangarra (the border sta-
tion) for four days—July 24 to 28.
THE FOLICY PARTLY REVEAL ED.
He was not quite sure whether, after the deluge of
legislation during the past three years—they had
passed 83 Bills, he believed—it would not be well
to have a period of rest. — Jokook, wistfully.
THE WATCHER : “Hi! When are yer goin' t'
set yer sails an' get a move on, Mister?”
THE GREAT NAVIGATOR : “All the sail’s set
that's goin ’ to be set. An’ what's more,
this voyage is bein' undertaken under me
own system of navigation We iust take
advantage of winds an' tides as Providence
sends 'em! ”
Bruce Smith threatens to go down in his-
tory as “The Great Decliner.” At various
times he has declined almost every honor
that could be laid upon him. He has twice
declined to be Speaker of the House of Reps,
—once the other day and once four years
ago. He declined a judgeship from Hairy
Parkes; he turned down an offer of the
Agent-Generalship ; and he rejected the offer
of a title. And his reason for these whole-
sale refusals was that he might be free to
fight on the floor of the House for the people
and the country. It is magnificent. It is
sublime. The only fly in the ointment is
that, after making so many sacrifices to be
free on the floor of the House, Bruce Smith
is so seldom there.
Once more Private Enterprise has inti-
mated that it doesn’t think much of any
sort of Private Enterprise except its own.
Hitherto the big Mount Morgan co. has de-
pended on outside Private Enterprise for its
coal, coke, engineering, timber, and other
supplies. Now it has decided that that is
an old-fashioned and frayed-at-the-edges
idea, and it is going to adopt the Socialistic
plan of being its own coal-miner and brick-
maker, and coke manufacturer, and lime-
burner, and engineer and timber miller.
The sanguine folk who built on a strong
Maoriland Labor party emerging from the
“Unity” conference, engineered by the “Red”
Federation, have spent the last few days
interring their hopes. The narrow majority
(14) by which the “Red” preamble was
rejected was a poor augury for a practical,
commonsense programme, and all chance of
such disappeared when, a few days later,
the spirit of the preamble was torn to
shreds—if spirits can be shredded. Despite
the cheers called for Unity by Mills, of Mil-
waukee, the railway delegates drew out,
followed by Fowlds, McLaren, and lesser
fry. Thereafter all amendments were
brushed aside, and with the stock phrases,
“class war,” “military tyranny” etc., the
“Red” platform went through. But the
Massey Government still survives; in fact,
the only damage so far has fallen on the
original trades and labor party, and pre-
parations for repairs are already in train.
Mills counts nothing.
The chief change brought by the new Par-
liamentary session in South Aus. is the
retirement of John Verran from the leader-
ship of the Opposition in favor of Crawford
Vaughan. Opponents tried to make capital
out of supposed dissensions in the Labor
ranks, but the transfer was conducted amic-
ably enough at the last. It is probably for
the good of the party. There is no obvious
reason at all why Labor should “wowse,”
but under Verran it was always liable to do
so at any moment, and a big section of the
party has been driven nearly frantic by
being dragged into partnership with the
anti-everything-cheerful fanatics. More-
over, Verran showed himself weak when
strikers took possession of Adelaide traffic
two years ago. Nothing but his bluff man-
ner and sterling honesty ever got him the
leadership, for which he was eminently un-
suited. Vaughan is a different type alto-
gether. Son of the chief Government photo,
lithographer, he has been well educated, and
has a sense of the fitness of things.
Whether he can lead remains to be seen;
but he is shrewd and cautious, has a fierv
eloquence rather of the Welsh type, and
has had experience of office as State Treas-
urer. Small-built, clean-shaved, and 40, he
has a clever wife, and a brother who is likely
to be the next S.A. Attorney-General.
“We are not interfering
with the maternity bonus,”
said Mr. Cook. “What we
propose to do is to abolish
the bangle bonus. What I
mean is tliat rich women
are not to get the money.”
What Mr. Cook really
means is that the woman
who isn’t rich is only to get
the money on condition that
she admits poverty, and in-
terviews a set of sour
officials, pulling a poor
mouth, and exulaining that
in having a child she has
gone beyond her means, and
must go on the parish to
the extent of £5. In which
circumstances, how many
Australian women will ap-
ply? What Mr. Cook means
is that he is going to knock
the initial letter out of the bonus, and leave
only the onus.
Someone asserts that P.M.G. Wynne’s
horse-racing colors have been introduced in
the new “loyal” Commonwealth stamp, from
which the purely Australian kangaroo has
been ejected like a non-paying lodger. The
Cook Stakes is not likely to be a long-dis-
tance event, and the P.M.G., in his des-
perate sprint, is evidently determined to
make every post a Wynning post.
The heart of Cofif’s Harbor was made
glad lately by the tabling of a report from
the N.S.W. Public Works Committee re-
commending harbor works there at a cost
of £190,000. Charles Oliveu as chairman
of the Decentralisation Commission, gave
Coif’s the glad eye; and Johnson, of the
Railways, is among its friends. Naturally
it isn’t to be compared with Port Stephens;
but Port Stephens is too far south to serve
the whole of the North Coast, and, anyway,
there is room and more for two good port's
between Sydney and Brisbane. But before
Coif’s comes into its full inheritance there
will have to be a line from Inverell to
Guyra, connecting thence with the line at
Dorrigo on to Glenreagh and the coast.
With that big work done trade will flow
down from back as far as Mungindi, and
then the man who now can’t get his wheat
shifted because the railways are busy haul-
ing other men’s goods over vast, unneces-
sary distances will take a few steps further
from the Bankruptcy Court.
McLeod, the elect of poor old Daylesford
in Vic. Assembly, figures now as the “double-
dyed traitor” of the House. He betrayed
the Liberal cause by scheming a Tory-Labor
combination to push the Watt Ministry out
of omce, and then he betrayed the Labor
party by backing down from his position
and crawling to his Tory corner again. Even
long-suffering Daylesford must have had just
about enough of McLeod. Last week he
decided to retire peaceably from his seat in
the local borough council on the ground that
his Parliamentary duties kept him too busy.
After next election those duties will cease to
keep him warm if a reasonably strong can-
didate runs against him.
If the just-concluded Westralian Labor
Congress has its way, much good blood will
be shut out of the Commonwealth and many
a strong shooting-arm lost. Taking a very
narrow view of things, it passed a resolu-
tion to the effect that non-English speakers
should not be employed in any industry.
There are reasons why the Dago miner who
can’t warn his English mates of a misfire
should be shut out of Australian mines, but
there is no reason why the white foreigner
who has not yet learned English should be
shut out of all Australian callings. The
Commonwealth could very well do with a
million such people as the German farmers
who have settled and thrived and become
first-rate colonists in South Australia. If
the Jap. comes, armed and angry, 200,000
strong, incapable of speaking English, but
quite able to shoot in his own language, this
country may be glad to have on its side any
strong, resolute and really white man who
can shoot in any white man’s language —
German, Danish, French, Swedish or even
broad Scotch. Quite a lot of good shooting
has been done in very unintelligible Scotch.
ACID AND ICE.
Richard Jebb’s delicate tribute to the
“Party Government’’ fetich, in his book,
“The Britannic Question” :
In Britain the whole political system depends now-
aday on the Conservative and Liberal machines. Its
life-blood is the secret party funds, which are care-
fully guarded against any attempt to enact pu -
U u l Swn‘„lSg«, l w2Tb P ,S
the scenes, contribute not only largely but regularly
to one or the other war-chest, and the withdrawal of
whose support would cripple the operations of the
party machine.... “Who pays the piper calls the
tune” is an axiom which, in proportion as party
organisation becomes more expensive, must have an
increasing application to the determination of party
politics.
In fact, Britannia s wheel is nowadays a
surreptitious sovereign.
The old gag concerning the “pap-fed” in-
dustry of growing sugar with white labor
will have to be given a rest at last; for the
“white” bounty came to an end in July,
As a matter of fact, there never was a
bounty in the ordinary sense. What hap-
pened was that the sugar-producer paid an
excise of 4s. per cwt.—a charge which would
have sent the wheat-farmer raving mad if
it had been applied to flour as well as sugar
—and the “bounty” was merely a remission
of the duty. The sugar-grower now knows
better where he is: there is no excise and
no “bounty,” and the only help he gets is
by way of a £6 per ton protection against
the grower who hires Javanese and other
nigger labor at 6d. a day and thereabouts.
An elevating incident of last week’s crisis
debate in the Vic. Assembly was the speech of
—haw !—Norman Ba.vles, member for Toorak,
denving his alleged desire to get into
a Ministry— any Ministry. Bayles railed at
Watt from a lofty social standooint. and
put on “dog,” regardless of offence. Gazing
haughtily at his political chief he said: “I
ask the Premier not to bring his usual North
Melbourne manners with him,” and then
went on to tell him: “You have to live up to
the standards of gentlemen, whose standards
are very much higher than yours. You have
to follow in the footsteps of men who be-
haved with a sense of the dignity of their
positions.” The real trouble with Watt,
so far as Norman Bayles is concerned,
seems to be that he can’t live up to a sense
of the dignity of Norman Bayles’s position
as member for Toorak.
THE CLEAN SHEET
With the scrutiny of the Beallarat Federal rolls
concluded, It is stated that the scrutiny has disclosed
nothing serious.
The same results are being obtained in other elec-
torates.
THE LIB. : “ Disgraceful, I call it! Why, the
infernal scoundrels are innocent, mum!”
When Premier Watt lately returned to
Melbourne from England, he said that “he
had been imbued with a new spirit of appre-
ciation of the Empire.” When he was in
London he said that he was struck by that
city’s “appalling and ghastly slums.” Evi-
dently there are two sides to Watt’s new
spirit of appreciation—a side that swells
with pride, and a side that shudders with
horror.
The other day the Argus set out to ex-
plain what a beautiful thing progress was,
and instanced Hamilton (Vic.), in the dis-
trict haunted by the big Western squatters.
Well, here is Hamilton’s record :
1898. 1913.
Population . . .. . . 3650 4900
Annual value of property .. £23,307 £33,507
Heaven save us from much of such furious
progress as that!
N. S. Wales Liberals are running a set
of doubles for the Sydney suburban seats of
Glebe and Enmore at the coming State elec-
tion. Dr. Otto Bohrsmann has been selected
to contest the former; and his brother, Dr.
Rudolph Bohrsmann, will carry the flag in
the latter. The Glebe seat was a squattage
for ex-Minister Jimmy Hogue until 1910,
when he was flooded out by a storm of en-
lightenment. Enmore is a brand new elec-
torate.
When South Aus. turned down the first
Referenda, in 1911, Peake, the then leader
of the Opposition, thought that Verran and
his Ministry should at once resign. He
said that the majority of Noes showed a
want of confidence in the Government, and
no self-respecting Ministry could remain in
office in the face of the reverse. Now Peake
sits in the place where Verran sat, the
Referenda proposals have been carried in
S.A., and the Labor party gently reminds
Peake of his 1911 speech. But Peake
simply smiles —and that smile illustrates
once more the hollowness of these magnifi-
cent party diatribes.
Another slander by the “Liberal” leaders
on those Australians who are opposed to
them politically has been blighted. After
the Federal elections there was a howl from
Macedonia, or Toryville, or somewhere, that
Labor had won seats by means of double
voting, and the Electoral Department was
ordered to moil at high pressure in order to
uncover the disgraceful doings of the —ah —
Labah mob. To date, the officers have
checked the rolls of 44 electorates out of 75
and ascertained that the average number of
double votes polled was about three per
1000. Allowing a reasonable margin for
mistakes by electors and poll clerks, the num-
ber of actual double votes was practically
nil; far too few, at any rate, to justify the
charges of corruption, to say nothing .of de-
composition and putrefaction. So far, no
apology has been forthcoming—naturally.
Having been informed by Joe Cook that
the navy’s “River” vessels were a species of
dinghy used in the navigation of creeks (on
the same principle the Shire liners are ves-
sels that trade between Hampshire and
Devonshire), _ Australia has now begun to
get valuable information from the same en-
lightened party about natural phenomena.
During his tour with Irvine, the Prime Min-
ister. according to the Argus, had this to
say at Wedderburn :
Mr. McDonald, the late Speaker, had employed
an unhappy metaphor in likening the work of the
Labor party to an earthquake. Perhaps Mr. McDon-
ald did not know how an earthquake did work.
First of all, it poured over the whole face of the
country streams of molten lava and buried every-
thing before it. It buried men and homes and insti-
tutions under its devastating flow. (Cheers.)
Next time Cook speaks he might tell us
what a volcano is. As likely as not he will
assure us that it is a narrow neck of water
entirely surrounded by sea-serpents.
UNIFORM DIVERSITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Cook Government has promised a Scotch mix-
ture in Melbourne that the Scottish kilt shall be re-
stored at the earliest opportunity as a uniform for
Australian soldiers.
In other lands are sons content
To be of their peculiar race;
But on this hapless continent
It seems that may not be the case.
Here any dashed old thing we’ll be
But what we are, if we can brook
The maddening perversity
Of Satan, Irvine and Jo Cook—
The trio bright
Who never fight
For poor Australia, wrong or right.
Since Sandy is to have his kilt,
His philabeg and other gear,
And march to where the blood is spilt
With bagpipes wailing in his ear,
Why shouldn’t Pat to battle go
Dressed like the son of Erin’s Isle
Staged by the elder Boucicault,
And Cockneys garbed in coster style
Lay down their lives
For hearths and wives
When foemen threaten us with gyves?
The Austral army marches past,
A motley throng in batches queer;
A clump of Scotties in the van,
A clutch of Hebrews in the rear,
And in between a thousand breeds —
Welshmen, Dagoes, Dutchmen, Chows,
Russians, Germans, French and Swedes,
In kilt, or petticoat, or blouse.
But we’ll be shot
If we can spot
A darned Australian in the lot!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The Bulletin was alone in recommend-
ing Ballista for the Australian Hurdle Race at
Caulfield, whilst suggesting that his latest previous
running was not conclusive proof of his unripeness.
Melbourne papers, without exception, ignored the
nag even as a place investment. A week earlier
the son of Carbinier and Phyl had run like one
short of work. Julian rushed past him at the end
of a two-miles hurdle at Moonee Valley, and his
chance of staying to the end of a 3i-miles go didn’t
look bright. Moreover, the stable kept Wimmera
in the race, and preferred him to Bailista on the
day, judging from market prices and touts’ talk.
Yet Ballista, starting at 10’s and 12’s to one in a
field of 11 (one of whom was a despised outsider),
won the race in record time. Wimmera made the
record pace by trying to win all the way. Until
their heads were turned for home, he looked like
staying in front, despite his burden of list. 31b.
Then Clontaft, with only 9st. 31b. to struggle under,
collared him; after that, Ballista, 9st. 71b., came
like a racehorse and won brilliantly. Wingarara,
the nearest and most dangerous of Wimmera’s pur-
suers half a mile from home, fell at the second-
last hurdle —the only member of the crowd to come
to grief. Wingarara was handicapped with a blind
eye, and it found him out when the pinch came.
The loss of half his eyesight had made him uncer-
tain in his jumping.
Ballista was expected to win the Caulfield event
last year, but he failed pretty badly through being
rushed to the front along with his impetuous mate
Wimmera, who finished second, after spoiling both
their chances.
For the Australian Steeplechase, due next
Saturday, a good many old battlers are possibilities
at time of writing. The writer takes Bullawarra,
Leah Itleshna, United Kingdom (apparently quite
recovered from his fall at Flemington), Scrutineer,
Ballista, Clontaft, Emiroo and Statecraft as the
most dangerous. Bullawarra and Ballista look par-
ticularly good chances. This vote is for Bullawarra,
the handsome son of Sir Simon, with 12st. 111 b.
against his honored name. It seems a “crushing”
weight to be carried in a four-mile steeplechase,
but some previous winners have been even more
“crushed.” Grafnax got home with 12st. 121 b. up.
Colonel Shillinski carried 13st. 21b. : Domino managed
to hump 13st. 51b. to victory; Daimio had 13st.
31b., and Redleap won easily with 13st. 121 b. up.
The writer reckons that none of these bygone cham-
pions, bar Redleap, had so much pace and strength
as Bullawarra.
In the Sydney Lacrosse competition
Eastern Suburbs got through another Saturday with-
out a defeat, and is still on top with 25 points to
Sydney’s 19 and the 15 of Petersham and Birch-
grove. Mosman has a modest dozen, while Bur-
wood, University and Manly are eight, four and four
respectively. The cup of joy was filled to the brim
for Eastern Suburbs by the lucky defeat of Peter-
sham, which has held the premiership so often.
But it was a close call. At half-time East led by
three goals to two; but, shortly after, Petersham
evened things, and later on it was four all, and
both sides going strong. Then East Sydney got a
burst of splendid luck and scored three goals in
quick succession. The dice of the gods were thrown,
and East won by seven goals to four. Mosman and
Sydney played themselves right out before time was
up, but Sydney had established a lead of two
goals, and it maintained its advantage to the end,
winning by five goals to three. In the first round
Birchgrove had stamped University flat by seven
goals to one; but when they met on Saturday the
Students made so stubborn a stand that at half-time
the score was one all. Early in the second half
Birchgrove let loose two splendidly maintained at-
tacks, and, bursting through, scored twice. Not
till just before time did Uni. manage to wipe one
off the deficit, so it went under with two to Birch-
grove’s three. Manly, which had hitherto scored
only one victory this season, smashed up Burwood
by four goals to two. How it happened no man
can tell —least of all any man who resides at
Manly.
Small-pox having scared off the Vic. and
South Aus. baseballers, the bottom fell out of the pro-
nosed inter-State carnival, and followers of the game
had to fall back on the second round of Sydney com-
petition on Saturday. Lane Cove and Mosman went for
Sydney, and looked like eating it up, but the Syd-
ney brethren in the fifth innings hit up a tally of
eight runs, and so won through by 10 to nine amidst
great excitement and bitter disappointment. Peter-
sham went home exhausted after doing Waverley by
five to four, while Burwood surprised and defeated
Leichhardt, nine runs to eight. University was out
for blood, and jumped on Paddington by 14 to two.
The semi-finals start next Saturday.
Saturday saw the start of the three com-
petitions for the Zenith Cun, the outward and visible
sign of supremacy among the auto-cyclists of N.S.W.
There were 51 entrants, of whom 45 faced the pistol
and started out for a run from Strathfield. through
Parramatta, Hornsby Gully, Bobbin Head. Turra-
murra and P.vmble, down to North Sydney Post
Office. The scenery along the run is beautiful, and
for the most part the roads were good; but there
were stretches north of Turramurra where a lot of
nice new clay had been laid. The motor-bikists
then knew exactly how a flv feels when he falls into
the honey-jar. As the handicaps were not declared
before the race it will be some days before the re-
sults are worked out. The second competition will
take place on September 16, and will be over the
Blue Mountains to Hartley, where the shale used to
come from. The final will eventuate a fortnight
later over a course that has yet to be chosen.
The eldest son of Lo’- Tennvson. who
got his early ideas of cricket in Australia when his
old man was vice-regalling at Adelaide, is now a
boss bat of the Hampshire County team.
The newly-formed Billiards Association in
Adelaide has begun work with an amateur champion-
ship. It provided some surprises, and proved that
players, like horses, run in all shapes. The various
club experts were knocked out, and the final was left
to Demodena, a fragile-looking little mandolin player,
and Cooper, a tall cricketing youth from Glenelg.
The fingl was one of the most exciting things ever
seen. At 950, with only 50 to go. Cooper led by a
hundred or so; but, then, experience or nerves or
luck, or something, came in, and Demodena got home
bv 16. It is feared that neither of them will be
able to represent the State in the forthcoming Aus-
tralian championships.
“0.K.” : The sculling championship of the
world, not a much sought-after distinction as it is,
would have lost its last vestige of glory if the con-
queror of Arnst had lost his title to Harry Pearce.
So, for the sake of what once was a matter of great
sporting interest, Barry’s defeat of the Australian
challenger can be accepted as a satisfactory result.
It leaves the opening for Arnst to have a go to
recover the championship. Barry had the bad luck
to be born SO years too late. Probably he is the
best man who ever pushed a racing skiff, but he gets
no boodle out of the game. Before he rowed Pearce
he complained that there was no “promoter” to put
up even his stake for him. He and his father had
to find the money. All things considered, the
Thames Conservancy might allow special sections of
the river bank to be reserved by speculators for a
champion boat race, and even with all the risks of
the London climate against them, they would be
game to do something in the way of guarantees for
winner and loser. Pearce and his supporters must
have paid dearly for his defeat. Boat racing is
sport, anyhow. The bloke behind the sculls doesn’t
sit on velvet. # *
Last week Richard Arnst formally chal-
lenged James Paddon, the holder of the Australian
sculling championship and the Referee Challenge
Cup, to defend his title to both baubles, lie also
includes a stake of £2OO among the items to be
raced for, and as an earnest of good faith, deposited
£25. Richard desires that the contest shall take
place on the usual course on the Parramatta River
on November 15, 1913. So far, the possession of
the championship has not brought the hefty Paddon
much cash, so he will, no doubt, be happy to oblige.
Arnst, apnarentlv, is not aware he is no longer as
young as he used to be.
Young Nipper (the pocket Hercules) is beaten
easily on points by Sid Burns at the Sydney
Stadium.
The destructive progress of Balmain in
the Sydney Rugby League competition came to a
sudden end on Saturday, when that explosive team
crashed into Eastern Suburbs on Sydney Cricket
Ground. Many of the righteous and the unrighteous
had prayed hard on Saturday night and Saturday
morning that Balmain might blow up with the usual
explosion and wreck Eastern Suburbs, and during the
first half it looked as though it might happen at any
moment. Balmain was clearly boss of the situation,
and scored five points to nil. But five points is not
an explosive score. East Sydney seemed to be deadly
stale. “They’ll wake up in the second half,” said
a Balmain admirer. But Balmain didn’t. Instead,
it was Eastern Suburbs which came to life, and
scored 13, while Balmain failed to cross the line or
tilt the ball over the bar. Both sides played wretch-
edly, but Balmain’s showing in the second half was
plain horrible. South Sydney came back to its old
form, and revived wonderfully, as Glebe found to its
grief. Play had hardly started when South broke
loose, and in a few minutes had five on the scoring
board. Glebe was out-run, out-played and out-
kicked, and when half-time whistle blew, South was
leading by 12 to three. In the second half Glebe
had the aid of the wind, and made desperate attempts
to even up. But it had no hope. South played
better than ever, and scored point for point, so at
full time it won by 15 points to six. Western
Suburbs had a great battle with Newtown, and, for
a time, almost up to whistle-blow, was leading by
11 points. Then, for some inexplicable reason, the
Western team seemed to ease up. Newtown broke
loose like a swarm of devils, and with a series of
splendid charges wiped out the deficit and got a
credit balance of three points, winning by 17 to 14.
Annaridale journeyed to North Sydney, and found the
stubborn tollmen waiting in battle array. Annan-
dale astonished the natives, and itself, by the
splendid quality of its play. Its charges were
brilliant and magnificently fast and well-pressed
home. The defence work was not so good, though
some of the tackling was very deadly. But, for the
main part, it was the brilliancy and speed of the
attack which won the game. Still, when North Syd-
ney got going it was not to be denied, and it was
as close a match as is possible in a League game in
which one side wins. The baby team won by 20
points to 18, but the difference in the quality of the
play was much more than that in favor of Annan-
dale. The result of the upheaval is to maintain
Eastern Suburbs in its lead, with time and the
competition quickly running out. Eastern Suburbs
has 22 points, South Svdney 18, Newtown 17, and
Glebe 14. Glebe is now out of the hunt, along with
the clubs which follow it. They have only single-
figure scores.
•» * * *
Although the Eastern Suburbs-Balmain
League tussle was, generally, a flat, stale and unpro-
fitable sort of mix-up, it had one exciting spasm.
That was shortly before the finish, when a couple of
the opponents lay on the ground and thumped each
other very merrily to wild applause from the jaded
spectators. The most interesting matches of the
League competition now are between the teams that
have absolutely no chance of getting to the top, and
are struggling like Satan to get as far from the
bottom as possible.

The Federal or Australian Competition
saw the second round completed on Saturday, an
Sydney team is atop of the heap with & 2 points.
The second place is filled by Paddington, which h
40 ; South Sydney and Newtown have each 32, North
Shore has 28, Balmain 20, and East Sydney 16.

Sydney nor Balmain can do it.
Anything more horrible than the showing
of the Queensland XV against the N.S.W. Rugby
Union’s team at the University Oval, Sydney, on
Saturday would be hard to imagine. It may
been that they had all been freshly vaccinated, but
had they been inoculated with the germ of utter
inefficiency they could not have made a worse showing.
Back machine there was none, and the forwards
looked as though they had never played together in
their lives before. During the first half the N.S.W.
team had a slight advantage from the wind, and
scored 14 points to nil. When the change of ends
came Queensland showed a slight flash in the pan,
and scored. But it was only a flash. The fire died
down as quickly as it had flamed up, and while
Queensland scored six points N.S.W. piled up 12, in
spite of the handicap of the wind, and finally won by
26 points to six. The thing is inexplicable.
■* * * *
The only Vic. League game which counted
on Saturday proved the most exciting of the season.
It was between St. Kilda and Carlton. The latter
was fourth on the list and half a game ahead of the
Saints. By concerted play and high speed the
Saints held their own and a bit over till the inter-
val, when the tally was Saints 41 points and Carlton
19. On resuming, drier conditions suited Carlton
better, and it started to reduce its deficit, to the
joy of its share of the 25,000 barrackers, who began
to “chat.” But there was no dropping of the
Saints’ bundle, and at lemon-time that crowd was
still six points ahead, 50 to 44. The last term
was a tremendous struggle. The Saints worked
methodically and secured a goal, and then another
and two behinds, and the game seemed safe. But
the Blues came again and piled up four goals and a
behind, and thereby won a lead of five points. The
minutes were hasting to the limit as the Saints
charged for the last time, and in a scrimmage in
front of the goal a Saint was slung bv the neck.
As he placed the ball the bell rang, but he was
entitled to his kick, and he drove it straight and
well, and won the game by a single point. Carlton
had dropped to fifth on the list; St. Kilda is
among the four for the present, but it has yet to
meet ’Wood, among others, and anything may hap-
pen. In the other disturbances South beat Mel-
bourne by 48 points to 19 ; Geelong Essendon by
76 to 53; ’Rov Richmond by 79 to 46; and
’Wood Uni. bv 'B7 to 70.

The position in the Ass. is still more or less
open, though it looks as if North, ’Scray, ’Don and
’Wick would be “the four,” P’ran’s chance having
been largely discounted by its walloping on Satur-
day at the feet of North, which inconsiderate com-
bination put together 54 points to P’ran’s 44. The
latter might have had a hope of winning had it made
some slight effort to score, or at least to effectively
defend during the first quarter, when North heaped
up 39 points to nil. That sort of disaster breaks a
team’s heart, and it rarely pulls level again. For
the rest, Brighton succumbed to Essendon, with 52
points to 86; Northcote to Brunswick, 74 points to
116 ; Port to ’Town, 48 points to 54; and the
Melb. Citizens serenely accepted their usual trounc-
ing, scoring 20 points to ’Scray’s 54.
When America trampled on Australasia
in the first round of the Davis Cup, it looked as
though nothing could stop them winning the chal-
lenge round against England. And nothing did.
England relied on Parke, Dixon and Roper Barrett;
America on McLoughlin, Hackett and Williams.
McLoughlin was, of course, mainly responsible—
helped by Dixon, who struck the shocking form he
showed here, and served 13 “doubles” in his match
with Williams. On the first day Parke beat
McLoughlin 3 sets to 2, although the American won
30 games to 29, and Williams beat Dixon after five
sets, 25 games all. In the doubles Dixon and
Barrett were within one stroke of the match; but
the Yankees took the match 3 sets to 2, 26 games to
23. England thus had to win both singles to keep
the Cup; but when McLoughlin beat Dixon three
straight sets England was sent for, and the U.S.A.
had the Cup. Parke and Williams played their set,
and the Irishman won after five sets. Thus does
America for the first time since 1902 take the trophy
home. The Davis Cup matches started in 1900. and
results since then have been: England 5
wins, Australasia 4, America 3. No matches were
played in 1901 and 1910. South Africa, France,
Germany, Canada, Belgium and Austria have all
tried and failed.
Jules Goux, the dapper little French
motorist who won £4OOO by getting home first in
the great International Motor Speed Contest at
Indianapolis (U.S.A.) some time ago, gave many
interesting particulars of the race to the Motor
Magazine. If Jules wrote the yarn himself, then
lie is a descriptive artist of parts. But the Chances
are he didn’t; it was probably the work of a
“ghost.” The course was miles in circuit, with
a bricked surface, which by mid-day was almost red
hot. Around it were over 100,000 people looking
down from grandstands and seats, and 30,000 seated
in closely-packed automobiles. Never before had
Jules Goux gazed upon such an ocean of faces. The
track was so narrow that for the start the 27 cars
had to be ranged in four lines. Had they been
allowed to start at full blast there would have been
an instant mix-up, so the President of the Circuit
took them off in his roadster ear, starting dead
slow, and accelerating his pace till they were nicely
strung out. Then he slipped off into a side track
and left them to it. The noise was deafening, for
all that vast mass of humanity was howling and
cheering, waving flags, and blowing trumpets. Oars
sufficient to hold 30,000 people tooted their horns,
and stowed away among the 80 grandstands were
four military bands making more row. On top of
it all was the roar and clatter of 27 high-powered
cars thundering over the bricks.
The Yankee drivers had drenched their machines
in lubricating oil, and when the cars got
hot this went off in dense clouds of smoke, which
obscured the track, along which the cars thun-
flered sometimes at 90 miles an hour. The effect ot
that maelstrom of sound on the drivers was to
duce a fearful exaltation. They became to them-
selves like gods, and reckless unto madness. Onlv
bv the greatest efforts could they bring themselves
to exercise the slightest caution. When tyres gave
out and tanks ran drv they pulled off, but onlv be-
cause their speed-making powers were interfered
with All were possessed with the one idea, to get
a break on the field with one tremendous dash and
so dishearten those behind, and the contest be-
came terrific as they bounded madlv past each other
through the clouds of drifting, rolling smoke. Goux
was lucky, and got to the front early, and owing to
careful handling his tyres lasted longer than those
of the majority. When he had to haul off to replace
those on his back wheel he lost a minute, but was
still to the good except for the car driven bv the
dare-devil Burman, the . Yankee motor “speed-king,’
who would not be shaken off. The famous driver,
Zuccarelli, had horrible luck; his petrol pipe broke
and he roared along in flames, a fearful sight., till he
could get out of the ruck and haul clear. By which
time he was getting rather scorched. No matter
what speed Goux soared up to, Burrnan was always
just on his wheel. But the car was unequal to the
strain and developed mechanical troubles, and the
gallant Burrnan had to retire —beaten by his makers.
The heat was terrific; whenever his car stopped
to replace tyres or refill the tanks, Goux had a
gallon of water poured over his head, only to find
it dry as a bone five minutes later. With Burman's
retirement serious opposition ended, and Goux won
with comparative ease. He was amazed at the finish
when the Yankees gave him a splendid ovation for
beating their own champion.
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen has ukased
that the Tamworth-Newcastle Dunlop Road Race shall
be run off on Saturday, September 20. Intending
competitors will therefore require to get busy, for
the race is the longest and most severe in Australia.
Via Werris Creek and Quirindi the distance is 184
miles, and the roads are unmentionable.
* * * *
“Young Nipper,” the victor of 200 fights
out of a total of 375, mostly per medium of the
order of the knock, made his Sydney debut on Satur-
day night at the Stadium, his opponent being Sidney
Burns. The two had met before in England several
times. Nipper was lOst. and Burns 4oz.
heavier. As usual, the heavier man won. Nipper
is certainly a mighty smiter, a fact that Burns
seemed to be particularly alive to, for he avoided
the wallops whenever possible with a loathing that
suggested they were charged with small-pox or dyna-
mite. He boxed beautifully and stalled off Nipper’s
frantic attentions with a very pretty straight left
against which Nipper dashed his nose in vain. On
that fist the nose could make no impression. Nipper
was not in condition ; he had got into the ring too
soon after his arrival, and after the ninth he was
cold pig. But he was still dangerous, and Burns,
after a few sharp shocks, decided it was not good
enough to go monkeying around for a knock-out
when he had a sure win on points; so he contented
his soul with very prettily out-boxing his exhausted
opponent. In the last round he took a few chances
and floored Nipper twice, but that young man got up,
and, although apparently almost unable to
stand, fought viciously and managed to see the thing
through. This time the referee’s job was dead easy,
and even the howling mob who are so ready to hoot
what they don’t understand did not dispute it.
Hock Keys is finding congenial work in
Maoriland. Some weeks ago he crossed the Tasman
under engagement to box Hegarty, at Wellington.
The bout resulted in a draw, although Keys reckoned
he should have been given the verdict. After that,
he was signed up for matches at Hastings (where
he easily defeated W. Hannan) and Auckland, with
good prospects of other engagements at Christchurch
and Wellington. At Auckland he will box Johnnie
Summers, the Englishman, who is paying a flying
visit to Maoriland. The chances are that Summers
will also face an opponent at Wellington before
leaving the land of the extinct moa and the active
I O U.
The A.J.C. has hit somebody very hard
with a new rule that bookmakers are not to train
their own horses after November 1 this year. Theor-
etically, of course, there is no reason why a book-
maker should not run his horses as honestly as any
punter of them all, and some bookmakers do. But
the point is that other bookmakers may arise and
run horses, not on their merits, but on the require-
ments of their book.
Bluff, ill-kempt Andrew Tennant, the S.
Aus. squatter whose death was reported last week,
was a thorough good sport. After Lady Rose had
won the Derby for him in 1891 he sold the mare to
J. H. Aldridge; but just after the sale, and before
the new owner could do anvthing with her, she was
killed. Aldridge’s cheque was, of course, forthcom-
ing ; but Tennant never presented it.

Sanatogen Brings New
Health and Nerve Force.
“ I have used Sanatogen with extra-
ordinary benefit. It is to my mind a true
food tonic, feeding the nerves, increasing
the energy, and giving fresh vigour to the
overworked body and mind.”
These words by Sir Gilbert Parker, M.P.,
the well-known author, have a helpful
meaning for you— if you need real help in
one of those crises of life when your health
is giving you anxiety.
They echo a sentiment of gratitude which
is expressed in many different ways in
thousands upon thousands of letters from
men and women who owe to Sanatogen new
strength and vitality.
The Rt. Hon. Sir John Gorst, member of His
Majesty's Privy Council, writes: “ Sir John
Gorst wishes to say he has long been acquainted
with the great merits of Sanatogen—has taken
it with excellent results, and when necessary
will certainly take it again. It was also used
by a daughter of his with great benefit.”
To you who are run down, nervous or
W eak —to you who are less than yourself —
Sanatogen will bring new health. To you
whose nerves*have gone wrong, and whose

The Care of the Wounded.
“Old Soldier” : Duncan Johnstone’s book
of experiences of the Balkan War, “With
the British Red Cross in Turkey,” shows
What are the realities of war. What sheep
we are! We imagine that some thousands
of men armed with rifles, and all dressed
alike, and trained to make certain mechani-
cal movements in concert are an effective
army. Well, they aren’t; at any rate, it
won’t last. The Turks were supposed to be
the champion fighters of Europe, though it
was admitted they had no one with brains
worth tuppence to handle them. But they
went all to pieces before the Bulgars, and
now the Bulgars have apnarently gone to
pieces before the Greeks and Serbs. The
origin of most of the trouble is that men
have been expended too recklessly, and no
care has been taken to pick up and save
the human wreckage of the battlefield.
The Bulgars thought at first that the one
thing for them to do was to break down the
Turks by the persistence and dash of their
charges. They did it, but they had forgotten
the smallness of their population, and the
impossibility of filling up the ranks if they
were too much thinned. They failed to
provide for the adequate cere of the nrnre
slightly wounded. The Turks were just as
bad, but they had reserves of men to draw
on, and now they are threatening to turn
the tables. What should we in Australia
think if something like the following de-
scribed the state of our wounded defenders
after the first fight with an invader? —
We found that most of the wounded were from
Kirk Kilisse, three weeks earlier. Many had not been
attended to since the first field dressing after the
battle, and were almost starving. The wounds were
horrible to look at, and had grown twice their size
through neglect. They were also full of maggots
and dirt worms, which had to be scraped out with
the fingers. One man had had some wooden splinters
behind his eyes for 12 days, without any attention.
The smell in the hospital was dreadful.
That seems more than sufficiently horrible
when you remember how necessary it is that
wounds should be promptly attended to if
the patients are to have a chance of re-
covery. Here is what was seen when a
second convoy of wounded came a day or
two later: —
The wounded were all in a terrible condition. 1
can remember one man who had a huge, jagged hole
in his shoulder, and, on examination, we found the
ring of a shell fastened on to the shoulder bone,
between it and the socket. The wound all round
was discolored, and full of pus ; there were also the
inevitable maggots caused by dirt, and altogether the
sight was most loathsome. Another, who lingered
on in great agony for a few days, had no back to
his head, which was all crushed in. I cannot make
out how thev survived the journey from the front.
The old soldiers among us said the sights were far
worse than any they had seen during the South
African War.
The time is coming when it will be neces-
sary to organise the military forces of Aus-
tralia for war. It would be madness to wait
till the invasion actually comes before doing
it It will be too late then, because all the
reasonably competent organisers will have
their time more than fully occupied in get-
ting ready the fightin" machinery, and will
not be able to so much as think of the pro-
vision of food, forage, baggage and supply
waggons, let alone lint, medicines, disinfect-
ants, and instruments —not to speak of
doctors, hospital orderlies, and, most im-
portant of all, stretchers, ambulance wag-
gons for the road, and hospital trains for
the railways. If Australia is going to pre-
pare in earnest to defend itself, it must
make sure that losses in battle shall be mini-
mised by ensuring the greatest possible pro-
portion of recoveries among the wounded,
and that can onlv be done by having a
thoroughly efficient medical and ambulance
service prepared in peace.

“Drivefast” on the stoppage of a leak: —
Speaking after seven years’ English and eight
years’ Australian experience of the motor trade, I
have no hesitation in saying that “10-2-4’s” idea
(B. 3/7/T3) of bringing out parts and assembling
cars here is scarcely worth troubling about, for two
excellent reasons. First, no maker of any standing
at all would supply a chassis not erected. Just as
much skill and experience are required to erect a
chassis so that it will give satisfactory results as
to make the parts in the first place; in fact, half the
battle in the turning out of a first-class machine
lies in careful erecuon and testing. Some makers
of cars go so far as to even refuse to supply any
car without the body complete, in order to prevent
a fool-builder this end fitting a bigger body than
the chassis was designed to carry, thereby injuring
the maker’s reputation in the long run. In the
second place, any chump who is careful can build
a cycle successfully, but there are no men to speak
of in Australia with the necessary experience to
take on chassis-building. The game calls for special
technical knowledge, gained only by years of work.
The remedy for tfie present importing craze is
to take a leaf out of America’s book and put on a
big duty—say 30 per cent, the first year, rising to
50 per cent, afterwards. The big output in the
Commonwealth is sufficient to keep three or four
factories going. The result of this policy would be
that the firms with a big hold on the Australian
market —Talbot (easy first), Star, Renault and Ford
—would establish works in Australia, sending out
nucleus staffs to start things. An annual output of
200 to 300 cars for the high-grade machine, and
say, 1000 for the cheap American type, would keep
these factories going, and enable the machines to be
sold here at the same prices as at present, or even
less, and the millions per annum spent in cars would
stop in the country that wants the work and the
money badly. Fat could still ride in his “im-
pawted” machine and pay through the nose for it;
but the average man would favor the Australian
) article every time. And the ability to obtain parts
in three days from the factory would be a tremen-
dous selling argument even in the case of Fat and
in spite of his penchant for foreign goods.
“Bookware”: There is much Melbourne
newspaper discussion just now on education.
One correspondent pitches into teaching nur-
sery stories to the young. “A pack of lies !”
says he. Without perhaps knowing it, he is
exactly the Bounderby of Dickens’s “Hard
Times.” Says Bounderby to the school-
master: “Facts, Mr. Gradgrind. Facts!”
Lady Somerset lately raised this note in
England. Says she: “There has been alto-
gether too much of this nursery-fable non-
sense. Filling little heads, addling little
brains with rubbish ! Teach them something
useful. What use is there in ‘Mother Goose,’
Aladdin,’ ‘Goodv Two Shoes,’ ‘Jack the Giant
Killer,’ and all that stuff?” Lady Warwick
rushed to the defence, with all the indigna-
tion of Dickens against Bounderby and
Gradgrind. She said: “The imagination,
according to Napoleon, rules the world. I
contend that it is the grandest quality to be
cultivated in young time. All the nursery
fables are parables, with profound morals,
or they would not be vital. See what an
unhappy stuffed little monster John Stuart
Mill was made by his father. The wretched
little chap, for he was actually wretched,
read Homer in the Greek at eight years old .
lie tells you in his autobiography how
Wordsworth saved him. He had become
like Mark Twain’s Jumping Frog, full ot
small shot. I thank Heaven for Jack ana
the Beanstalk,’ and ‘Rip Van V) inkle, and
‘Peter Pan,’ and ‘The Blue Bird.’
From a laborer in the vineyard :
Anglicans in Australia are beginning to interest
themselves in social questions, and there are sjffus
that the old-time starchiness is getting limp. Mel-
bourne took the lead with a Social Questions Com-
mittee, on which L. V. Biggs, a Parliamentary hand
of the Age, who is (or was) a suburban municipal
light in his spare time, was prominent. Courses of
sermons and lectures on “Social Sins” last year, in
which parsons and medicoes collaborated, drew a good
deal of attention —especially the doctors’ remarks on
the “Red Plague” (syphilis) and “Race Suicide.
A course on “Social Diseases” this year cut less ice,
even though “The Sacredness of Sex” was advertised
as one subject. Adelaide Anglicans got busy shortly
afterwards, and arranged lectures on such topics as
“Housing Reform” and “Town Planning”—on which
Adelaide thinks, with some justice, that it knows a
th ; ng or two. Another set of sermons begins in
Adelaide this month (August) on “The Stewardship
of Life’s Values.” As Australia is almost the only
country in the world whose rich men, speaking
generally, do nothing for the community which gives
them- their wealth, these sermons may hit the tar-
get. Sydney, not to be left too far behind, is coming
in with six lectures on “The Social Problem.” After
one on the development of capitalism comes a second
on the development of the Labor party, by Rev. C.
E. Curtis, who is shepherding now in a Sydney sub-
urb. Curtis is High Church and a Socialist, neither
of which qualifications tends to make him more
popular in ruling church circles. Some years ago
he was a parson at Narrabri (N.S.W.) when George
Reid was pursuing his famous Anti-Sosh tiger. So
wild did Curtis get at George’s farrago of tarra-
diddles that he hired a local hall and replied to the
Dry Dog’s master. Subsequently., he was in charge
of a mission to navvies on the North Coast railway.
After he had been there awhile, the “eminent busi-
ness men” who were on the council of the diocese
observed that no cash was coming in, and they wrote
asking Curtis what he was doing with the collec-
tions. He replied that he hadn’t had any collec-
tions, and didn’t intend to have any ; and now some-
one else has been sent to chase Bill and Jim for the
infrequent thrum.
“Henry Horsecollar” writes ;
It is a feature of the row raised in the name of
“Protestantism” against Irish Home Rule that a
very large section of the Church of England furi-
ously denies that it is Protestant. Certainly it
didn’t join in Luther’s famous Protest; it had very
little use for Luther; and it kept very far aloof
from many Lutheran ideas. It is far more aristo-
cratic than the Roman Church, for its visible head
is an hereditary monarch, while Popes are elective,
and, as often as not, come of very humble families.
One recent Pope had a brother who was a postman.
The Anglican Church claims to have preserved the
regular line of bishops from St. Peter onward, and
to have inherited the apostolical succession from the
Church of Rome. William 111. certainly was a
Protestant. He was a Calvinist who believed in
predestination and the damnation of such infants as
were not “elect.” The Georges were Protestants of
the old German school, with which Luther was
identified. But the Established Church of England,
over which they ruled, always hovered between two
opinions, and hung on hard to its apostolical sue-
cession. To a large section of Presbyterians and
Nonconformists it is Roman. To almost all Roman
Catholics it isn’t Roman. It is a picturesque and
dignified institution, but indefinite. Also, it is
mixed Some of it still believes in transubstantiation,
confession, the celibacy of the clergy—in fact, some
of it is as Roman as it can be without losing its
billet. (Incidentally, Queen Elizabeth, who has long
been held up as a champion of Protestantism, used
to regard married ecclesiastics as persons living in
adultery, and to insult their wives as women of loose
character.) Some of it, on the other hand, looks
very much like plain Methodist. It contains wider
differences of opinion than any other Church on
earth, and is about the hardest sect to define on the
globe. The Presbyterians of Scotland, in the davs
when they sent a Presbyterian colony to the north
of Ireland, regarded the Anglican Church, of which
George V. is now the head, as an idolatrous insti-
tution born of Rome, and this point was discusse
with great anger and bloodshed for a good part of
a century. The Anglican crowd is addicted to de-
scribing its reverends as “priests,” and in its fond-
ness for archbishops and bishops and canons and
priests and curates and deacons and cathedrals and
vestments it adheres very closely to the Roman
model. In fact, it is hard to say whether George
V. is a disguised Roman Catholic or a shandygaff.
“10-2-4”: I have just finished readirv-
“Priees, Price Indexes and Cost of Living
in Australia” —a little brochure that was
“published under the authority of the Min-
ister for Home Affairs, Hon. King O’Malley,
M.P.” the other day. (The King’s capacity
for putting his name and divnity on every-
thing within reach was akin to the yearning
of Smith, the tourist, to engrave his style
and title and his address at Peckham Rye
on the Pyramids.) As light literature it
has a charm which is all its own. It is
just the sort of thing that one can imagine
Edden or Ryrie, M.P., or Onslow or West,
M.P., bearing gleefully back to the home
and reading aloud to an enraptured family
circle. Take, for example, the section
where proof is advanced that “the Method of

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
J. Parr: If the wheat-farmer can’t afford
to pay decent wages, because he has to take his pro-
duce into the world’s markets, then wheat-growing
is no good for Austi'alia. But tell us, if you are an
experienced man, whether the most successful far-
mers aren’t those who treat their workmen best.
Haven’t you one named Lord in your own district?
And have you heard of another called Brunskill in
the Riverina ?... . Jimmo: You’ve got the rhyming
faculty, but you don’t know what to do with it yet.
....Broncho George: No, Broncho; we can hold out
no hope of making it “worth your while’’ to become
a constant contributor... .R. Jackson: A vast and
purposeless ghost yarn. With all these spectres,
snakes, devils, chills, girls, beers, shadows and sun-
dries to work upon you should have produced some-
thing better.... Thomas V. Tierney: It is fine, ring-
ing, patriotic verse. But, unfortunately, the gallant
muster of the untrained —the tumultuous men who
saddle the old roan horse, and take down the old
shotgun, and start with great fervor to walk or ride
to the undefended North in the day of invasion—
however heroic it may sound, is of little use. What
is wanted is the well-trained Bill or Jim who knows
exactly where to step into line, who has his uniform
on (because there is an unpleasant habit of treating
soldiers out of uniform as brigands), who gets his
up-to-date rifle and ammunition and his rations
served out to him like clockwork, and starts for the
scene of action by railway. He may not shout so
much as the voluntary or mob variety of Bill, but
when he Gets There he breaks things. There is no
mention of any well-organised medical department
or commissariat in your yarn. And Bill can’t eat
gum-leaves C.0.M1P.: Composition all right; but
matter scarcely of wide enough interest. .. .W. C.

Bellerive: Another tragedy :
THE BLACK SHEEP.
Neath the silvery beams
Of the pale moonlight
Through a mansion window crept
A desperate thief one night;
The hour was late and
All around was still
As he lowered himself down
From the window sill ;
When he emerged with plunder
A grim rifle ball
Laid him lifeless in front
Of the garden wall;
A veteran bent o’er him
With the fatal gun—-
He’d lodged a bullet in
His black-sheep son.

THE BOWER BIRDS.
I love the Western wild woods
Where roams the kangaroo
I hear the little bower birds
chopping in the mountains blue
In the wild woods he is falling
And driveing on his teams
The wild ducks he is calling
On western wooded streams
He is gathering pretty shells
” And building up his bowers
He is singing in the dells
And stealing pretty flowers
On the stock route he is singing
He’s a drover in his teens
His stockwhip it is ringing
Every where it seems
Near the running tumbeling water
He acts and mimics all day long
He imitates the kuckaburras laughter
And sings the wild, birds song
He imetates the dingo and the wild dogs barking
On the phantom lakes and in the mulga trees
And the boom of the wild waves breaking
On the granite reefs In the western seas,

Public Opinion and
Beale High-grade Pianos.
at th!lag„l d de St oftrlH Ind™ « jLj" ““ B ’“*“ e,,,!y WPW “ ed
regaltmats. HO ' iZOntal G “" d , “ 8 for S ° me “ tbe State Government House, Sydney, and gives every satisfaction to the viee-
atith' 1 Bea ' C P ?T '’T* W °" I,ighest rec ognition for their musical and lasting qualities. This is simply because
Bea e 1 lanos possess al the improvements found m the best makes of modern pianos, and, in addition, they have patent features-such as our
steel tuning system—which are not obtainable in any other pianos.
Many eminent men, in every walk in life, have recorded their appreciation of our instruments, and we give below the opinions of a few.
HERR WILHELM GANZ, one of the best-known men in the musical
world of London, played on onr pianos at the Franco-British Exhibition, and
afterwards sent us the following purely voluntary testimonial:—
“It gives me great pleasure to state that I consider the Grand Pianoforte
which you exhibited at the Franco-British Exhibition a very first-rate instru-
ment. I much enjoyed playing on the same. The touch is excellent, likewise the
tone. I especially admired the wonderful tone of the bass. In fact, I hardly
remember a pianoforte with a more resonant sound, and Australia may he very
proud of your having produced such a fine instrument. With every good wish
for your success in the production of these pianofortes, I remain, yours verv
faithfully, Wilhelm Ganz.”
(At the Fra neo-British Exhibition, London, 1908, Beale Pianos were
awarded Grand Prize and three Gold Medals.)
THE HON. T. WADDELL, ex-State Treasurer, New South Wales: —
‘I heartily wish success to Beale & Co., Ltd., in their great manufacturing
enterprise. They deserve every encouragement that can be given them. It is to
enterprising men such as those connected with this firm that we must look to
find profifable employment for our own people, and to push the country ahead.”
HIS EMINENCE THE LATE CARDINAL MORAN, Archbishop of Sydney:
“I have had great pleasure in visiting Messrs. Beale & Co.’s grand centre of
industry at Annandale, and in congratulating them on the magnificent example
they have set of enlightenment and energy.” Ills Eminence also said : “We must
all, I am sure, rejoice in the wonderful development of industry such as has
been demonstrated by this institution. It has been a revelation to me. I take
this opportunity of testifying my admiration of the work of Messrs. Beale & Co.
I had no idea that in the musical department such perfection had been attained.
I am delighted that such beautiful instruments as those we have seen and heard
are produced in Australia for the cultivation of music, and we must all wish
Messrs. Beale & Co. continued success—a success they undoubtedly deserve.”
IHE HON. SIR ARTHUR RLTLEDGE, K.C.M.G., ex- Attorney-General, Q. :
“I have been much astonished and delighted to witness the operations of an
industry that is rich in promise for the prosperity of New South Wales.” Sir
Arthur also said : “That which interested me most, as I thoroughly understand
the subject, is the apparatus and system for seasoning timber. Not merely is
the timber exposed to the air, under cover, for years, but, above all, the perfect
method of uniform extraction of the sap without injuring the fibres, will enable
the wood to resist all climatic influences. It is the only perfect system I have
met with in Australia, and I was not aware until to-day that it existed amongst
us. Messrs. Beale & Co. are enabled to use in Australia patents for timber
seasoning which are elsewhere exclusive. Hence the complete success.”
COMMANDER WILLIAM J. COLQUHOUN, Commonwealth Naval Forces:—
“The extent and importance of this manufactory has been a revelation to
me, and the proprietors may be justly proud of the result of their energy and
enterprise. From what I have seen of the methods of manufacture, I believe the
Beale Pianos are just the instruments to retain all their fine qualities in all
climates where severe and rapid changes are frequent, and I think they must be
successfully introduced into India, China and Japan.”
THE HON. SIR WILLIAM J. LYNE, K.C.M.G.. ex-Minister for Customs,
Commonwealth of Australia :
"The example Messrs. Beale & Co. have set by employing Australian labor
and skill in turning out the highest grade of artistic work deserves all possible
recognition. Having personally witnessed the manufacture of the parts of their
pianos, I cannot speak too strongly as to their excellent quality.”
THE LATE HON. E. W. O’SULLIVAN, Minister of Public Works, N.S.W.:—
“I consider it the duty of every citizen of the Commonwealth who can afford
it to purchase an Australian-made piano. Messrs. Beale & Co.’s pianos being
of the very best description, and, for our climate, superior to any imported, all
who intend purchasing a piano should show their patriotism by giving preference
to the Australian-made article.”
By our method of selling direct from factory to user we save you all the usual agents and middlemen’s profits.
We have always on hand a number of good second-hand pianos, taken in part payment of Beale Pianos.
If you cannot call, write for Catalogue, which we will be pleased to send, post free.

22 Automatic Rifle
The novelty of its operation is one of
the fascinating features of the Win-
chester .22 Automatic Rifle. Instead of
the downward and upward motion of a
finger lever, or the backward and for-
ward thrust of a slide handle, the pull
on the trigger is the only effort required
to shoot the rifle ten times in success-
ion. The only limit to speed in firing
is the rapidity with which the trigger
can be pulled before each shot. With
this speed is coupled fine accuracy,
light weight, simple and strong con-
struction, and ease and quickness of
handling. Note the beautiful appear-
ance of the rifle. It makes an ideal
rifle for holidays, and is suitable either

SAW.
(Patented.)
Cutting steel shapes by
high-speed friction discs
is the most rapid and
efficient method known.
It is the method used to
the exclusion of all
others in the largest steel
mills, structural, and car
shops where a large ton-
nage of steel is turned
out.
THE RYERSON HIGH-SPEED FRICTION SAW has a capacity to cut
continuously. 18-inch 100-pound beams without turning the 'beam. A 10-inch beam
can be cut in from 28 to 38 SECONDS, and smaller sections in proportionate time.
The Saw Discs are made from ordinary flanged steel, and two are furnished
with each machine. The only sharpening necessary is to occasionally renick the
blades on the edge, which operation can be done in about fifteen minutes with a
special chisel Vhich is furnished with the machine. NO GRINDING OR
GULLETING NECESSARY. Beams or other shapes may be cut on a 'bevel or mitre
at any desired ft'trgle. r

Use Big Ben all day long
Big Ben not only gets you up on
time every morning, but he serves
the whole family all day long as a
reliable clock to tell the right time by.
He’s really two good clocks in one
—an excellent time-keeper and a
splendid alarm.
He can ring you up in the morning
just when you want, and either way
you want —five straight minutes or
every other half minute for all of
ten minutes.
If you’re a light sleeper, turn on
the half-minute taps before you go
to bed. If yon sleep heavily set the
five-minute call and you can slumber
then without the get-up worry on
your mind.

to tell the right time by. He stands
seven inches tall, and his great big
open face can be seen distinctly across

Big Ben is triple nickel-plated and
wears an inner vest of steel that
insures him for life. His big, bold
figures and hands are easy to read
in the dim morning light. His large,
comfortable keys almost wind them-

or ten intermittently. If he is oiled
every other year, there is no telling
how long he will last.
Big Ben is made in La Salle,

The Inky Way.
Died recently at Sydney, within a fortnight of his
91St birthday, William Bramwell Withers, old
S’African and Victorian journalist. After being by
turns farmer and pressman in Natal, and digger,
wharf clerk and reporter in Victoria, he settled down
to half a century of newspaper work in Ballarat,
chiefly as a member of the Star staff. At one period
he was part-proprietor of that journal with H. R.
Nicholls, the late octogenarian editor of Hobart
Mercury. His “History of Ballarat,” a record of
the once-Golden City’s early childhood, now serves as
a fruitful hunting-ground for Eureka Stockaders and
other story-tellers.
• * * *
“J. 0.8.”: J. G. O’Connor—“the Old White Hat”
—has passed away at Newcastle (N.S.W.). He took
early to the Inky Way, but the amoupt of bad lan-
guage he had to set up drove him to the pen, and
founding new papers and magazines became a chronic
malady. He was never quite sure what would take
best —the political, the social, the denominational,
or the convivial. When at a dead-end he favored
the convivial. He was roused to such white heat
over O’Farrell’s attempt to shoot Edinburgh at Clon-
tarf that McLerie, then Inspector-General of
Police, had to take a hand—a distinction which
“J.G.” thoroughly enjoyed. In Parliament for a
while he was a merry sprite, but did little good.
When clouds darkened, his friends got into power,
and the influence of his brother-in-law (“Tom” was
“doing splendid” just then) .shunted him on to a
Government job with a decent salary. His only
deflection from decorum was an occasional “hurroo”
on Patrick’s Day.
* * .* *
“Vagabond” (from London): The Daily Herald
(Labor) dies hard On the petition of the printing
co. which was the principal creditor an order was
made for compulsory winding-up; but the editor-
manager announces that a new syndicate is being
formed, and that the paper will struggle on under
the old name. If the new people could not have
bought the title, they meant, to run it as The Limit
a term which Llovd George applied as an insulting
epithet, but which the Herald seized and trans-
mogrified into a proud boast. The paper has con-
tinued to toil at the multitude of its job for some
weeks as a cheaply-printed four-page affair with
caricaturist Dyson filling a quarter of the sadly
reduced space, and the suffragettes clinging tight to
most of the balance.
* * * *

instead of caving in, stood to his ukase and smashed

THE NEW GODIVA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I saw Godiva through the city streets
upon her fav’rite courser shyly ride
But yesterday in fancy.
ha if- 7a golden stream—fell down her back
And veiled the snow-white wonders of her side ;
And, as she rode (the time was torrid noon),
There fell a quaint amaze
Upon the decent few—for down the street
A line of kodaks stood—and snapshot men,
t/ong-nosed and eager-eyed,
Prepared to pick her off the moment that
she paced the cameras by.
With notebooks armed the canny journalists
Surveyed the novel scene,
And muttered: “By Gemini— what a tale
lo teH our readers in the Morning News—
Mho, sick of wars, and blood and foolish crimes,
And stupid strings of matrimonial woes,
Pine all for Something New.”
At tattling telephone and telegraph
The spry attendants stood
And flashed abroad to North, South, East and West
the tidings: “She has just commenced her ride,
And starts this moment from the castle yard,
Where all her maidens stand a-weeping sore.
In very sooth, she’s passing through the town
Garbed simply, solely, in her glorious hair.
half-past twelve,” again the wires would tattle,
She passed just where the enterprising Times
Had picketed its dozen, smartest men
To note her progress through old Coventry,
Down to minutest details.
Five minutes later did she face the kodaks,
And brave the spying eyes of all the imps ’
Who, in the name of journalistic zeal,
And high devotion to the name of duty,
Tread decent modesty and shy reserve
Down in the very dust.
Old Coventry, that one time would have hid
Its shamfid head behind a thousand walls,
Is now a garrison of Peeping Toms ;
And every Evening Star and Morning Journal
Will lend ten thousand eyes to all the luckless
Who could not gaze their greedy fill and glut them
What time Godiva rode.”
There was, that day, within that gaping city
One Wight, who did not gaze,
Who sought no window, bought no pictured papers,
And who—when Gossip gabbled of the passing,
And wondered, how the Countess could endure it—
Would halt amazed and stop his modest ears
And bid the babblers cease.
But him his fellow-cits der : ded hugely
As filled with foolish shyness and unfitted
To battle in these grand and stirring days
When he who’d press within the foremost phalanx
Of men o’ the world and pushing sons of Business,
Must hear all, know all, grasp all,
And, from the first, must see all.
And so old Coventry* its new Godiva
Back to her brutish Lord and his high hall
With gibing drove. Ahd now (they say) that noble
Will not remit the tax, be had promised,
Because (he ’plains) : “She hath not kept her pact
In that through ev’ry city in the kingdom,
And not through ancient Coventry alone,
By virtue of the Press’s million eyes,
She hath performed her journey, and so''shamed me.”
"' : V-
How that may be, I know not. But Godiva
Doth now immure herself within her castle
And comes forth never more.
Perchance she feels all sacrifice is useless ;
Perchance she feels the Age is vile and worthless,
Perchance, again, the thought may grieve and vex
her
That Modesty, her first and dearest jewel,
Was but an idle off’ring on the altar,
Of Caliban, the Mob, whose Heav’n is Lewdnese,
Whose priestess is the Press!

FINNEY’S,
THE BIC .BLOCK,
BRISBANE.
IN BANANA LAND
“Chop-Chop”: A gang of eight men, at
Hambledon, near Cairns, cut and loaded 50
tons of sugar-cane from Hives’s farm in one
recent day, and hauled the same over one
and a half miles of portable tramline (very
portable in places) to the siding. This, which
just about constitutes a record, is regarded
up here as a rather decent performance for
mere whites to put up in the tired, torrid
North, where, as Bruce Smith knows, whites
cannot worK and thrive, but shrivel up and
die young. This 6j tons per man also com-
pares favorably with the two tons per man
which, cut and loaded, was considered a
creditable day’s work in the days of our
dusky kanaka friends. Yet there are alleged
whites who still compare black with white
for this work —or any work, for the matter
of that.
The Butterman Ministry proposes to
restore the postal vote in the blessed hope
that that do<jg6 may make the Government’s
position more secure than the Federal elec-
tions in Queensland foreshadowed. Some
of the Ministerials bleat; about giving sick
women facilities to vote; they conveniently
forget {he cases that came' to light before
an Elections Tribunal, where it was shown
that, when the system was practised before,
some of the free and independent males who
were not sick were sent out on polling day
to estates near Warwick to cut burr at
4s. 6d. per hour, while agents handed in
their postal votes. Toryism is far too
squeamish ; what it should do is to restore
the plural vote. In 1893 the late B. D.
Morehead had 19 votes. He used 11, and
no doubt would have us*d the other eR-t
if there had been any real necessity.
“Rufus”: The myth that a place must
necessarily be hot because it is in North
Queensland is responsible for a lot of fool-
ish practices. Take the case of Herberton.
for instance. Here, for the past four
months, the weather has caused cold shivers
to crinkle the spine of the most hardened
southerner. Day after day and week after
week, the wind howls like seven devils, with
an accompaniment of sleety rain. Inhab-
itants flop through the streets muffled in
great-coats, heads drawn low between
shoulders, . and the bark of the influenza
fiend is heard in the land. If a
fine day happens, a frost invariably
freezes the whiskers of the night. And we
come home to houses built on the same plan
as those in the stewpan of Cairns! On
high piles, in exposed positions (as I write
the wind has caught my papers, and I shiv-
eringl.v chase them), and, worst of all, no
firedaces! Probably not half a dozen
houses in the town can boast of these latter
comforts. And all this because Herberton
is in North Queensland, and because, being
in North Queensland, a stuoid tradition
says we should be eternally grilling.
Noon.
[FOR TH BULLETIN.]
The sun is drawing vapor from the sea.
And curling it about the distant hills.
A greedy lover is the brazen sun,
He scorches to despair the stirless leaves,
Shrivels the .lichen’s loving on the rocks
And bares their nakedness before his kiss ;
He slays the shell-starred weeds upon the
beach
The Sea, his rival, cast there at the Flow.
A dawn gift for the decking of the world.
He sucks the scented resin of the pines.
He presses wide the drowsy eyes of flowers,
He fills with lust for him the modest buds
And makes their petals flame in wanton red.
The very edges of the waves are warm,
Inconstant things, that treacherously flee
The mournful pleading of pellucid depths
And the still coolness of the ocean’s heart.
And now across the brown of fallen cones,
The blood-warmth of the narrow trail of
sand
Between the shiny bracken's folded cowls.
There is the shimmer of an ivory flank ;
A satin skin the bold sun dare not smite
And the rose-arch of wilful, tripping feet—
With the black hair that hides the bosom’s
tips
Falling like some swart cloak from her small
head
To let the swelling snow break now and
then
Through the close, spicy clinging of a tress,
And the faint reek of some wild creature’s
hide
As, sleek and subtle in his yellow stripes.
Pale green-eyed glimmer, and fierce dripping
jaw,
Rubbing and purring, with his great claws
, sheathed.
Come the witch woman and her beast, to
bathe.

M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, August 2. 1913.
My Dear Thargominpau,—
George V.’s substitute in Bananaland, the
ehiel Maegregor, has been attending to his
diplomatic duties with care and punctuality
since I forwarded my last oiiicial despatch.
During the week he supolied lustre to the
annual meeting of the Children's Hospital
and assisted to open a wood-pulp business
at lanaman. Alter that he escorted his
family to the home of Mrs. McConnel, at
Cressbrook, for a restful week-end. The
Vision put in a morning at the Prevention
of Cruelty and Crfeche and Kindergarten
ball meeting, and also blew in to the theatre
one night with Mrs. A. J. Cotton and her
daughter.
Private Secretary Byth left on Friday to
join the Suevic at Melbourne, and every-
body (you know the kind of everybody I
mean), is wondering who will be invited to
take his place. The Maegregor started a
new and very sensible idea when he selected
Australians for A.D.C. and private secre-
tary, and no other vice-regal team here-
abouts ever ran with so few hitches. It is
expected that the Chiel will express his
satisfaction by appointing another Brisbane
boy. The Byth young man, who has won a
scholarship at Oxford, intends to remain in
that classical spot for two years. After
that he hopes to return to his native sun-
shine and bore into journalism or some such
highly remunerative toil.
THE SUPPLIANT.
INQUISITIVE FARE (who has parted the barely legal fare): “ Er — cabman, what's the

CARRY (hoarsely): “Not a word , sir, not a word! He's goin ’ down on his knees ter
