The Brisbane Cricket Ground was well
strewn with pleasure pilgrims to-day when
the Carnival of Nations took place. Premier
Denham and his wife, the Arthur Morgans,
the Clara Butt lady and her husband, and
several other more-or-less notable birds
occupied front perches. Over 1000
kiddies took part in the show, and
though some of the nations represented
were grossly maligned in their “national
costumes”’ at least the small Aus-
tralians had a real day out. The Norman
Park section of the Bth Battalion Senior
Cadets (the champion team of Australia)
gave a display of physical exercises, and
the rest of the programme was filled up
with goat races and other mild excitements.
Financially the carnival was a great suc-
cess, and the Residential College for Women
and the National Council of Women should
feel a distinct swelling of the bank account.
There were only two dark spots on the
management’s record. The first was the
absence of ushers, whereby those of the pub-
lic who had paid for reserved seats were in
many cases unable to occupy them —-a dis-
tinct advantage to those who had not re-
served seats. : The other had to do with tea.
Tickets for the refreshment were on sale at
the gate, and dozens of patrons bought them
there. In most cases that closed the trans-
action. Anyhow, at i to 5 p.m. the cup that
cheers wasn’t doing it, unless by stealth.
The Arts and Crafts Society burst into
public notice on Friday, when it held its
first annual exhibition. The sister society
of Sydnev had lent a supply of china-paint-
ing, metal-work. etc., and a Melbourne girl
had forwarded -a consignment of leather
things. Amongst several beautiful old art
relics which had been loaned were a pair
of 18th-century candlesticks and a Nnrem-
burg bread box, belonging to Mrs. Robin
Dods; a beaten copper begging-bowl from
India, owned by Miss Townley; and some-
body else’s Florentine cloth. The society
has only 27 members so far, yet the general
display was a credit. Miss Kate Armstrong
sent some stencilling, Isabel Townley and
Gwen Stanley china painting, Mrs. Bob
Philp. leather work, little Agatha Fitzgerald
(vvFo has only just put up her hair) exqui-
site book-illustrations. Mrs. Priestley, Mrs.
Tivey and Mrs. Scott Fletcher kept up the
University’s reputation with samples of
their skill, and envious eyes rested on
the brass work hammered by Mrs. Moran
and designed by Miss Lahev. The artistic
side of the Australian is not the void some
inartistic people suggest.
Mrs. Dudley White, who used to be Miss
Yaldwyn, has been staying with relatives in
Toowoomba, and is now in Brisbane.
Colonel Dudley White belongs to a very
well-known Queensland family, and both
husband and wife were received with a per-
fect deluge of tea and smiles. Mrs. Fred
Taylor, of England, is here. too. On Thurs-
day morning Mrs. MacNish nourished some
dozens of local inhabitants at the Cafe
Eschenhagen in honor of both ladies. In
the afternoon Mrs. Cecil Palmer repeated
the operation at Hamilton.
On Wednesday afternoon Minna
McGrath’s teapot was upturned for Evelyn
Kibble, who is to be married to Dr. Walker
early this month. Mrs. McGrath is an ex-
cellent hostess, and daughter Minna assisted
to keep the pot boiling and the sandwich
circulating.
Mollie Ferrier gave a dance on Thursday
night in honor of the two White girls, from
Pikedale Station. It was a glorious night,
the floor was all that could be desired, and
the supper called for the manufacture of
some new adjectives; but many - of the
guests were suffering where the doctors
scored, and the dancing lymped.
Three pretty girls are to be married this
month. One is Ililma Ohman, and the
others are two cousins. Gladys and Eileen
Markwell. Gladys is to marry a disgust-
ingly-rich American named Drake, and
Eileen, who has the bluest of eyes and the
curliest of dark hair, is to marry Norman
Heaslop, who is a very respectable, nice
boy despite the fact that he is a footballer
by inclination.
The Liedertafel 'concerted on Tuesday
night. Miss Emily Holberger was the solJ.
and sue soloed to satistaction. Leonard
Francis has done much for the choir, but
the orchestra still requires somg blue re-
marks for errors.
The Australian Composers’ concert on
Thursday night would have been much bet-
ter for some of the composers if some of
the performers had stayed at home. The
virtues of the compositions were hidden
beneath the sins of the singers, especially
in, some of the quartettes and part songs.
Several items had to be cut out, in conse-
quence of a strong desire on the part of the
audience to get home before the milkman:
and even then the programme was far too
long.
The bunny-huggings, of the week were
under the sanction of the Orchid Assembly,
the Caledonian Society, the Younger Set!
and the Brisbane Ladies’ Rowing Club.
Show- Week is full of hops, in more senses
than one.
Clara Butt and her husband are filling
the Exhibition Hall with song to-night and
“The Count of Luxembourg” is busy at His
Majesty’s.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Died, at Mount Morgan, D. D. O’Connell,
sport, philanthropist and good Liberal, lie
was a kinsman of the great Daniel, but most
of D. D.’s oratory was poured out, not from
the platform, but across his drapery counter.
Adamson’s motion the other day in favor
of the partition of Queensland reminded
Townsville of the days when it saw 'separa-
tion so surely coming that it had a land
sale near the site of the residence it had
chosen for the new Governor of the North.
As much as £4O was paid per acre. Any-
body who offers £4 now can have it—and
the seller will shout.
At last the Government is making some
little reparation for the atrocities committed
on the best timbers in the world. The For-
estry Department has lately cleared away
the bush round some thousands of young
cedars in the Atherton Scrub, so perhaps
young Australians of a hundred years hepce
may still be able to see the sort of stuff that
pigsties used to be made of.
Frivate, Enterprise is about to make an
onslaught on what should be the municipal
services of Townsville. Having acquired a
25 years’ option over the streets, P.E. is
floating a co. to instal cars, driven by storage
batteries, the first to be used in Bananaland.
Like the patriot that he is, Private Enter-
prise intends to import his wares from the
U.S.A.. and will probablv bring also a few
managers and the materials for a handful of
strikes and other scoui'ges.
From Rockhampton :
Magnus, manager of Mount Morgan, has set a
good example to all big wage-paying companies. He
has met his employees and a minimum wage has
been arranged, to the satisfaction of the men, and
without the consumption of money and time of
formal arbitration proceedings. The minimum for
ordinary labor has been raised to 9s. a day, while
machine men, handle men, timber men and truckers
all go up an extra shilling. Holidays, overtime and
contract ’work have also been provided for. All em-
ployees will participate in the increase, except en-
gine-drivers, electricians and others who had; re-
cently been dealt with ; and the men at Marmor and
Many Peaks, as well as those at Mount Morgan, come
under the new arrangement. The scale has to go
to the. directors for their approval, after which it
will be brought before Judge Macnaughton to be
registered as an award, j
The Farmers’ Union, * which
started out with' a fine blare of trumpets,
has been brought to heel. It is now “the
Liberal Country Party,” prepared to
do battle for the middlemen who boss
Queensland. The bogey of the Rural
Workers’ Union has worked the change. Six
months ago the Farmers were crowing
loudly about the candidates they were
going to send to Parliament; now the mem-
bers go down on their crops to the Govern-
ment wirepullers with H whining “Will I
suit, please V”

The Best Saddle in Australia
for the Stockman
-£6llOl-
is
I
We have been making saddles for close on half a century—and
all we know about the game has gone into the making of this
famous Stock Saddle of ours. It is hand-made throughout—no
cheap and nasty machine work anywhere. It is built on the
staunchest tree of specially tempered steel, fitted with an
unbreakable gulletplate, to which are riveted (not screwed, mind
you) solid steel hind crupper plates. This makes the tree
absolutely unbreakable, yet gives just the elasticity and “give”
needed. The seat is made of specially tanned bag leather, with
very long, thick harness leather flaps, that do away with the
need of wearing leggings. There are leather points to the panel, that will
not wear out for the whole life of the saddle. The knee pads are 54 inches
high, and made of solid leather, double sewn inside so that thev won’t sag
or give over. The thigh pads are made in the same wav and 1J inches
high. We have the seats with as high a dish as 6 inches, but any lower
r^ 011 want we can kive you.'i The mountings are brass I 'and nickel, and
the Dees are fastened so securely that no tug will strain them. The panel
is serge lined, and with extended facings so as not to fray or wear out by
the rubbing of the swag or tucker gear. The saddle is sold fitted with
fold or split leather girths, 4 bar irons, stirrup leathers, ring or ordinary
crupper and surcingle.
OUR GUARANTEE.—Any number of these saddles of ours 'sold ten years
ago are in use to-day and practically as good as new. No stauncher saddle
could be made. The one you buy from us we will guarantee fob two years.
If within that time the saddle proves faulty in material or workmanship, if
you return it to us, we will give you a new saddle in place of it free of

ARE YOU
deaf?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier ’to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by talcing the strain off them—the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum ha 3 been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
horn deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
cured and you can be cured, too. You mav wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take thorn out
whenever you wish Children can wear them (w
have special sizes for children from six to twelve)

Scalp Dry. Hair Fell Out in Large
Quantities. Used Cuticura Soap
and Ointment few Days. Itching
Stopped. In About Three Months
Had Full Growth of Hair.
Orchard St., Armadale, Vic. —“About
two years ago I was employed as a boundary
rider in the north of Queensland. My head
and scalp became quite dry
and my hair began to fall
out in large quantities. For
a time 1 paid no attention
to it, I did not think it was
serious, and my head be-
came bald in patches.
“I was almost giving up
in despair when I noticed
‘an advertisement of the
Cuticura Remedies and made up my mind
to give Cuticura Soap and Cuticura Oint-
ment a trial. I used the Cuticura Ointment
and washed my head with Cuticura Soap
night and morning. After I had continued
this for a few days, the itching seemed to
stop, and after about three months I had
my full growth of hair again.” (Signed)
John Williams, July 19, 1911.
For pimples and blackheads the following
is a most effective and economical treatment:
Gently smear the affected parts with Cuticura
Ointment, on the end of the finger, but do not
rub. Wash off the Cuticura Ointment in five
minutes with Cuticura Soap and hot water
and continue bathing for some minutes. This
treatment is best on rising and retiring. At
other times use Cuticura Soap freely for the
toilet and bath, toassistin preventing inflam-
mation, irritation and clogging of the pores.
Cuticura Soap and Cuticura Ointment are
sold throughout the world. Liberal sample
of each mailed free, with 32-p. Skin Book.
Address R. Towns & Co., Sydney, N. S. W.

H. D. Morton, vHio succeeds the prickly
Willis in the chair of the N.S.W. Assembly,
is compounded of bank clerk, squatter, agri-
cultural-showman, coursing, racing and
jumping enthusiast (he owned the crack
“leppers” Desmond and Lady Morton) and
football referee. He will probably find the
last ingredient the most useful in his new
job.
“Dollar” : Bulletin remarks about cram-
ming houses into small spaces in Sydney
remind me that at Kalgoorlie and Boulder
jerry-built dwellings are crowded up as if
land was scarce at £lOOO a foot, while ven-
tilation and natural lighting are entirely
neglected. Backyards are almost non-exist-
ent, and the garbage heap is usually within
a few feet of the dining-room window. Ocu-
lists thrive, because houses are so dark that
most of the population has to wear specs.,
while still 30 years young. Though millions
of acres lie vacant all around, it is not
uncommon to see linen hanging to dry on a
balcony or a house-top. At times there is
talk of enforcing the Building Act, but as
action must be taken by local bumbles, and
as local bumbles are mostly landlords, the
bad state of affairs is likely to continue.
“The Longshoreman”: If Bishop Crossley,
late of Auckland, isn’t superstitious about
the loss of his cross while boarding a
steamer, as related by “Nuki” (B. 24/7/T3),
he ought to be, for he hasn’t had much luck
since. First came his total breakdown and
consequent resignation of a fairly good and
thoroughly congenial billet. And last month,
while preparing to embark at Brisbane for
Manila —accidents while about to embark
appear to be his strong suit —he blew the
dame of a methylated spirit lamp into his
face, injuring both eyes. Some sermons
which lie preached in Auckland last year on
“Prayers for the Dead” got him into much
hot water with the furiously Protestant
members of his flock —the same lot who
worried his predecessor, Neligan, into resign-
ing—so it would seem that for the future
he had better keep off spirits, both methy-
lated and disembodied.
Lindsay Russell, the unusual Victorian
girl who wrote “Smouldering Fires,” and
lias got a similar sectarian novel going in
England, seems to be an intersting medical
subject, apart from her literary pretensions.
Awhile ago, when she was in London, the
physicians at the Brompton Hospital diag-
nosed her tubercular trouble to be due to
pressure of bone against the wall of one
lung. The bone, they said, had practically
killed the lung, and the dead tissue bleeds
away, hence her coughing of blood. The
doctors reckoned that if they had her under
their care for six months, to be duly fed up
and rested before and after the operation,
they could remove the dead tissue. A man
who had this operation performed when he
was 30 is said to be going strong at the age
of 72. After hearing nil this, Lindsay Rus-
sell returned to Melbourne and went on
catching cold as before. Apparently she is
full of undisciplined talent and eccentric
energy. The titles of her books—“Smould-
ering Fires,” “Souls in Pawn,” etc., are com-
mercial inspirations.
Melbourne Brick Commission is learning
more of the ways of the blessed Combine.
The contractor for the Queensland Insurance
Building, in Collins-sfreet, wanted “facing”
bricks for the ferroconcrete building. The
Combine, either because it didn’t approve of
concrete, or because it didn’t like the builder,
declined to supply them, and gave no reason.
The contractor was therefore driven to Syd-
ney, but the Combine had a “horrible under-
standing” with the Harbor City, apparently,
for, after he had got a few loads, the Sydney
cos. unaccountably refused him any more,
and he had to obtain what he wanted
through a suburban builder, and ship them
over as “terra-cotta.” At another Mel-
bourne job a carter, on a Saturday after-
noon, unloaded some fancy bricks over a
locked gate. Many were broken and use-
less, and the contractor put in a claim. The
only reply he got was by telephone: “Unless
your account is settled in 24 hours we refuse
supplies.”
“The Old Soldier” on cheap war:—
Why are the ‘‘Great Powers of Europe” so miser-
ably helpless against the small Powers as they have
proved to be during this wretched Balkan racket?
To the Government of a great and highly-civilised
nation —Britain, France or Germany—war, even on
a comparatively small scale, is not an adventure to
be lightly entered on. It is not only necessary to
see that the fighting men are there with their arms,
horses and weapons, but to provide for their con-
veyance to the theatre of war, which is sure to be
100 miles or more away, to feed them when they
get there, and to see that medical and surgical help
is available when they get sick or are hurt. If any
of the three Governments named failed to provide
food or medical care for its soldiers in the field, the
consequences would be serious. During the Crimean
War, the failure of the British commissariat and
medical departments wrecked the Aberdeen Govern-
ment, and in the war with Austria in 1866 an out-
break of cholera in the Prussian army compelled an
early conclusion of peace. Such considerations have
had no influence with the Balkan States. They went
to war with an enemy living just across the road,
or at most only a block away. Their fighting forces
were well armed and equipped, but there was nothing
behind them. The supply, ambulance and medical
services simply did not exist. Food and ammuni-
tion were sent forward to the army in bullock-carts
requisitioned from the peasants and driven by any
old men and boys who were available. For back-
loading there were the wounded, roughly dressed on
the field of battle by the utterly inadequate medical
staff, and laid in the bullock carts for transport to
the base at Sofia over a road already cut up by the
passage of the army with all its artillery and train.
No one will wonder that the slow-moving strings
of bullock-carts took a week, or a fortnight to
traverse 50 or 60 miles, and the wounded they
carried often died on the way, whilst the wounds of
those who lived to get to the base became poisoned.
That is one of the ways of making war cheaply. The
Turks at the beginning forgot to provide for feeding
their men, beside neglecting the hospital service.
If such things had been done anvwhere but in east-
ern Europe, or in Asia, they would be called incredi-
ble, but hardly anything is incredible when the
Asiatic, or the eastern European, who is at best
only a half-civilised Asiatic, is concerned. In any
case, making war on the cheap usually proves an
expensive game in the end. The Bulgars have found
it so, for they began by expending men instead of
monev. The two regiments raised in Sofia contained
all the literates in the place—the lawyers, scientific
men and a good many schoolmasters. The archi-
tect who planned and constructed the new public
buildings that had made the place one of the finest
in Europe shouldered a rifle in the ranks. In the
first encounters with the Turks these two regiments
were nearly wiped out. Then the ranks were filled
up from the reserve, and they were sent into action
again, and again they were nearly wiped
out. Men are, no doubt, cheap in the
Balkan Peninsula, but if the supply is limited,
it is much cheaper to expend time and money and
save the men. Bulgaria seems to have pretty nearly
run out of its supply of fighting humans.

j know I die the morn, and 1 don’t
care,
For I have kept my soul unstained of crime,
i'et such is fool-made law that I lie pent
Waiting for death because I slew a mayor !
And yet not all for that —’twould be for-
given
Had I not killed a loyal Liberal
Who raved about a Governor’s eviction!
Nor all for that again—I slew a bore.
So here I lie —comparing, wondering
Why soldiers’ fame rests on the cruel
slaughter
Of decent men, and I who kill mere cits
Am given a well-soaped rope that to my
weight
Shall come as taut as any ’cello string.
Small crime I knew except my grocer’s
work —
Selling faked conserves and explosive sauce,
Raspberry jam of carrot and tomato,
Or pepper thinned by turmeric and flour,
Or “coffee” strongly touched with powdered
acorns,
Oatmeal that’s not all oats, sultanas
From no seraglio, but pressed in casks
By feet of superstitious Grecian serfs
Who dare not wash near the Aegean Sea
(Nor elsewhere either), orange peel
Posing as candied lemon from old Bastia,
Dates that were never held for any play-
house,
But brought with other merchandise from
Bagdad
(Making the camel’s load all day, and.
seemingly,
Making his bed by night—he turning in
With all his clothes on), tea that’s mostly
titree,
Figs from some weirdly dirty lied Sea
- hamlet
And loaded up with living organisms
As but to be palatable entymology
When eaten in the dark. These tilings 1 did
Exclusively to help my owner’s trade,
lint no crime of my own. I held myself
High above blame!
n . . , Ah, me! My holidays
beginning on the third week of November,
Free of the shop and all that therein is,
Enfranchised of the stencil of pastry butter,
I went out laughing to my liberty.
I threw a tramway ticket on the street!
And straight was carried to a dungeon cell
And made to pay a sovereign for the crime.
The mayor had acted very like a cow !
How silly ’tis to try to clean a town
When all the councillors cry out for lysol
Or other disinfectant help for Tammany?
He misliked Shakspeare, too. He was a
bore.
He died the death !
It was a nine days’ wonder,
And then forgot! And I was unsuspect!
And next I found a loyal Liberal:
lie was a bore ! Oh. how I hated him !
Hate fierce as fire and cold as icicles —
And so he died, and all the world was gay.

RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Psychologist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
aEORGE RONALD BURTON (I
believe he had a never-exercised
right to string half the alphabet
after his name) was a tall, blonde, rather
cadaverous individual, with no pronounced
feature save his close-set, curiously brilliant
eyes—violet, l think they were. At the
tir “ e I write he was between 45
and 50, a bachelor of independent means,
pursuing in almost solitary absorption the
scientific researches to which he was de-
voted.
A chemist of unusual attainments and
a master of anatomy, psychology had be-
come the chief hobby of his later years.
Having exhaustively studied and analysed
the composition and attributes of the body,
he ardently sought knowledge of the immor-
tal, immaterial Something by which all
ages have believed the body to be animated.
Burton rented apartments a few doors
from my office. He had occupied them
for over a decade, during which time I had
known him at first slightly, and for the last
two or three years more intimately, though
our intercourse had never gone beyond those
scientific interests from which it had sprung.
It was as a scholar, a mentality, rather
than as a human being, that Burton im-
pressed me. His lore was so much in ad-
vance of the times, so out-of-the-way, that
I regarded him as 1 might a wonderful
volume embodying the psychic wisdom of
centuries hence.
Physically he was the reverse of prepos-
sessing. Morally he seemed perfectly nor-
mal; and, though I knew nothing of his
earlier life, I should say that on the whole
he was rather better than most men. He
lived a quite orderly life with his books
and apparatus, mixing seldom with his
kind, but, whenever sqlicited, freely, if ab-
stractedly, contributing to the relief of their
needs and sufferings. In fact, he had but
one apparent vice—-the intense selfishness
of the self-centred recluse.
And yet, in spite of all that, one day his
own soul rose up and slew him!
It was a steaming hot afternoon of
midsummer when Burton walked into my
consulting-room, where I lounged behind
drawn blinds. He plumped himself down
before me.
“The human soul,” he said, without even
the preface of a greeting, “weighs one-
quarter of an ounce.”
“Indeed,” I said,' not unfamiliar with his
ways, and too hdat-enervated to evince
much interest on this occasion. “I fancy
I did read something to that effect lately.
They arrived at that conclusion by a pro-
cess of weighing first the dying and then
the dead —did they not?”
“They did; but I long ago deduced that
by just such means.”
“Ah?”
“Yes; I refrained from publishing my
discovery, but that is the case. With
finely balanced scales I experimented with
over a score of subjects, and the results
were practically invariable. Moribund per-
sons were placed on the scales immediately
before death, and the position of the indi-
cating needle carefully noted. In each in-
stance that needle dropped one-quarter
ounce—roughly speaking—at the exact
moment of death.”
“Strange !”
“Thereby proving the existence within
us of some impalpable essence which is at
least the concomitant, if not the actual
source, of human life.”
“What about animals?” I queried lazily.
“ ‘Of human life,’ I said,” he snapped.
“With animals I have also experimented
in the same way; but their weight does
not fluctuate either way.”
“Still?”
“Well, if the —let us say soul; if the
soul, the human soul (there would appear
to be no animal soul) —if the soul, then,
weighs even one-quarter of an ounce, that
soul, be it ever so ethereal, must also pos-
sess substance since it possesses weight.”
I ftsscntcd#
“And,” he continued, “if the soul pos-
sesses substanch, the soul, separated from
the body, must refract light.”
“Then,” I commented, “it would be vis-
ible ! Yet I haven’t” —
“That is to say,” he broke in. “you do
not admit the argument. But, in the first
place, substance is not -necessarily visible,’
he persisted. “The air we breathe is sub-
stance, yet it is invisible to the eye;. but.it
can be photographed when it is set in vio-
lent motion. Second: I have an idea that
the Will —call it what you like —which cer-
tainly dominates all things, animate and in-
animate, hypnotises the death-watchers and
paralyses the more delicate optic senses.
“Now,” he continued, “a highly-sensitized
photo-plate behind a high-power lens is more
susceptible than the healthiest and keenest
eye ever set in a human head —and is not.
I believe, subservient to hypnotism. There-
fore, I am going to photograph a 50u1....
I am come to ask your assistance.”
For a space I stared at him in dumb
astonishment; then I laughed.. “Your the-
v,,ry.” I said at last, “is ingenious and fas-
cinating: but how in the world can I be of
"Assistance to you ? Do you- expect me to
obtain admission for you to the bedside of
the next patient I am unlucky enough to
have dying on my hands? Or am I to pro-
vide a subject and contrive”—
“You jest!” he cut in. “Do not concern
yourself. I propose to provide, my own
subject —in my own person., I simply ask
that you will hypnotise me.”

“Hypnotise you!”
“Just that. I am convinced that in the
deeper hypnotic trance-state the spirit is
liberated from the flesh as surely—though
only temporarily and without entire sus-
pension of the physical functions —as in
death. I am aware that, in your treatment
of neurotic disorders, you have frequently
had recourse to hypnosis, and that in that
branch of modern therapeutics you have a
considerable reputation. I merely ask vnu
to use your powers upon me; my apparatus
will do the rest.”
Even as he spoke the thought came to me
that, with his high-strung, nervous tempera-
ment, he would probably prove extraordi-
narily sensitive to the force he desired me
to exercise.
“But,” I questioned, “how, if I consent
and plunge you into the deepest hvpnotic
sleep—how will that serve your end?”
“Come with me to my rooms,” he said,
rising.
Arrived there he proceeded, with his
usual directness, to the matter in hand.
“I propose,” he began, 1 “that we should
use this room. There stands a loaded cam-
era which perfectly fulfils the conditions I
mentioned. It is set and focussed to take
the full length of that couch —and no more.
I shall first draw che blinds, rendering the
room comparatively dark ; then stretch my-
self on the couch. After your first few
passes, if you perceive that I am in any way
responsive, you will remove the cap from
the lens and, standing at my head-—-about
a foot away from it will bring you out of
the camera’s range—you will intensify the
trance until it is as absolute as your will
can make it. That done, leave me so for 15
minutes; then arouse me. Simple enough,
you see. Will you do it?”
Simple enough, as he said ; yet my nerves
shrank from the proposition in a manner
for which my brain failed to account. I
recollect that I grew angry with myself,
with my unreasoning, unreasonable repug-
nance, and that I forced out on affirmative
to his eager question.
“Thank yon ! At once then” ; and imme-
diately he stepped to one window after the
other, lowering the heavy, opaque shades.
This done, the room was, to unaccus-
tomed eyes, black as/ the Pit. I heard him
grone. without a word, to the couch, and
cast himself upon it; and I felt my way to
his side.
“Commence,” he commanded softly.
(Why does darkness beget whisners?)
Forthwith I took up ray position at his
head as he had indicated, and proceeded in
my usual manner to hypnotise him.
As I had foreseen, he proved an easy sub-
ject. In less than five minutes he became
somnolent. I carefully moved to the cam-
era, uncovered the lens, and regained my
place. Gradually, stage by stage. I deep-
ened the trance till I was conscious that
my personal efforts could carry it no far-
ther ; and, somewhat exhausted, I found a
chair and sat down to await the expiration
of the prescribed quarter-hour.
I was obliged to guess the time, but I en-
devored to be on the sure side, and it was
doubtless all of 15 minutes before I re-
turned and applied the customary restorative
treatment.
In this instance unconsciousness had
been so profound that the awakening was
a somewhat prolonged process, but it must
have been well within half an hour, reckoned
from my initial passes, that Burton was on
his feet again, in the full, soft glow of the
sunset, and warmly thanking me for my
performance.
“Sit down, doctor, sit down!” he ex-
claimed. “It has been a success, an entire
success—l feel it! Wait but another 10
minutes and you shall look upon that which
no mortal eyes ever before beheld—the neg-
ative of an actual portrait of a soul! He
jerked the plate-holder from his instrument
and rushed into his dark-room, and the door
banged behind him.
I sat there in the mellow evening light—
five minutes —10 minutes? I do not know.
I was dreaming strange dreams. As to the
result of Burton’s experiment I was singu-
larly unconcerned; its success was so un-
thinkable. I suppose, too, my late exertions
had rendered my brain lethargic. So I sat
on.
Then from the dark-room came a thud
and a clatter. What.... I sprang to the
Moor, flung it men, and stiffened as I stood,
with my body bent forward over the thresh-
liold !
Before me was a tiny room. Dim snapes
of many bottles and jars rose upon the
tiered shelves. To the right w T as a long,
narrow bench. On it stood a ruby-lantern
and an over-turned shallow tray. The
blood-red rays from the lantern, dimly light-
ing the interior, streamed close to a huddled
heap on the floor almost under the bench —-
a black blot, over which trickled the spilled
liquid from the upset tray.
Without the least sense of volition, I
stepped forward. The removal of my bulk
from the doorway permitted the entrance of
a stream of twilight from the outer room,
and this tracked across the grey gloom that
underlay the glow of scarlet, and fell re-
vealingly upon the contorted shape of
George Ronald Burton.
Mechanically—at the moment I was con-
scious of neither thought nor feeling—l
turned and straightened the body, and,
kneeling, fumbled for the pulse, while listen-
ing with my ear over the heart; but I
knew he was gone—had known it in the
instant that I heard his fall.
In shifting the dead man a small square
of glass, lying on the floor close beside him.
had been exposed to view. Presently I
noticed it. His negative—ah! With a
faint but moving interest I picked it up,
and. rising slowly to my feet, held it up to
the open doorway.
What I saw in a single glance will haunt
me to the grave : —beyond, it may be.
I cannot describe that which, in black and
white, stood out on that bit of glass between
me and the light. The portrait of a naked
soul! Picture the foulest fiend ; conceive of
a Thing festering with all the sores of all
the diseases man is heir to. with their mul-
tiplied agony and all mortal anguish stamped
upon its distorted features. Imagine. .. .
No—you cannot! George Burton looked
unon the presentment of bis own soul —and
died. I looked, and, looking, swooned and
lay among the splintered fragments of that
awful plate.
And now I know there is a Hell.
COSMOS.
The Lady of the Mist.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
THE all-night boat has simple ways,
It bears a simple crew.
And he who has a sixpence pays
And joins the festive few —
The beer-belated waifs and strays
Whose homing hour is tw T o.
Alas ! My pockets hung forlorn.
Limp, vain as some cool dream
Dreamt by young wethers lately shorn
Beside a withered stream,
Or like the cow tli&t in the morn
Is emptied of her cream.
I had no coin wherewith to woo
The kindness of that ark
Which o’er the sleeping harbor drew
Her bulk out of the dark.
I lingered in the solemn dew,
And made a stern remark.
I said a thing profane and loud,
And with an angry eye
I scanned the void and saw a. cloud
Slow drifting down the sky—
A misty thing serene and proud,
That looked x-emote and dry.
Like some god’s whiskers it was curled,
And when it heard me speak
It whisked its bulk across the world
And made a shining streak,
As though by some large person hurled
To smite me on the cheek.
It fell about me as a fog
Where yet in wrath I stood,
With all the bitterness of Gog
And Magog in mv mood.
It fawned upon me like a dog
Whose hairy tail is crude.
I stroked it with a kindly hand.
My anger passed away
To see so large a creature stand
Between me and the bay,
And making all the sea and land
One blotch of formless grey.
But as it laid a hairy paw
Upon the sleeping hill,
A vision of delight I saw
That made my heart stand still.
(Remember I had drunk beer raw.
Then blame me if you will.)
Her hair was black as thrice-combed ink.
Her bosom paper white.
Her soft eyes looked like things to drink
In unrestrained delight.
And standing there I could not think
What kept her up all night.
I said: “Bright vision of the dark.
Come watch with me a while.
The happy fog-dog has no bark.
The night is free from guile.
And there is no one to remark
If you and I should smile.”
Then was I glad in all my soul
I could not pay the boat,
For as ray arm around her stole
Joy caught me by the throat —
I was as playful as a foal
Or youngling of the goat.
Fog-wrapped amid a sleeping world
She drew me to her breast,
And at her touch all cares were whirled
Far to the east and west,
I did not care save for those curled
Rod lips so hotly pressed.
When I had kissed her eighteen times
I paused to dr*"*- a breath.
(This is an awkward word for rhymes
Except in odes to death
Or writing of far distant climes
Where dwelt the sons of Heth.)
When eighteen kisses I had pressed -
Upon her lovely lip
I paused to take a little rest,
As bees that honey sip
Their summer banquet must digest,
Poised on a blossom’s tip.
And as I paused a person strode
Between me and the maid;
The hint of wallop that he showed
All passion far outweighed;
His fist was like a camel’s load,
And made my soul afraid.
I trembled like a cabbage leaf
When shaken by the breeze.
I wilted like a round of beef;
My sick heart felt like cheese;
Whiie hands of rage and fear and grief
Seemed plucking at my knees.
His was a dreadful eye to watch,
It seemed so fierce and dark.
No living wight could beauty snatch
From such a fearful nark
“Can you oblige me with a match?”
Was his insane remark.
I breathed again. He said “Good night!”
And vanished in the mist;
Rut nowhere within sound or sight
That loveliness I wist.
Nowhere were lips of red delight
That waited to be kist.
And all the hours of dark I spent.
My hot heart fierce and blind.
Till morning that grey fog-veil rent
And made the oink east kind ;
Rut she who came and kist and went
I simplv could not find.
Bv day I dream, by night I seek
That phantom lady fair.
Whose dark hair falling on ray cheek
Made gladness and despair.
As well might I pursue last week
Or chase a tuft of air.
Yet some time, on the all-night boat,
Or on the black shore near.
Out of a fog her eyes shall float
To fill my soul with cheer.
Till then I sit in pubs remote,
Sustaining dreams on beer.
N.S.W.
